1201 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Cassidy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 180; 399 N.Y.S.2d 20; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13538; 97 L.R.R.M. 2057



October 24, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, dated December 30, 1976, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner board brought a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7503(b) to stay arbitration respondent union sought to invoke as a provision of its collective bargaining contract, which had provided for a five percent salary increase and periodic additional payments. The board sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, which granted the union's cross-motion to compel arbitration.


OVERVIEW: The union failed to receive the salary increases for which it had contracted with the board because the legislature adopted the Financial Emergency Act for the City of Yonkers (FEA), N.Y. Laws, ch. 871 (1975), which suspended all salary increases of covered organizations. The union asserted that the wage freeze violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, the constitutional ban of any state to impair the obligation of contracts. The court found that there was no impairment of the collective bargaining agreement, only a temporary suspension of increases. The union asserted the FEA violated equal protection by leaving unimpaired the contract rights of other contractors with the city, but the court found no taking of or deprivation of property, only a suspension of the wage increase payment. The court found no basis in the language of the arbitration provisions of the collective bargaining agreement for granting a stay of arbitration because although the statutory ban precluded an arbitrator from fashioning a remedy violative of the statute, it allowed the arbitration to address other issues. Further, the wage freeze was not unconstitutional, as it was a valid exercise of police power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court to compel arbitration.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- emergency suspension of municipal employee wage increases -- arbitrability.

1. Section 10 of the Financial Emergency Act for the City of Yonkers (L 1975, ch 871), which suspended all salary and wage increases of city employees taking effect after November 20, 1975, is constitutional, except insofar as it prohibits calculation of pension benefits based upon the suspended increases. The impairment of contract clause of the Constitution is qualified by the power of the States to maintain order and protect the public welfare, and there is no impairment, but only a suspension. There is no deprivation of due process, since the suspension is a proper exercise of the police power, and there is no deprivation of equal protection, since the distinction between public employees and other municipal creditors is a rational classification [***2] based upon the flexibility of the employment relationship. There is no deprivation of property without due process of law, since the wage increase is suspended instead of eliminated.

2. Respondent public employee union is entitled to arbitrate its grievance arising from the refusal of petitioner board of education pursuant to the Financial Emergency Act to pay wage increases, increments and longevity payments under a collective bargaining agreement between the parties. The agreement limits arbitrable grievances to claimed violations, misinterpretations or inequitable applications of the agreement, laws and rules of the board, which terms exclude an action reviewable by law. Express denial of grievance status in certain instances indicates that not all grievances reviewable by law are exempt from arbitration, and if every grievance reviewable by law were exempt, the arbitration provision would be illusory, since almost any grievance is reviewable in an action on contract or in an article 78 proceeding.

3. The arbitrator may fashion a remedy appropriate to the circumstances, preserving the pension rights of respondents and dealing with any rights respondents might have to a restoration [***3] of increases after suspension, but not conflicting with the ban on payment of increases during the emergency period.

 

1202 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Short v. Nassau County Civil Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 157; 398 N.Y.S.2d 540; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13280



October 11, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered April 5, 1973, modified, on the law, by (1) deleting the first decretal paragraph thereof, (2) deleting from the second decretal paragraph thereof the provision dismissing the petition in all respects and substituting therefor provisions (a) granting the petition to the extent of dismissing Specification 3 of Charge 1 and Specification 2 of Charge 2 and (b) reducing the penalty imposed upon the remaining charges to a suspension for a period of one year. As so modified, judgment affirmed, without costs or disbursements, and proceeding remanded to Special Term for a hearing to (1) determine the outside time limit within which petitioner could reasonably have been expected to perfect his appeal and (2) fix the amount of back pay due him, in accordance with the Per Curiam opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an accounting executive with the Nassau County Department of Social Services, instituted a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review the determination of respondent Nassau County Civil Service Commission. The Supreme Court at Special Term, Nassau County (New York) granted the commission's motion to dismiss the proceeding and the executive appealed.


OVERVIEW: The executive was dismissed from his position on the basis of misconduct and insubordination and dereliction of duty. The executive appealed to the commission pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 76, and the commission sustained the dismissal. The court determined that the commission's actions in sustaining the charges were purely arbitrary and that the penalty of dismissal was so disproportionate to the offense as to shock the court's sense of fairness. Accordingly, the court reduced the penalty to a one-year suspension, with an amount of back pay to be determined upon remand. The court held that the executive did not let his personal convictions interfere with the good faith performance of his duties and the record indicated that he sought guidance and legal advice on the issue of whether intrauterine devices were abortifacients. The was remanded to the trial court for a hearing to determine an outside time limit within which the executive could have reasonably been expected to perfect his appeal and to determine the compensation, in the form of back pay, to which the executive was entitled during that period.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment by reducing the penalty imposed on the executive to a one-year suspension and otherwise affirmed. The court remanded to the trial court to determine the outside time limit within which the executive could reasonably have been expected to perfect his appeal and to fix the amount of back pay due him.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- dismissal -- misconduct and insubordination -- penalties -- back pay.

1. The Nassau County Civil [***2] Service Commission arbitrarily sustained charges of misconduct against petitioner arising from a memorandum he issued as accounting executive of the Nassau County Department of Social Services ordering deletion from State and Federal reimbursement of the salaries of county medical center personnel who were making referrals of elective abortions. The memorandum was at most an overzealous application of the law as it stood at that time and the matter was dropped without harm to the department when overridden by petitioner's superior officer.

2. Although a finding of insubordination based upon petitioner's refusal to certify claims for reimbursement for expenditures for intrauterine devices (IUDs) was not arbitrary, the penalty should be a one-year suspension and not dismissal. The court has the right to set aside administrative disciplinary rulings which are so disproportionate to the offense as to shock one's sense of fairness, and petitioner's injudicious actions do not merit dismissal. Although petitioner had raised a proper question concerning reimbursability of county expenses for IUDs in light of respected medical opinion that the devices are abortive in nature, instead of [***3] receiving an answer based upon law or policy, he was orally instructed to certify the claims. Petitioner had an excellent record and the county ultimately received reimbursement.

3. Accordingly, petitioner should receive back pay from the day following completion of one year of suspension. However, since petitioner's appeal should expeditiously have been heard prior to the close of the 1973 term of court, back pay should be paid only to that date, unless extenuating circumstances are found excusing the delay.

 

1203 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


First Nat'l City Bank v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 710; 398 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13677



October 11, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State of New York, the Department of Environmental Conservation, the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation (commissioner), and the Office of Parks and Recreation, appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which directed them (except the commissioner) to submit to disclosure pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3102(f), in plaintiff trustee's action to have the Tidal Wetlands Act declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The trustee owned over 500 acres of land on behalf of several trusts. The uses to which the land could be put were drastically limited by the Tidal Wetlands Act (Act), N.Y. Envt'l Conserv. Law art. 25. The county commenced a condemnation proceeding and title to the land has vested in the county. The trustee then brought the instant action seeking a declaration that the Act violated its federal constitutional rights. The trustee sought to compel defendants to submit to disclosure, but the supreme court held that a motion made under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3102(f) did not require the production of documents or permit any specific type of disclosure, and that an order entered pursuant to that section merely permitted the trustee to utilize the disclosure devices provided for in N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 31. The court concluded that the statute's usual meaning indicated that the trustee had to apply to the supreme court for an order permitting disclosure because the state was a defendant. The court modified the order to require defendants to submit to oral depositions as to the manner in which the Act would be applied and the nature of defendants' roles in condemning the land, and otherwise affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court modified the supreme court's order to the extent that defendants would submit to depositions upon oral examination as to the manner in which the Act had been and would be applied and the nature and evolution of defendants' roles in financing and otherwise participating in the condemnation of the land. As so modified, the court affirmed the supreme court's order.

 

1204 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Stato v. Squicciarini


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 718; 398 N.Y.S.2d 373; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13690



October 11, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged a decision by the Supreme Court of Orange County (New York), which dismissed the declaratory judgment cause of action as moot and granted a motion by respondents gravel pit owners and a cross-motion that sought dismissal of the injunctive cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The pit owners sought a special use permit from the planning board so that they might operate a gravel bank on property owned by them. The court ordered the planning board to issue the requested permit pursuant to a provision of the zoning law. The landowners then commenced an action seeking various types of relief, alleging that the provision of the zoning law, which gave the planning board authority to issue special use permits was in violation of the town law and, therefore, was unconstitutional. The landowners also sought to enjoin the operation of the gravel bank. The supreme court dismissed both causes of action. The court held that the contention that the town board's delegation of authority to the planning board was unconstitutional was rendered moot because the Legislature explicitly sanctioned such a delegation during the pendency of the matter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the landowners' action.

 

1205 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Windsor Park Tenants' Asso. v. New York City Conciliation & Appeals Board


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 121; 397 N.Y.S.2d 828; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12881



August 29, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Queens County, dated December 14, 1976, affirmed, without costs or disbursements, and with a direction that the escrow agreement with regard to rent increases be continued as to future rents pending the final determination of the issues in this proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, New York City Conciliation and Appeals Board (CAB) and the landlord, sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Queens County (New York), which remanded, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, the grant of a comparative hardship rent increase to the CAB for further hearings to afford respondent tenants an opportunity to examine the landlord's books at the tenants' expense.


OVERVIEW: The landlord purchased the 20-building complex in 1969. In 1975, the landlord requested a comparative hardship rate increase from the CAB. The landlord offered reports certified by independent CPAs. The tenants requested to inspect the landlord's books but the CAB denied the request The tenants objected that a rent increase should not be retroactive to the filing date because of the landlord's unresponsiveness. The CAB granted the landlord a comparative hardship increase under the New York City Rent Stabilization Law, New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code tit. YY, § 51 (1969), effective the date of filing. The tenants brought a petition in the trial court, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, challenging the increase. The trial court granted the petition, averring that the tenants risked valuable property rights in the hearing and remanding the case to the CAB to permit the tenants, at their expense, to examine the books. On appeal, the court held that the tenants had no constitutional property rights against increased rents; however, the CAB had to conduct a meaningful review of the application, including an independent financial inspect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which remanded the case to the CAB to permit the tenants to review, at their own expense, the landlords' books to determined whether the comparative hardship rent increase was proper. The court also directed that the escrow for future rents be continued until the conclusion of the proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation.

1. Although the New York City Conciliation and Appeals Board (CAB) is funded by owners of real property through the Real Estate Industry Association, it is statutorily empowered to hear complaints by tenants and requests by landlords for hardship rental increases under the New York City Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 (Administrative Code, tit YY, ch 51) and its members are appointed by the Mayor. Notwithstanding [***2] that tenants have no constitutional, vested property rights against rent increases, the board must conduct a meaningful review of owner applications and cannot subdelegate its functions. Accordingly, upon an application for a hardship rental increase it was improper for the CAB to accept the findings of certified public accountants retained by the respondent landlord with regard to base period rents and expenses, subject only to any contrary information which might be supplied by the petitioner tenants, while simultaneously refusing the tenants the right to audit the landlord's accounts. An audit should have been conducted by the CAB or a tenant audit should have been permitted.

2. Although the determination of the CAB accepting the base period supplied by respondent landlord was properly remanded, because made without benefit of an independent audit, the determination would otherwise be entitled to controlling weight because made by the agency administering the Rent Stabilization Law. The determination was rationally and consistently made upon evidence that the owner could not obtain records of periods prior to its taking title and, accordingly, could not utilize the statutory [***3] base period in full.

3. The CAB is a city housing rent agency under section 8 of the Local Emergency Housing Rent Control Act (L 1962, ch 21, § 1) and, accordingly, is entitled under CPLR 5519 to a stay of the judgment made by Special Term remanding the agency's determination.

 

1206 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Beltrami v. Nadjari


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 568; 397 N.Y.S.2d 825; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13355



August 23, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate challenged a decision by the Supreme Court of Queens County (New York), which granted the applications and directed the board of elections to remove the candidate's name from the Republican Party line of the ballot in any primary or general election in a proceeding to invalidate petitions designating the candidate in the Republican Party primary election for the public office of District Attorney.


OVERVIEW: The candidate was registered and voted in Suffolk County and moved to Queens County. The candidate completed and executed an application for the purpose of transferring his registration by mail pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 153. The lower court found that a transfer of registration and enrollment from Suffolk County to Queens County was not made by mail, and that the candidate was not an enrolled member of the Republican Party as required on the day his designating petitions were filed. The court held that the Legislature amended the statute so as to allow for inter-county transfers of enrollment with immediate voting rights. The court held that it authorized the transfer of the candidate's enrollment from Suffolk County to Queens County and gave the candidate the right to be designated as a candidate by petition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, denied the applications, and the board of elections was directed to place the name of the candidate upon the appropriate ballot.

 

1207 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Carroll v. Ingram


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


59 A.D.2d 85; 397 N.Y.S.2d 220; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12459



August 4, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition and complaint dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, petitioner permit applicant's agent (applicant) challenged the determination of respondent zoning board of appeals (board), which denied a special use permit to the applicant to continue the former operations of a race track that the applicant intended to purchase. The applicant also sought a declaration that respondent town's comprehensive zoning ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Certain land within the town was formerly used as a race track, but its stock car races had been terminated for several years. After its closure, a number of residences and a commercial development were built adjacent to the track. The applicant sought to purchase this property and once again utilize it as a stock car race track. The board denied the applicant a special use permit to continue the former operations, and the court confirmed the board's determination. The court held that stock car racing was not an activity that was expressly permitted by the town's ordinance; thus, the board's refusal to grant the special use permit was proper. The denial of prior non-conforming use status was also proper because the race track had not been in continuous operation for a long time, nor was it at the time that the ordinance was enacted. The applicant was not entitled to a variance because it failed to demonstrate that the property in question could not be reasonably employed in conformity with the relevant zoning regulations. Further, a reasonable return to the agent could be obtained from permitted uses and a mere lessening of profits did not render the ordinance confiscatory.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the board's determination that denied a special use permit to the applicant.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning.

1. An application by petitioners for site plan approval or a special use permit to continue a stock car race track formerly operated on certain property was properly denied. Since the proposed use had nothing to do with housing or the enjoyment of mountain land, stock car racing would not be permitted under the provisions of the town zoning ordinance. The terms "Park, reservation and/or recreation facility" found in the ordinance must be interpreted in their most obvious sense and an examination of the ordinance reveals a legislative intent that the terms apply only to noncommercial parks and recreational facilities. Moreover, a commonsense evaluation of the wording is enough to exclude a stock [***2] car race track.

2. The denial of prior nonconforming use status was proper as the race track had not been in continuous operation. The evidence established an intent to abandon the use as a speedway nine years prior to the adoption of the zoning ordinance.

3. Petitioners are not entitled to a variance for they failed to demonstrate that the property could not be reasonably employed in conformity with the zoning regulations.

4. The zoning ordinance is not unconstitutional. There is no unlawful delegation to respondent. Standards and guidelines are clearly expressed in the ordinance. A reasonable return to petitioners may be obtained from permitted uses and a mere lessening of profits does not render the ordinance confiscatory.

 

1208 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. O'Neill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 540; 397 N.Y.S.2d 408; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13330



August 1, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which convicted him of manslaughter in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: At the time of the alleged crimes defendant was a police officer. He fatally shot the victim, who was the manager of a bar. The victim was also the boyfriend of defendant's daughter, who had been absent from home for three days. She was 19 years old at the time and had a history of drug abuse. Defendant was charged with one count of manslaughter for causing the death of another while acting with intent to cause serious physical injury. A second count charged him with causing the death of another with intent to cause the death of another while acting under the influence of extreme emotional disturbance. The second count was dismissed at trial. The trial court reinstated the second count for retrial after jury failed to reach verdict on first count. The court reversed, holding that a second trial of the dismissed second count was unconstitutional as violative of the right not to be placed twice in jeopardy for the same offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for manslaughter in the first degree.

 

1209 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ames Volkswagen, Ltd. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 454; 397 N.Y.S.2d 173; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12428



July 28, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant automobile dealers challenged the decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which declared that the provisions of 1976 N.Y. Laws 89 were valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The automobile dealers in the state of New York sought a judgment enjoining the state from enforcing N.Y. Tax Law § 1137-A, and from imposing penalties and interest under N.Y. Tax Law § 1145(a), and a determination that N.Y. Tax Law § 1137-A and portions of N.Y. Tax Law § 1145 were unconstitutional. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of such statutes. The court affirmed, holding that it was constitutionally permissible to require a taxpayer to estimate and prepay taxes in a number of tax situations. Under N.Y. Tax Law § 1132, a vendor responsible for collecting sales tax held such taxes as trustee for and on account of the state. The obligations of such a vendor were those of a taxpayer. No legitimate basis was raised for distinguishing sales taxpayers from other taxpayers required to estimate and prepay their tax obligations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality of provisions of 1976 N.Y. Laws 89.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- sales taxes -- estimated sales tax returns and payments.

1. Section 1137-A of the Tax Law (added by L 1976, ch 89), which requires certain large-volume vendors to file, as part of their sales tax return and payment due on March 20, a return setting forth the total sales taxes estimated to be payable by them for the month of March, and to pay to the State Tax Commission the tax so calculated, and section 1145 (subd [a]) of that law (as amd by L 1976, ch 89), which subjects those vendors to interest and penalties for underestimating by more than 10% the actual sales tax found to be due at the end of March, are constitutional. The procedure set forth therein is neither an arbitrary nor unreasonable procedure for the collection of revenue by the State.

2. It has been held to be constitutionally permissible to require a taxpayer to estimate and prepay taxes in a number of tax situations, and, while, under [***2] section 1132 of the Tax Law, a vendor responsible for collecting sales tax holds it as trustee for and on account of the State, the obligations of such a vendor are those of a taxpayer. No legitimate basis has been raised for distinguishing sales taxpayers from other taxpayers required to estimate and prepay their tax obligations.

 

1210 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Carter v. Carter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


58 A.D.2d 438; 397 N.Y.S.2d 88; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12426



July 25, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Family Court, Rockland County, dated October 22, 1976, reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and proceeding remanded to the Family Court for determination by it of the needs of the child of the parties and for the proper apportionment thereof between the parties in accordance with their respective means.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father sought review of a decision from the Family Court (New York) that dismissed his request to direct respondent mother to contribute to the child's support. The father had custody of the parties' infant son.


OVERVIEW: The father sought to receive child support from the mother for their infant son, who was in the father's custody. The lower court dismissed the father's request but, on appeal, the court reversed the decision and ordered the cause to be remanded. The court had to determine whether §§ 413 and 414 of the Family Court Act were unconstitutional insofar as they appeared to place the primary duty of child support on the father and made the mother's duty of support of the child secondary by making that obligation conditional on the incapability of the father to support the child. The court read the two sections as meaning that both the father and the mother were equally responsible for the support of the child and that it was within the power of the Family Court to apportion the costs of support between them in accordance with their respective means and responsibilities, without regard to the parent's sex. It followed that the lower court was in error when it read the two sections as barring it from apportioning part of the cost of support to the mother unless the father could establish that he was incapable of supporting his child, a condition which he conceded he could not meet.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the proceeding to the lower court for a determination by it of the needs of the child of the parties and for the proper apportionment thereof between the parties, consistent with their respective means. The court found that support was to be provided to the infant equally between the father and the mother and it was the duty of the lower court to apportion the costs of such support between them.


HEADNOTES

Parent and child -- support of children -- apportionment of support.

1. Under sections 413 and 414 of the Family Court Act, the father and the mother of a child are equally responsible for its support, and the Family Court has the power, and the duty, to apportion the costs of such support between them in accordance with their respective means and responsibilities, without regard to the sex of the parent, even when it appears that the father is fully capable of supporting the child out of his own means.

2. Section 413 of the Family Court Act, which provides that the "father of a child under the age of twenty-one years is chargeable with the support of his child and, if possessed [***2] of sufficient means or able to earn such means, may be required to pay for such child's support a fair and reasonable sum according to his means, as the court may determine", does not make the duty of support the primary obligation of the father, either standing by itself or when read with the opening language of section 414 of the act, which provides that "If the father of a child is dead, incapable of supporting his child, or cannot be found within the state, the mother of such child is chargeable with its support * * * and, if possessed of sufficient means or able to earn such means, may be required to pay for its support a fair and reasonable sum according to her means, as the court may determine. The court may apportion the costs of the support of the child between the parents according to their respective means and responsibilities". The opening language of section 414 does not mean that the mother becomes liable for contribution to the support of the child only if the father is dead, incapable of supporting the child or cannot be found within the State. And, if the section were susceptible to an interpretation that the mother's obligation is a secondary one, which would result [***3] in unequal treatment of the parents on the basis of their sex, in violation of the constitutional guarantee of equal protection, its opening language may be excised in order to sustain its constitutionality.

 

1211 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Segar v. Youngs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 368; 396 N.Y.S.2d 912; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12416



July 21, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, by reversing so much thereof as denies summary judgment to plaintiffs against defendant Reuss, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant first purchaser sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) that denied in part his motion for summary judgment against appellee second owner in a property dispute.


OVERVIEW: The matter before the court concerned the sale of certain property. The first purchaser obtained a judgment of foreclosure and sale against the second owner. The second owner failed to redeem any of the purchased tax liens. The court determined that the second owner's tax sale certificate was the result of a private lien. The court stated that municipal tax sales provided for by statute were in essence budgetary provisions that enabled the respective municipality to realize by sale of tax sale certificates the uncollected taxes for the year involved and thereby carry out budgetary expenditures and protect municipal borrowing credit. The court held such purpose should not be frustrated by permitting municipal liens to be extinguished when the property was conveyed by the municipality to the holder of a tax sale certificate. The court held the order should be modified.


OUTCOME: The court held the order should be modified by reversing the portion that denied the first purchaser summary judgment against the second owner.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax liens, tax sales and tax titles -- conveyance of property for unpaid taxes -- encumbrances.

1. A conveyance of real property by the county to the plaintiff holders of a tax sale certificate purchased four years earlier extinguishes the interest of defendant, a subsequent purchaser of a village tax certificate on the subject property, since section 1020 of the Real Property Tax Law et seq., provides that a county conveyance vests in the grantee an absolute estate in fee, subject only to county and State encumbrances, actual occupancy and recorded mortgages. It is irrelevant that defendant's certificate had been owned previously by the village, since it is still a private lien. Accordingly, summary judgment [***2] should have been granted to plaintiffs in their action to clear title.

2. Special Term properly denied summary relief to plaintiffs and granted such relief to the defendant village on its cross motion with regard to a tax certificate held by the village. Although section 1020 of the Real Property Tax Law subjects a county conveyance only to county and State encumbrances, it does not follow that village encumbrances are extinguished. Tax sales provide a salutary scheme for payment of municipal taxes and that scheme should not be frustrated by permitting municipal liens to be extinguished.

3. Summary judgment was properly granted against the defendant owner of the subject property, who failed to redeem the tax certificates and another holder of a tax certificate who did not answer or appear.

Gowan v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 936; 397 N.Y.S.2d 427; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13137



July 21, 1977 

 

1213 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Leonard


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


59 A.D.2d 1; 397 N.Y.S.2d 386; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12441



July 18, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, rendered July 16, 1975, reversed, on the law and the facts, plea of guilty vacated, and motion to suppress statements made by defendant granted to the extent that all statements made by him on July 11, 1974, subsequent to 6:00 p.m., are suppressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), convicting him of murder in the second degree, arson in the second degree, burglary in the second degree, and petit larceny.


OVERVIEW: Defendant first confessed to burglary of a building. After being taken some distance from his home by officers and undergoing an intermittent but intense interrogation over a period of more than 12 hours, defendant confessed to intentionally starting a fire that killed 24 people. He pleaded guilty and was convicted. Defendant appealed, alleging that his confession should have been suppressed. The court reversed the conviction, vacated the guilty plea, and granted the motion to suppress some of the statements. The court considered the totality of the circumstances and found: (1) defendant's faculties were impaired; (2) he was physically separated from familiar surroundings; (3) the interrogation was lengthy and intense; (4) arraignment was delayed; (5) the evening polygraphic examination was misused; (6) officers' false statements and threats impaired defendant's capacity for self-determination; and (7) the questioning was suggestive. The court concluded that only those admissions made after 6:00 p.m. were involuntary and, therefore, inadmissible.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment convicting defendant of murder in the second degree, arson in the second degree, burglary in the second degree, and petit larceny, vacated defendant's guilty plea, and granted his motion to suppress statements made after 6:00 p.m. on the date in question.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- confessions -- voluntariness.

1. In a prosecution for murder in the second degree, arson in the second degree, burglary in the second degree and petit larceny, the People have not sustained their burden of proving beyond a reasonable doubt that statements made by defendant during and immediately after an evening interrogation were voluntarily made. Defendant had slept only three hours the night before and had requested that a polygraph examination held earlier in the day be terminated because he felt ill. Defendant had eaten only lunch from 6:00 a.m. until 10:30 p.m.,  [***2] when he was delivered to the jail, and was under severe emotional stress, as evidenced by his repeated crying. The interrogating officers arranged for a place for examination far from defendant's home and questioned him intermittently, but intensely, for over a 12-hour period, repeatedly telling him that "the professionals" could not accept his story, that he was lying and that he had "torched that place". In view of a statement defendant made earlier in the day, it is clear that he would not have been free to leave. Nevertheless, no arrest was made and, although there was technical compliance with the State of Connecticut's arraignment statute, it appears that arraignment was delayed. The polygraph was misused to extract defendant's confession when defendant was told that the examiner was there to help him, that the machine was infallible and that only the guilty wished the attachments removed. False statements and threats served to overbear defendant's will. He was told that he would be thrown through a window for failing to look at his interrogator, that his bail would be raised and he would be shipped to New York State, but that, if he stated that he used chemicals to "torch"  [***3] the building, there would be no murder charges. The suggestiveness of the questioning rendered the statements inherently unreliable. After intense questioning, defendant was told that he had lit a piece of paper with a match and he then simply agreed or parroted what he had been told to say. The totality of the circumstances renders the statements inadmissible, while statements made earlier in the day were admissible.

2. Defendant's confession to burglary was not a confession to felony murder, since he did not admit causing deaths in the furtherance of the burglary, but stated only that he had accidentally started the fire.

 

1214 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sturman v. Public Health Council


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 389; 397 N.Y.S.2d 168; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12419



July 14, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nursing home operator brought a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to review a determination of respondents, the Public Health Council and others, which revoked her approval of establishment of a nursing home.


OVERVIEW: The nursing home owner's approval for the establishment of a nursing was revoked. She challenged this, contending that the grant of power to the Public Health Council in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2801-a(10)(a) was unconstitutional because of a failure to incorporate legislative standards therein. The court disagreed, finding that statute did enunciate certain standards which had to be applied in granting a license and that these standards governed as guidelines and standards for the revocation of the prior approval of an establishment, including the limitations inherent in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2800 as they related to the continuance of a prior approval. The court found that there had not been an unconstitutional delegation of power to the council in regard to providing for the revocation of approval of an establishment. Further, since the regulation did not mandate a revocation upon a conviction and was discretionary, the court found that it authorized a revocation unless the conviction undermined the standards relating to the original issuance of an approval and in conjunction with the guidelines set forth in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2800.


OUTCOME: The determination that revoked the approval of the nursing home owner's establishment of a nursing home was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals and asylums -- nursing homes -- revocation of approval.

1. After petitioner, who held an approval from the Public Health Council to establish a nursing home, pleaded guilty to offering a false instrument for filing in the first degree, a felony, based upon transactions involving the Medicaid reimbursement forms utilized in the nursing home operation, and following a hearing at which careful consideration was given to all of the proof, including those matters which would relate to the effect of her conviction in terms of the standards which would be applicable in granting an approval for an establishment, the council revoked her approval. Its determination was supported by substantial evidence, and it is confirmed.

2. Paragraph (a) of subdivision 10 of section [***2] 2801-a of the Public Health Law, authorizing the council to adopt rules and regulations to effectuate the provisions of that section, which relates to establishment of hospitals, including nursing homes, and requires the council's approval before establishment thereof, and authorizing it to provide for the revocation, limitation or annulment of approvals, is not unconstitutional for failure to incorporate legislative standards therein. In subdivision 3 of the section, certain standards are enunciated which must be applied in granting an approval, and these same standards, by any reasonable interpretation of the powers granted, including the adoption of rules and regulations, would govern as standards for the revocation of a prior approval, including the limitations inherent in section 2800 of the law as they relate to continuance of a prior approval.

3. Pursuant to the authority granted in section 2801-a (subd 10, par [a]) of the Public Health Law, the council has adopted a regulation providing that an approval of establishment "may" be revoked if the council finds that the established operator has been convicted of a "crime" (10 NYCRR 600.5 [e]). Viewed literally, this regulation [***3] would appear to exceed the implicit statutory guidelines by permitting a revocation simply upon proof of conviction of a crime, which language would include both misdemeanors and felonies and might factually have no substantial bearing upon the continuation of a prior approval. However, since it does not mandate revocation but is discretionary, it is not found to authorize a revocation unless the conviction undermines the standards for approval of an establishment, to which standards all the grounds for revocation must be related.

4. The fact that petitioner received a certificate of relief from disabilities does not prevent the council from exercising its power to revoke her approval.

 

1215 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mulroy v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


58 A.D.2d 207; 396 N.Y.S.2d 929; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11857



July 12, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed, without costs, and petition dismissed in accordance with opinion by Witmer, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state governor and associated individuals, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which denied appellants' motion to dismiss the petition filed by respondents, County Executive and Chief Budget Officer and district attorney, in a case concerning the validity of an executive order that directed the county to pay the costs of an investigation.


OVERVIEW: Respondents initiated a proceeding against appellants and sought to enjoin appellants from proceeding with an investigation into the alleged unlawful acts of persons within the county. Respondents specifically challenged the validity of the executive order which provided that the county was to pay the expenses of the investigation and that removed the county district attorney from office. The lower court denied appellants' motion to dismiss and appellants sought review. The court reversed the lower court order and granted appellants' motion to dismiss on the ground that no justifiable issue existed for trial. The court determined that the executive order was legal and valid because the governor invoked his power to order the investigation and the governor's exercise of his executive power was not subject to judicial review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court order, granted appellants' motion to dismiss, and dismissed respondents' petition.


HEADNOTES

Governor -- directive to Attorney-General to investigate unlawful acts -- superseding District Attorney.

1. Executive Orders Nos. 42 and 42.1 issued by the Governor directing the Attorney-General to investigate alleged unlawful acts of persons in Onondaga County with respect to payments in connection with obtaining public office or contracts, and suspending the District Attorney of that county are legal and valid and not subject to attack. Section 3 of article IV of the State Constitution charges the Governor with the duty to "take care that the laws are faithfully executed" and the Legislature has expressly implemented that provision by enacting subdivision 2 of section 63 of the Executive Law requiring the Attorney-General to conduct such proceedings before a Supreme Court and Grand Jury as the Governor shall direct concerning [***2] the investigation of criminal activities and to supersede the District Attorney in so doing. By the terms of the orders, the Governor invoked his powers on information received from a Grand Jury and the District Attorney of New York County. The Governor stated the basis for his action in Executive Order No. 42 and the exercise of his discretion is not subject to judicial review.

2. The enactment of article 16-A of the Election Law, transferring to the State Board of Elections the former authority of the Attorney-General to investigate election frauds, was not designed to and did not release the Governor from the duty imposed on him by the Constitution. Moreover, the Legislature did not repeal subdivision 2 of section 63 of the Executive Law. The Governor's authority is all embracing and Order No. 42 may contemplate broader action than that envisaged under article 16-A.

3. The application to limit the bills for expenses which may be presented to Onondaga County in connection with the investigation is premature.

 

1216 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Sullivan County Democratic Committee


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 169; 397 N.Y.S.2d 163; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11851



July 7, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant democratic committee sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which granted appellee voters' motion for summary judgment and directed the committee to amend their rules so as to provide for a system of weighted voting, pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 12, on all voting matters before the committee.


OVERVIEW: The committee's rules provided for the election of two members per election district, with no additional members. The committee's rules also provided for weighted voting only in selecting party nominees but not with regard to its voting on "internal" party matters. The court noted that N.Y. Elec. Law § 12(1) required such weighted voting without excepting any category or subject matter in county committees. The committee argued that the statute was unconstitutional. The court held that the method of apportionment established by N.Y. Elec. Law § 12(1) did not violate the constitution. The legislature was empowered to act upon the assumption that the proportionate party vote for a public office in various election districts within a county had a sufficiently substantial and direct mathematical relationship to the proportionate party enrollment in the various election districts so as to provide a satisfactory formula for weighing the votes of committee members. Accordingly, the order directing the committee to amend their rules was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order requiring the committee to amend their rules so as to provide for a system of weighted voting was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- county committees -- voting power of committee members.

1. A county committee was properly directed to amend its rules so as to provide for a system of weighted voting on all voting matters before it, including voting on internal party matters as well as selecting party nominees. Its rules provide for the election of two committee members per election district, with no additional members, and, under section 12 (subd 1) of the Election Law, where no additional members are provided for by the rules of the committee, the voting power of each member shall be in proportion to the party vote in the district for Governor or, in specified situations, for certain other elective officers. That section makes no distinction between selection of party nominees and [***2] internal party affairs.

2. Section 12 (subd 1) of the Election Law is constitutional. Reasonable legislation regulating party committees is valid, and, while it may be that the "one man, one vote" principle need not be applied to internal party matters, there is nothing to indicate that it may not be properly applied to such matters. Nor does the section work an unconstitutional disenfranchisement of enrolled party members in basing the weighted voting system upon the vote for elective office rather than upon actual party enrollment. While apportionment by party enrollment may be preferable, this does not mean that the scheme as enacted by the Legislature is without rational basis. It is empowered to act upon the assumption that the proportionate party vote for a public office in various election districts within a county bears a sufficiently substantial and direct mathematical relationship to the proportionate party enrollment in the various districts so as to provide a satisfactory formula for weighing the votes of committee members.

Klein v. Klein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


58 A.D.2d 811; 396 N.Y.S.2d 266; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12978



July 5, 1977 

 

1218 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cecchini


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 713; 396 N.Y.S.2d 708; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12825



June 30, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the County Court of Ulster County (New York), which convicted him on his plea of guilty to criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree and sentenced him to an indeterminate term of six years to life in prison. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in failing to inquire into the facts of the crime at the time of his guilty plea.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted after his guilty plea to sale of a controlled substance in the second degree and sentenced to an indeterminate term of six years to life in prison. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the trial court questioned defendant thoroughly and was satisfied that he fully understood the consequences of pleading guilty. The court ruled that defendant's disagreement with the prosecutor's outline of his involvement in the crimes did not require the trial court to question him extensively about the facts because defendant was represented by competent counsel and admitted that his plea was to his benefit by minimizing his potential sentence. The court found that defendant waived his challenge to the composition of the jury panel when he waived his right to a jury trial, and that he had ample time to examine the jury list, but chose to plead guilty to the lesser offense instead. The court concluded that the sentencing statutes under N.Y. Penal Law art. 220 did not impose cruel and unusual punishment, rejecting defendant's argument that cocaine was a relatively harmless drug.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant on his plea of guilty to sale of a controlled substance in the second degree and sentencing him to an indeterminate term of imprisonment with a mandatory maximum of life and a minimum of six years.

 

1219 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hunter v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


58 A.D.2d 136; 396 N.Y.S.2d 186; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11844; 3 Media L. Rep. 1025



June 28, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on or about February 10, 1977, unanimously modified, on the law, to the extent of striking therefrom the paragraph providing for granting of defendants' motion dismissing the complaint; declaring unconstitutional the provision of Local Law No. 1 of 1975 that the information furnished pursuant thereto is to be made available to the public as a matter of course; and otherwise declaring Local Law No. 1 to be constitutional and the order and judgment (one paper) is otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from an order and judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County (New York), which granted a motion by defendants for summary judgment dismissing the class action complaint brought by plaintiffs to enjoin enforcement of a local law requiring financial disclosure by city employees earning a salary of $ 25,000 or more.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs occupied positions in the civil service of New York City. The lower court upheld the constitutionality of the law. Plaintiffs contended that the legislation infringed upon their right of privacy in their financial and marital relationships and had a chilling effect upon their right of association, belief and expression. Plaintiffs argued that these were fundamental rights under the Constitution, so the legislation should be examined with strict scrutiny. Plaintiffs further claimed that there was no rational basis for distinguishing between employees earning $ 25,000 or more and those earning less than $ 25,000 and that the law was vague and ambiguous. The court concluded that the law's provision for disclosure of the financial statements without affording the employee the opportunity to present a claim for privacy should be stricken and that disclosure not be made public absent the implementation of such safeguards as were consistent with due process. Thus, the court modified the judgment by declaring unconstitutional the provision of the law that the information furnished was to be made available to the public and otherwise held that the law was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by declaring unconstitutional the provision of the law that the information furnished was to be made available to the public, but otherwise held that the law was constitutional. Thus, the court struck the paragraph of the judgment providing for the granting of defendants' motion dismissing the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- financial disclosure by public employees -- right of privacy.

1. Local Law No. 1 of 1975 of the City of New York requiring every city employee earning a salary of $ 25,000 or more to file an annual questionnaire disclosing the financial affairs of the employee and his or her spouse relating to outside sources of income is [***2] not constitutionally offensive when the rights and interests of city employees, as citizens, are balanced against the rights and interests of the city, as employer. The requirement of disclosure by spouses is not violative of a right of privacy and the requirement that those earning $ 25,000 or more disclose their affairs, without any showing that those persons exercised discretion or performed management functions, does not constitute a constitutionally impermissible classification, since that classification has a rational basis. (See Evans v Carey, 40 NY2d 1008.)

2. However, public employees do not surrender their constitutional right to due process. Deprivation of liberty is entitled to the protection of due process and privacy is a right within the concept of liberty. Insofar as Local Law No. 1 requires that the detailed disclosure be made available to the public, it is invalid. There is an insensitivity to rights of privacy and a disregard of claims of irrelevancy. Accordingly, the provision in Local Law No. 1 for disclosure of the financial statements without affording employees the opportunity to present a claim for privacy should be stricken and disclosure should [***3] not be made public absent the implementation of such safeguards as are consistent with due process.

Beltone Electronics Corp. v. Selbst


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


58 A.D.2d 560; 396 N.Y.S.2d 21; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12564



June 28, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an Illinois hearing aid franchisee (franchisee), appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied the franchisee's motion for a preliminary injunction against defendant, a former franchisor, and dismissed the franchisee's complaint for failure to comply with N.Y. Bus. Corp. Law § 1312.


OVERVIEW: The franchisee sought injunctive relief and damages against the former franchisor for continuing to do business using the franchisee's name after the termination of their agreement. The trial court denied the franchisee's motion for a preliminary injunction and granted the franchisor's motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the franchisee failed to comply with N.Y. Bus. Corp. Law § 1312. The court reversed. The court found that the affidavits presented provided insufficient evidence to decide as a matter of law that the franchisee was "doing business" in New York so as to be subject to § 1312. The court found that the question of whether a company was "doing business" for the purposes of this statute was not the inquiry made when considering the issue of jurisdiction. The court noted that a corporation could be doing sufficient business to subject it to service of process, but not enough to require it to obtain a license under § 1312. The court remanded for a factual determination of whether the franchisee was "doing business" within the meaning of § 1312.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order which denied the franchisee's motion for a preliminary injunction against the former franchisor. The court also reversed the order which dismissed the franchisee's complaint for failure to comply with the New York Business Corporation Law, and remanded for a hearing in the issue of that compliance.

Strescon Industries, Inc. v. Boulevard Manor, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


58 A.D.2d 561; 396 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12565



June 28, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a corporate landowner and others, sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which granted the motion of respondent lienor to intervene in an mechanic's lien action as a subsequent lienor and party defendant pursuant to N.Y. Lien Law § 62.


OVERVIEW: The lienor filed a motion to intervene in a mechanics' lien action as a subsequent lienor and a party defendant under § 62. § 62 provided, inter alia, that, upon good cause shown, the court was required to order a lienor who had filed a notice of lien after the commencement of an action to foreclose or enforce a mechanics' lien to be brought in by amendment. On appeal from an order granting the motion, appellants claimed that § 62 was violated due process of law, arguing that the failure of § 62 to specifically provide for service of responsive pleadings and for the conducting of pretrial disclosure inherently prejudiced the landowner. The court affirmed, noting, inter alia, that appellants' contention that § 62 was unconstitutional was insufficient to overcome the presumption of constitutionality. Further, the trial court, under § 62, retained sufficient control of the action to frustrate appellants' abstract claim that §62 was violative on its face of due process. Moreover, appellants were afforded an opportunity by the trial court to obtain disclosure with respect to respondent. Appellants were granted leave to apply to the trial court for the time to conduct such disclosure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to grant the lienor's motion to intervene in an mechanics' lien action as a subsequent lienor and a party defendant.

People v. Figueroa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


58 A.D.2d 655; 396 N.Y.S.2d 63; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12747



June 27, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendant sought to reverse his weapons conviction imposed by the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York) on the ground that his pretrial suppression motion should have been granted.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal defendant was arrested and charged with unlawful possession of a weapon. Prior to trial, he unsuccessfully sought to suppress the weapon, a firearm recovered after the police stopped him on the street on suspicion of robbery. He was convicted. On appeal, the state appellate court reversed his conviction, finding that the trial court erred in denying his pretrial suppression motion. The court held that appellant's motion should have been granted since he was stopped unconstitutionally. The court noted that appellant was within a block and a half of the robbery situs well after the robbery was committed, whereas it was likely the perpetrator would have been long gone by the time police arrived on the scene. The court also noted that his physical appearance did not match the description provided of the assailant and that he gave no indication that he was armed and presently dangerous when he was stopped. Given those facts, the court held that the weapon was unlawfully seized.


OUTCOME: Appellant's weapons possession conviction was reversed. The court held that the appellant's pretrial suppression motion should have been granted since inadequate grounds existed either to stop him or to frisk him.

Sephton v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department

Baycrest Manor, Inc. v. Commissioner of Environmental Conservation Dep't


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


58 A.D.2d 601; 395 N.Y.S.2d 486; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12649



June 13, 1977 

 

1225 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Redfield v. Melton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


57 A.D.2d 491; 395 N.Y.S.2d 725; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11402



June 9, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment in Action No. 1 affirmed, without costs. Judgment in Action No. 2 modified, on the law, by declaring that so much of chapter 50 of the Laws of 1976 as authorized the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles to establish fees sufficient to cover the total cost of operating the driver improvement program was unconstitutional, and, as so modified,  [***2] affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent commissioner and petitioner drivers sought review of two decisions of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) that found the state driver safety assessment fee regulations to be invalid.


OVERVIEW: Respondent motor vehicles department commissioner established regulations, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 15, § 170.2(a), permitting the doubling of the normal license fee for license renewal to those drivers who had one or two convictions or bail forfeitures within the 36-month period preceding the stated expiration date of the driver's license being renewed. Petitioner drivers objected to the fee. The reviewing court found that the regulations were unconstitutional. The legislature retained the authority to fix fees for the issuance of licenses. This was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power that violated N.Y. Const. art. III, § 1, because the legislature did not furnish the agency with rules and principles to guide its exercise of discretion.


OUTCOME: Two decisions finding that the state driver safety assessment fee regulations were invalid were affirmed and modified because the regulations were unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- increased fee for renewal license -- funding for driver improvement program.

Chapter 50 of the Laws of 1976 appropriated funds for a driver improvement program and provided that expenditures from that appropriation were to be reimbursed by receipts from fees established by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles sufficient to cover the cost of operating the program. Pursuant to that enactment, the commissioner promulgated regulations permitting the doubling of the normal license fee for license renewals to those drivers who have had one or two convictions or bail forfeitures within the 36-month period preceding the renewal. While the money was appropriated and the commissioner was authorized to set fees, the program was never instituted and such ambiguity is violative of section 1 of article III of the New York Constitution which provides that the legislative power of the State is vested in the Senate and the Assembly. The statute neither sets bounds to the delegated authority, nor formulates standards to govern the administrative exercise of the delegated discretion. The statute does not define what the driver improvement program [***3] is and thus leaves to the commissioner's sole discretion the determination of what activities are encompassed in the program. Such unfettered discretion in an area otherwise characterized by explicit legislation with respect to license fees and other charges stamps the subject legislation as an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority. Accordingly, so much of chapter 50 as authorized the commissioner to establish fees sufficient to cover the cost of the driver improvement program was unconstitutional.

 

1226 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rutkowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


58 A.D.2d 14; 395 N.Y.S.2d 729; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11806



June 9, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, by reversing so much thereof as granted plaintiffs summary judgment on their fourth cause of action, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state sought review of a judgment by the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendant landowners on the state's action to enjoin the landowners from selling or offering for sale any lot on the land they purchased until a subdivision plan was approved. The landowners cross-appealed the grant of summary judgment to the state on its nuisance action.


OVERVIEW: The state sought (1) to enjoin the landowners from selling any part of their land or erecting a permanent building upon their land until a subdivision plan was approved; (2) an assessment of civil penalties against the landowners pursuant to N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 17-1705,1707 and N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 12, 1116; and (3) to require the landowners to abate an alleged public nuisance. The trial court granted summary judgment to the landowners on all except the nuisance claim. On appeal, the court affirmed, but modified the judgment by reversing the grant of summary judgment to the state on its public nuisance claim. The court concurred with the trial court that N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 17-1705,1707 and N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 12, 1116 were so vague and indefinite that they were unconstitutional. The court found it would often be impossible for a person who wished to sell portions of his property to determine with any degree of certainty whether or not he would be creating a subdivision and had to comply with the statutes. Lastly, the court ruled that triable issues of fact existed as to whether the landowner' septic system was actually malfunctioning.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the landowners. However, the court modified the judgment by reversing the grant of summary judgment to the state on its nuisance action.


HEADNOTES

Health -- filing requirement for residential subdivisions.

1. Special Term properly granted summary judgment to defendant sellers of land declaring sections 1115 and 1116 of the Public Health Law and section 17-1505 of the Environmental Conservation Law unconstitutional. The statutes require subdivision plans to be filed with the Department of Health for sale of "any tract of land which is hereafter divided into five or more parcels along an existing or proposed street * * * for sale or for rent as residential lots". Absent any definition of "tract", "parcel"  [***2] or "residential lots", it would be impossible for a seller to determine if his sale constitutes a subdivision in such instances as sales from contiguous lands acquired under separate deeds or sales of portions of a parcel over a long period of time where each purchaser constructs a residence. Since the term "subdivision" is not sufficiently definite to warn "men of common intelligence" of the application of the enactments, the statutes are unconstitutional and the causes of action enjoining further sale, directing compliance and assessing civil penalties were properly dismissed.

2. Special Term improperly granted summary judgment to plaintiffs requiring defendants to correct a public nuisance, consisting of a faulty septic system on a lot they sold, since there is no evidence of a malfunction or whose responsibility such malfunction might be.

Ferrer v. Ferrer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


58 A.D.2d 529; 395 N.Y.S.2d 197; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12497



June 7, 1977 

 

1228 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. McGrath


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


57 A.D.2d 405; 394 N.Y.S.2d 885; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11812



May 31, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered on September 20, 1976, unanimously reversed, on the law, the motion to suppress granted and the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which convicted defendant upon the jury verdict of criminal contempt in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 215.51.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appeared before a grand jury and was granted transactional immunity. Defendant inquired if the grand jury questions to have been asked were based on evidence acquired through electronic surveillance. After the state answered in the affirmative, defendant's motion for a full suppression hearing was denied. Defendant then answered the grand jury questions "under protest." On the basis of defendant's grand jury testimony, he was indicted on the charge of criminal contempt in the first degree in violation of § 215.51. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress his grand jury testimony and convicted defendant upon the jury verdict of criminal contempt in the first degree. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, granted defendant's motion to suppress, and dismissed the indictment. The court held in part that defendant was entitled to have had his testimony suppressed and the indictment dismissed, as the grand jury questioning was the product of improper electronic surveillance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's conviction of defendant for criminal contempt in the first degree, granted defendant's motion to suppress his grand jury testimony, and dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- criminal contempt -- questions based on illegal electronic surveillance.

1. A Grand Jury witness who has been indicted for criminal contempt based on alleged equivocal, evasive and patently false answers may have such testimony suppressed and the indictment dismissed on the ground that the questioning was the product of improper electronic surveillance. The New York eavesdropping statutes were intended to harmonize State law with Federal law and section 2515 of the Omnibus Crime Control and Safe Streets Act of 1968 (US Code, tit 18, § 2515) has been viewed as an unequivocal bar to questioning a Grand Jury witness if the questions are derived from improper electronic surveillance. Accordingly, the contempt power may not be used to compel such testimony or punish the witness for his silence.

 [***2] 2. The decision of a Grand Jury witness to answer questions is not an independent intervening factor sufficient to dissipate the taint arising from the illegal eavesdropping which formed the basis for the questions and a witness who answers evasively may raise such eavesdropping as a defense to a contempt indictment. No distinction is served in contrasting contempt by evasion with contempt by a flat refusal to answer. The indictment is based on both questions and answers and not answers alone.

3. It was error to admit defendant's entire Grand Jury testimony at the trial for it included irrelevant matters. Some effort at redaction should have been made.

4. Upon defendant's raising of his Fourth Amendment right at the Grand Jury proceeding, defendant was properly taken before the court which inspected the surveillance orders in camera and directed defendant to answer. Defendant was not entitled to a full suppression hearing in the course of the Grand Jury inquiry.

5. Accordingly, the judgment convicting defendant of criminal contempt in the first degree, after a hearing in which a motion to suppress defendant's Grand Jury testimony was denied, should be reversed, on the [***3] law, the motion should be granted and the indictment dismissed.
