People v. Foggie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


57 A.D.2d 964; 395 N.Y.S.2d 68; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12247



May 31, 1977 

 

1230 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Stubbart v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


58 A.D.2d 25; 395 N.Y.S.2d 307; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11808



May 27, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the county, the County Sealer of Weights and Measures, and the district attorney, appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Monroe County (New York), in favor of petitioner, owner of a gasoline service station, in the owner's action challenging the validity of the Monroe County, N.Y., 1975 Local Law No. 2, §§ 1B4, 1B5 and the owner's petition to show cause and for a temporary restraining order.


OVERVIEW: The owner filed a petition to show cause and for a temporary restraining order and a declaratory judgment action seeking to have the maximum sign restrictions in §§ 1B4, 1B5 for advertising prices of gasoline and petroleum declared null and void and to permanently enjoin defendants from enforcing or attempting to enforce such provisions. Defendants filed motions to dismiss. The trial court denied defendants' motions and treated the owner's motion for an injunction as a motion for summary judgment as to the issues in the declaratory judgment action, which it granted. Defendants appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the constitutionality of §§ 1B4, 1B5 could not be justified by the county's power to regulate community aesthetics because such purpose was not mentioned as a legislative intent, and other types of service station advertisement were not regulated. Sections 1B5, 1B5 were unconstitutional because the size of the signs bore no reasonable or rational relationship to the deterrence of fraudulent practices. The owner also successfully demonstrated that he suffered substantial pecuniary loss from such provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the owner, which declared provisions of the Monroe County Local Laws restricting the maximum size of signs used to advertise the price of gasoline and petroleum products to be null and void, and which permanently enjoined defendants from enforcing or attempting to enforce such provisions. The court also held that the issues raised with regard to the show cause petition were moot.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of gasoline price signs -- injunctions.

1. Monroe County Local Law No. 2 of 1975, to extent it attempts to regulate the number and maximum size of all gasoline price signs that a gasoline dealer is permitted to have in view other than those signs required to be affixed to individual gasoline pumps by said local law, fails in its avowed legislative purpose and is unconstitutional. The constitutionality of the statute cannot be justified by the consideration of community aesthetics. Aside from the fact that the expressed legislative intent does not offer aesthetics as a reason for its enactment, the statute itself, by failing to regulate other types of service station advertisements, would be void on the ground of [***2] arbitrariness. Nor can the statute be justified as a deterrence of fraudulent practices, its stated legislative purpose, since restrictions on the size and number of such signs bear no reasonable or rational relationship to the deterrence of such practices.

2. As to whether Special Term should have granted the District Attorney's motion to dismiss petitioner's preliminary injunction proceeding on the ground such relief was not authorized by law or whether the petitioner should have sought a writ of prohibition in order to enjoin the criminal action pending against him for a violation of Local Law No. 2, the fact remains that the temporary restraining order has by its very nature expired and has been superseded by the permanent injunction entered in petitioner's declaratory judgment action. Consequently, the validity of the grant of this order, together with petitioner's right to seek such relief, is for all intents and purposes rendered moot.

 

1231 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Quintard Associates, Ltd. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 462; 394 N.Y.S.2d 960; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11824



May 27, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Determination unanimously confirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensed operator (operator) filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R 78, transferred by order from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Onondaga County (New York), to annul an order from respondent state liquor authority (authority), which suspended its license for 30 days for violating Rule 36 (1) (q) of the Rules of the State Liquor Authority, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 53.1(q).


OVERVIEW: The club operator asserted that there was insufficient evidence to support the authority's suspension of his license and the statute was unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the authority's findings, holding that the ruling was supported by substantial evidence because 25 citizens testified that the noise generated from the club prevented them from sleeping, that the noise came from the music, from vehicles entering and leaving the premises, and from boisterous patrons. This testimony supported the determination that the club operator conducted its operations in such a manner that property owners in the area were repeatedly and regularly deprived of the quiet enjoyment of their homes. The club operator asserted that the statute provided no guidance as to what conduct was proscribed, was unconstitutionally vague, and that suspension of his license for a period of 30 days was an abuse of discretion. The court held that club operator failed to carry his burden of proof that the statute was void for vagueness, and that in light of the on-going nature of the violation, it could not be said that the period of suspension was shocking to the conscience or an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the authority suspending the operator's liquor license for a 30 day period.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- licenses -- suspension -- noise or disturbance.

1. A rule of the State Liquor Authority ( 9 NYCRR 53.1 [q]), which provides that a license may be suspended when any noise or disturbance occurs in the licensed premises or the parking lot of said premises adversely affecting the health, welfare, safety or repose of the inhabitants of the area, is not unconstitutionally vague. It is not so drawn that men of common intelligence must necessarily guess at what conduct is prohibited. The words of the rule have acquired a content that conveys a sufficiently accurate concept of what is forbidden.

2. The rule is reasonably related to the broad goals of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law.

3. The rule is not directed toward the abridgement of free expression.

4. There was substantial evidence to support the determination of the State Liquor [***2] Authority suspending petitioner's license on the ground that the premises have been a source of noise or disturbance adversely affecting the health, welfare, safety or repose of the inhabitants of the area.

5. In the light of the on-going nature of the violation, it cannot be said that the 30-day suspension is shocking to the conscience or an abuse of discretion.

 

1232 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sroka v. Municipal Civil Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 1064; 395 N.Y.S.2d 854; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12376



May 27, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former patrolman appealed a judgment of Erie Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed his complaint against respondent, the Municipal Civil Service Commission of the City of Buffalo. The patrolman sought reinstatement and back pay after his conviction for conspiracy was reversed and the indictment dismissed.


OVERVIEW: When the patrolman was first convicted of conspiracy, the commissioner of police removed him from office pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 30(1)(e). The patrolman argued that conspiracy to commit bribery and grand larceny was not a crime involving a violation of his oath of office as a policeman. On appeal the court found that conspiracy to bribe was no less a violation of the oath of office than the acceptance or giving of the bribe itself and fell within the terms of the statute. Upon the patrolman's conviction his office was vacated immediately by operation of § 30(1)(e), not as a matter of punishment to him, but because the term of office was limited by statute and terminated upon the happening of the event. The subsequent reversal of the conviction and dismissal of the indictment may have removed the patrolman's disability to hold office but it could not entitle him to reinstatement with back pay. The patrolman's removal was not unconstitutional because the legislature could fix the terms of the office.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the patrolman's complaint.

 

1233 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ciampa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


57 A.D.2d 932; 394 N.Y.S.2d 727; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12190; 2 Media L. Rep. 2293



May 23, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment by the Supreme Court of Queens County (New York), which convicted him of obscenity in the second degree, upon a jury verdict.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of obscenity in the second degree, and a sentence was imposed. Defendant's appeal brought forth for review the denial of defendant's motion to suppress certain statements. The prosecution of the case arose out of the exhibition of a 65-minute color motion picture. On appeal, defendant raised the issue of the admissibility of certain statements he made to a police officer. Defendant argued that his statements should have been suppressed because when he asked to call his attorney, he clearly expressed a desire not to be interrogated. The court stated that the testimony clearly showed that defendant voluntarily and spontaneously spoke after the police responded to his question. Therefore, the three statements made by defendant to police were all properly admitted into evidence as voluntary, unsolicited, or spontaneous statements. The court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the supreme court, which convicted defendant of obscenity in the second degree. The court held that defendant's statements were admissible because the statements were given voluntarily and they were unsolicited.

 

1234 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Peninsula General Nursing Home v. Sugarman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


57 A.D.2d 268; 394 N.Y.S.2d 644; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10983



May 17, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County entered on September 5, 1975, affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner department of social services appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County (New York), which was in favor of respondent nursing home.


OVERVIEW: The nursing home provided care for the deceased. After Medicare was exhausted, the nursing home applied for Medicaid coverage on behalf of the decedent and the department of social services denied the application. The trial court declared N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 366 unconstitutional to the extent that it denied the nursing home a fair hearing, and ordered a hearing. The hearing was never held. On appeal, the department of social services claimed that the nursing home was not denied due process because it had an adequate remedy at law, via a plenary action against the deceased, while the nursing home claimed that it was entitled to reimbursement and to a fair hearing. The court held that the nursing home was entitled to a fair hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which declared § 366 unconstitutional and ordered a hearing.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Medicaid reimbursement -- right of provider of services  [***2] to fair hearing.

1. Petitioner voluntary not-for-profit nursing home has standing to challenge the constitutionality of section 366 of the Social Services Law and the regulations thereunder insofar as petitioner is not provided with the opportunity for a fair hearing to challenge an administrative determination denying eligibility for medical assistance to a patient, since an impenetrable barrier would otherwise be erected to any judicial scrutiny of the legislation.

2. A facility providing services to an indigent who does not challenge a determination denying eligibility should have the opportunity to review the determination, since the fundamental purpose of the Medicaid law is to assist needy persons through the co-operative efforts of the hospital, patient and social services department, and payment is made directly to the provider of medical services. Petitioner nursing home was deprived of due process by denial of the right to a fair hearing when a fair hearing request by the family of the patient was withdrawn at his death, since the property right to reimbursement for services already provided is in question and a person aggrieved has a constitutional right to a hearing.

 [***3] 3. Petitioner should not be relegated to a plenary suit for breach of contract against the patient for services rendered. Such a suit would be contrary to the legislative scheme and would require a patient who is unable to pay for his medical care to defend a lawsuit for those payments, when a fair hearing might secure reversal of the adverse administrative adjudication. While the patient has standing, he might fail to pursue the matter out of intention, lack of knowledge and, if he is dead, so might his family. The provider of services has a more direct interest, is more likely than the patient to possess the specialized knowledge of legal requirements and has the resources to challenge an adjudication of ineligibility.

4. Accordingly, section 366 of the Social Services Law and the regulations thereunder unconstitutionally deprive petitioner of a fair hearing and Special Term properly directed that such hearing be held.

 

1235 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Peasley v. Reid


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


57 A.D.2d 998; 394 N.Y.S.2d 471; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12289



May 12, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners filed a complaint against defendants, New York Commissioner of Environmental Conservation and New York Attorney General, for declaratory and directory relief annulling the State's appropriation of their real property. The landowners sought review of the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Hamilton County (New York), which granted defendants' motion for summary judgment and dismissed the landowners' complaint.


OVERVIEW: The landowners owned property in a forest preserve. The Commissioner appropriated the property under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 3-0305. The landowners claimed that an inaccurate certification of title by the Attorney General rendered the appropriation invalid. The court modified the trial court's judgment. The court held that the Attorney General certified that the title to the property was in the landowners, that the landowners were notified of the appropriation, that the only inaccuracy in the certification was that the landowners' interest was stated incorrectly, that the mistake was not sufficient to invalidate the certification, and that the landowners were not prejudiced. The court held, however, that the action was for a declaratory judgment, the trial court erred in dismissing the complaint, and that the judgment was required to contain a provision declaring that the State's appropriation of the property was not invalid by reason of the inadequate certification of title.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment dismissing the landowners' complaint. The court struck the portion of the judgment dismissing the complaint and substituted a provision declaring that the State's appropriation of the landowners' property was not invalid by reason of the inadequate certification of title by the Attorney General.

 

1236 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. McNeil v. New York State Board of Parole


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


57 A.D.2d 876; 394 N.Y.S.2d 230; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12104



May 9, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parole board filed for a writ of habeas corpus, challenging the decision of the Supreme Court, Dutchess County (New York), which held that N.Y. Correct. Law § 803(5) was unconstitutional as violative of the Equal Protection Clause.


OVERVIEW: The state refused to permit defendant parole violators to earn good time credits against the remainder of their terms. Defendants argued that N.Y. Correct. Law § 803(5) discriminated, for purposes of good time eligibility, between those parole or conditional release violators who had more than one year left to serve and those who had less. They maintained that the distinction was wholly arbitrary and did not rationally serve any legitimate state policy. On review, the court disagreed and reversed. The court found that at stake was not the individual's fundamental right to liberty, but rather that the giving of good time credits to an inmate was a matter of legislative grace. The court determined that prisoners had no constitutional right to release prior to the maximum expiration date of their sentences. The statutory provision in question did not work a denial of equal protection, the court concluded, because a rational basis existed for its enactment.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding the corrections law unconstitutional was reversed.

 

1237 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Dyson v. Miles Laboratories, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


57 A.D.2d 197; 394 N.Y.S.2d 86; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10948



May 5, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant manufacturer sought review of a decision by the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss four causes of action for misbranding brought by plaintiff Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of the State of New York.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturer sold and marketed four food products that were labeled as breakfast links, patties, strips, and slices. The Commissioner sought to enjoin the distribution, marketing, selling, and advertising of the products, contending that each product was an imitation under N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 201(3) and misbranded. The manufacturer sought to dismiss the Commissioner's claims on the ground that § 201(3) was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite. The court held that based on federal case law interpreting essentially identical federal statutes, § 201(3) was constitutionally sound. The court found that a federal regulation establishing specific standards of nutritional inferiority was not a required element of an imitation under New York law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Food -- misbranding of food -- imitation food.

1. Section 201 (subd 3) of the Agriculture and Markets Law, providing that food shall be deemed misbranded if it is an "imitation of another food", unless its label bears the word "imitation" and the name of the food imitated, followed by a statement showing the constituents thereof, is not unconstitutionally vague and indefinite. Under Federal decisions dealing with essentially identical Federal statutory provisions, the ordinary understanding of "imitation" is an adequate statutory definition. Nor does the fact that there are Federal regulations establishing specific standards of nutritional inferiority as a test of whether one product is an imitation of another, while there are no such standards under the State statute, render it invalid, since, although the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets has discretion to adopt Federal regulations, he is not required to do so.

 [***2] 2. In an action by the commissioner to enjoin the distribution, marketing, sale and advertising of food products labeled as breakfast links, breakfast patties, breakfast strips and breakfast slices, and intended as substitutes for sausage, bacon and the like, on the ground, alleged in four causes of action, that those products are imitations and misbranded, a motion by defendant to dismiss those causes of action was properly denied. Whether a product is imitative of another depends upon the totality of the various factors present in a given case, and the resolution of this question must await the presentation of proof.

 

1238 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Paul "X"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


57 A.D.2d 216; 393 N.Y.S.2d 1005; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10954



May 5, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law and the facts, without costs, and matter remitted to Ulster County Family Court for expedited proceedings in accordance herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the mother of an allegedly neglected child, and the child, appealed an order of the Family Court, Ulster County (New York), which adjudged the child neglected and placed the child in the care and custody of the Ulster County Commissioner of Social Services for a period of 18 months.


OVERVIEW: A neglect petition was filed when appellant's child was a few months old, and she was granted an adjournment in contemplation of dismissal pursuant to N.Y. Family Ct. Act §1039. As a condition, appellant was to cooperate with the appropriate departments and avail herself of mental health counseling, parental care, and public health nurse instructions. The Department of Social Services charged that appellant failed to cooperate. Although the family court found that there was no evidence of either neglect or abuse, malnutrition or illness, at the dispositional hearing the child was placed in the custody of the of Social Services for 18 months because of appellant's serious lack of cooperation. Both appellant and the child sought review, alleging that the removal of the child was unwarranted. The court found that not only was there an absence of findings as to the best interests of the child, but there were no findings and no evidence of neglect, abuse, malnutrition, illness, or the like. The court reversed the order because of the denial of due process to appellant, the natural parent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the family court adjudging the child neglected and placing him in custody because there were no findings or evidence of neglect, abuse, malnutrition, illness, abandonment, surrender, unfitness or the like.


HEADNOTES

Parent and child -- termination of custody -- neglected child.

1. Although appellant mother in a child neglect proceeding was granted a six-month adjournment in contemplation of dismissal (ACD) (Family Ct Act, § 1039) on the condition that she co-operate with the various supportive services offered to her, when the matter reappeared on the Family Court calendar, it was found that appellant had failed to substantially observe the terms and conditions of the ACD and, as required by subdivision (e) of section 1039, the Family Court found that circumstances of neglect were deemed to exist and proceeded to a dispositional hearing. Even though the Family Court found at the dispositional hearing that there was no evidence of either neglect [***2] or abuse, malnutrition or illness, the child was removed from the custody of the appellant and placed in the custody of the Commissioner of Social Services. Mere proof of violation of an ACD order cannot by itself support a deprivation of custody. Under the circumstances constitutional due process requirements have not been met, since not only is there an absence of findings of the best interests of the child, but there has also been no actual judicial finding of neglect or abuse. Moreover, by consenting to the entry of the ACD order, appellant did not waive the need for proof and findings of neglect and/or abuse. Accordingly, the order must be reversed and the matter restored to the Family Court calendar for further hearings at which proof of facts constituting neglect, abuse or the like may be presented and findings made as to appellant's present capability to care for her child and the best interests of the child under present circumstances.

2. Since notice of the challenge to the constitutionality of section 1039 was never given to the Attorney-General as required by section 71 of the Executive Law, the court is precluded from entertaining that question.

 

1239 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Davin v. New York State Board of Regents


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


57 A.D.2d 690; 393 N.Y.S.2d 832; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11724



April 28, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an action pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6510(4) to annul, modify, and vacate an order from the Commissioner of Education (New York), which revoked the nurse's license to practice nursing for fraud and deceit, being addicted to the use of morphine, cocaine or other drugs, unprofessional conduct, and having been habitually drunk in violation of several subsections of N.Y. Educ. Law § 6509.


OVERVIEW: The charges against the nurse arose from the nurse's alleged unlawful diversion for her own use of certain controlled substances and prescription drugs from hospitals in which she was employed, the alleged addiction to specific drugs, and her habitual drinking that led to her treatment at a clinic for chronic alcohol addiction. The nurse claimed that N.Y. Educ. Law § 6509 was unconstitutionally vague in that inadequately defined the proscribed conduct with which the nurse was charged. On review the court held that the statute in each instance sufficiently apprised a member of the profession of the scope of permissible conduct. The court noted that its review was restricted to a consideration of whether the decision of the agency was supported by substantial evidence and was not arbitrary or capricious. That same limited review applied to proceedings to review the weight of evidence in disciplinary matters that involved professional licenses. The court found that there was substantial evidence to support the determination that the nurse was addicted to drugs and the revocation of her license was not so disproportionate to the offense as to be shocking to one's sense of fairness.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the commissioner of education's determination that revoked the nurse's license.

 

1240 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Phillips v. Oriskany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 110; 394 N.Y.S.2d 941; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10932



April 15, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously reversed without costs, defendant's motion granted, and complaint dismissed. Appeal from an order denying a motion for reargument dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant village appealed an order from the Supreme Court, Oneida County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss the complaint of plaintiffs, former owner and current owner of business, for failure to state a cause of action, and granted plaintiffs' cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant village appealed the trial court's order, which denied its motion to dismiss the complaint of plaintiffs, former owner and current owner of business, for failure to state a cause of action, and granted plaintiffs' cross-motion for summary judgment. The trial court's order was reversed and plaintiffs' complaint was dismissed. Defendant's motion for reargument was dismissed because an order denying a motion for reargument was not appealable. The court held that the trial court erred in declaring any part of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 25 unconstitutional. The court held that an ordinance could forbid a change of use. Thus, a non-conforming use of land could not be changed to another non-conforming use, and the change from a restaurant and soda fountain business in 1965 to a tavern in which liquor was dispensed was clearly a change of use. The court held that it did not have jurisdiction to rule upon the validity and extent of the variance because the zoning board of appeals was not made a party to the action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and dismissed the complaint of plaintiffs, former owner and current owner of business, because the trial court erred in declaring any part of the state constitution unconstitutional and a non-conforming use of land could not be changed to another non-conforming use.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- nonconforming use -- variance.

1. Plaintiff Phillips, who was using his property for a restaurant and soda fountain operation at the time defendant village enacted its 1962 zoning ordinance, which placed plaintiff's property in an R-2 District, obtained a liquor license in 1965 and converted the use of his property to that of a restaurant and tavern. In settlement of an action brought in 1969 by defendant against plaintiff to enjoin him from continuing the operation of the tavern business, defendant's zoning board of appeals granted a variance to plaintiff for such use, but "only so long as said premises [***2] shall be operated as a restaurant and/or tavern by the applicant". In 1974 plaintiff Phillips entered into a contract to sell the property and business to plaintiff Burth on condition that the variance to operate the tavern would be extended to the buyer. However, the buyer's application to the zoning board of appeals for an extension of the variance was denied. Insofar as Special Term granted judgment determining that the 1974 determination by the zoning board of appeals denying an extension of the variance was void and insofar as it declared that the restriction in the 1969 variance should have no legal significance, the judgment is a nullity, since the zoning board of appeals was not made a party to this action and the court was without jurisdiction to make such an adjudication. Moreover, such review of a variance may only be had by a CPLR article 78 proceeding and, with respect to the 1974 determination denying the variance extension, must be brought within 30 days (Village Law, § 7-712, subd 3). That same statute also bars an action for declaratory judgment for such review.

2. The provision of section 25 of article IX of the zoning ordinance upon which Special Term relied [***3] to find the section unconstitutional, to the effect that "All nonconforming uses of land shall be discontinued within two (2) years after the effective date of this ordinance as amended", was not invoked by the defendant or the zoning board of appeals in this case and therefore there was no occasion for the court to rule on its validity. However, that part of section 25 of article IX of the ordinance which provides that a nonconforming use of land shall not be changed to another nonconforming use, is relevant. Nevertheless, since the 1965 change to a tavern was clearly a change of use which violated the 1962 ordinance, and since an ordinance may forbid such a change of use, the court erred in declaring any part of section 25 of article IX of the ordinance unconstitutional.

3. The failure to join the zoning board of appeals as a party to this action deprived Special Term of jurisdiction to rule upon the validity and extent of the variance and to grant judgment declaring that the changed nonconforming use is transferable, assuming that the order may be interpreted as granting such relief. Had the board been made a party, it might have asserted that the variance was personal to plaintiff [***4] Phillips and if plaintiff Phillips denied that he so understood it, the board might have claimed that the variance was granted under mutual mistake of fact and demanded its rescission. If rescission were granted, there would have been no 1969 limited variance in effect and the change of use to tavern in 1965 would constitute sufficient ground for denying the requested variance.

4. The appeal from the order denying the motion for reargument should be dismissed. Said order is not appealable. In any event, since such a motion is made on the papers submitted on the original motion and new facts may not be presented, additional facts contained in the papers in support of and in opposition to the motion for reargument may not be considered on this appeal.

People ex rel. Moore v. Henderson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 726; 395 N.Y.S.2d 559; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11780

1242 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Marcus Associates, Inc. v. Huntington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


57 A.D.2d 116; 393 N.Y.S.2d 727; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10933



April 11, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, entered January 12, 1976, modified, on the law, by deleting the second decretal paragraph thereof and substituting therefor a provision declaring that plaintiff has not met its burden of proving the unconstitutionality of subdivision 10 of section 62-6.1 of the Zoning Ordinance of the Town of Huntington. As so modified, judgment affirmed, with one bill of costs payable by plaintiff to defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lot owners brought an action against respondent city, challenging the constitutionality of the city's ordinance regarding the zoning of industrial property. The Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York) found for the city. The lot owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The lot owner purchased lots within the city. The lots were zoned residential at the time of purchase. The area was rezoned light industrial. The city ordinance was then amended to limit industrial buildings to no more than three uses per building and by not more than three occupants, Town of Huntington, N.Y., Ordinances § 62-6.1, subd. 10. The court held that the lot owners carried the burden of proving beyond a reasonable doubt no legitimate basis for the ordinance. But the lot owners failed to show no reasonable basis for the ordinance or that the ordinance was contrary to the city's master plan. The city's expert testified that the ordinance served a legitimate governmental purpose and that limited user tenancy was necessary to have maintained the area's character as a predominantly single-tenanted, large floor area. As to whether the ordinance would have achieved its purported goal of reducing density, the court reviewed the ordinance's constitutionality, not its wisdom or practicality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's ruling that the ordinance was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- limitations on industrial uses.

1. The owner of property located in a light industry district of a town has failed to meet its burden of establishing that no reasonable basis exists to sustain the constitutionality of an amendment to the town code restricting the use of a building or premises in the district to not more than three permitted uses, by not more than three occupants, with each separate use to occupy no less than 20,000 square feet of building gross floor area. On the rather eminent expert testimony [***2] presented by the town, legitimate governmental purposes were sought to be achieved by the ordinance, and thus there exists the requisite foundation upon which constitutionality can rest. As to testimony on behalf of plaintiff, consisting largely of projections as to whether the ordinance would result in lesser density, whether or not an ordinance may achieve one of its purposes is not determinative of constitutionality.

2. The ordinance is not ultra vires. It promotes the general welfare, for it is reasonably related to the preservation of the character of the area, one of the most attractive industrial areas on Long Island. It is not contrary to the town's master plan, nor does it preclude the use of plaintiff's property for a purpose to which it is readily adaptable.

 

1243 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Dur-Bar Realty Co. v. Utica


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 51; 394 N.Y.S.2d 913; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10477



April 7, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court, Oneida County (New York), which dismissed the landowner's action for declaratory judgment that the "Land Conservation District" provision of the zoning ordinance of respondent city, Utica, N.Y., Ordinance 39 (1967), was unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: The landowner purchased property and made applications for special use permits, which were denied. The landowner filed a declaratory judgment that the "Land Conservation District" provision of the city's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional and void. The trial court dismissed the action. On appeal, the court affirmed. The ordinance was comprehensive and contained detailed use provisions and a carefully drawn map that evinced orderly and painstaking forethought. In addition, the ordinance contained a recitation that it was based on the master plan for the city. The subject parcel was in a flood plain and, therefore, presented unique use control problems. Thus, the fact that the ordinance failed to allow any uses as of right did not demonstrate lack of a comprehensive plan. The provisions did not represent an impermissible delegation of legislative authority to the board of appeals. There were adequate standards to guide the board in granting the special permits. The "Land Conversation District" provisions bore a substantial relation to legitimate governmental purposes and a reasonable relationship to the goal of flood safety.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which dismissed the landowner's action for a declaratory judgment that the "Land Conservation District" provision of the city's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional and void.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- land conservation district -- flood plain.

1. The zoning ordinance of the City of Utica does not provide for any permitted principal or accessory uses in a "Land Conservation District" in which plaintiff's property is located but allows only certain special uses requiring a permit. The ordinance is comprehensive, containing detailed use provisions and a map which evince orderly and painstaking forethought, and is based on a master plan. The "Land Conservation District" lies in a flood plain and, therefore, presents unique use control problems. The district is a product of the assessment of the character of the land in the light of the public health and safety being protected from flooding resulting from building in an area unsuitable for intensive development. Accordingly, the district provisions are "in accord with a well considered plan" (General City Law, § 20, subd 25) and are not ultra vires.

2. The "Land Conservation [***2] District" provisions do not represent an impermissible delegation of legislative authority to the board of appeals. The ordinance sets forth adequate standards to guide the board in granting special permits. It is insignificant that the ordinance does not permit any use as of right in the district.

3. The "Land Conservation District" provisions bear a substantial relation to a legitimate governmental purpose and a reasonable relation to the goal of flood safety. It has not been shown that the special uses authorized for the district are so restrictive as to destroy the economic utility of plaintiff's property. Accordingly, the ordinance does not violate due process and is constitutional on its face.

4. The failure to order a trial of the questions of fact raised by the attack on the constitutionality of the ordinance as applied to plaintiff's property was not error since that question was not before the court and the judgment of dismissal did not affect the portion of the complaint which raised that issue.

5. Since there has been no final determination by the State Department of Environmental Conservation that plaintiff's property cannot be filled, the complaint is premature [***3] insofar as it attacks the ordinance as applied, on the ground that the denial of the right to fill the property violates due process. Plaintiff has not exhausted his administrative remedies.

 

1244 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Conrad v. Beneficial Finance Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 91; 394 N.Y.S.2d 923; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10484



April 7, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment unanimously modified on the law and facts in accordance with opinion by Dillon, J., and as modified affirmed with costs to plaintiffs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs and defendant sought review of the order issued by the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), that granted plaintiffs partial summary judgment in their action against defendant financing company for its failure to make requisite disclosures in loan documents.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff husband and wife sued defendant financing company for its failure to comply with the Truth in Lending Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1601 et seq., in connection with a loan to plaintiffs. Plaintiffs claimed the loan's disclosure statement was unclear and confusing. Plaintiffs and defendant filed cross motions for summary judgment. Partial summary judgment was granted to plaintiffs. On appeal, the court affirmed summary judgment in plaintiff's favor as to a single claim. Defendant's disclosure statement was defective because it grouped together certain terms which were neither arithmetically nor logically related, and it failed to present related terms in a sensible fashion. As a result, plaintiffs' loan was void and as a couple, not individually, plaintiffs were entitled to the maximum amount permitted as a statutory penalty.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment for plaintiff affirmed in part, reversed in part and modified. Plaintiff husband and wife were entitled to the maximum statutory penalty as a couple, not individually, for defendant's failure to provide an unambiguous disclosure statement to plaintiffs regarding the terms of a loan.


HEADNOTES

Consumer protection -- loan agreement disclosure statement -- truth in lending.

1. Plaintiffs entered into a loan obligation with defendant consumer finance company. Thereafter, they brought suit against the defendant, claiming that the disclosure statement furnished them by defendant failed to comply with the Federal Truth in Lending Act (US Code, tit 15, § 1601 et seq.) and the regulations promulgated thereunder (Regulation Z, 12 CFR 226.1 et seq.) [***2] . In their first cause of action plaintiffs allege that the absence of an acceleration clause in the disclosure statement violated section 1639 (subd [a], par [7]) of the act and section 226.8 (b) (4) of the regulations. Both sections require disclosure of default, delinquency or similar charges payable in the event of late payments. To the extent that this loan contract prescribes the same rebate method for refunding unearned precomputed interest upon acceleration as upon prepayment, the acceleration is not a "charge" requiring disclosure. However, since plaintiffs argue that disclosure is required because the formula for rebating insurance premiums upon acceleration results in a greater amount retained by the creditor than upon prepayment, the question arises as to whether defendant complied with section 1605 (subd [b]) of title 15 of the United States Code and 12 CFR 226.4 (a) (5) (ii), which provide that insurance charges shall be included in finance charges unless the debtor gives specific written indication of his desire for insurance coverage. Whether such an indication was given here is specifically at issue in plaintiffs' second cause of action which cannot now be [***3] resolved since the issue has not been directly addressed by the parties. Accordingly, plaintiffs are not entitled to summary judgment on their first cause of action.

2. Defendant's statement of disclosure fails to set forth the items which must be disclosed clearly and conspicuously and in meaningful sequence. The disclosure statement groups together certain items which are neither arithmetically nor logically related, and fails to present sensibly those terms which do bear such relationships. Accordingly, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment on their third cause of action alleging that the disclosures were not made in a meaningful manner in violation of subdivision (a) of section 1631 of the Federal Truth in Lending Act and section 226.6 (a) of the regulations promulgated thereunder should have been granted and they should be awarded a statutory penalty of $ 1,000 together with reasonable counsel fees. Plaintiffs, husband and wife who entered into this single credit transaction, are not each entitled to recover a $ 1,000 statutory penalty.

3. Plaintiffs are not entitled to summary judgment on their request for a refund of their payments on this loan pursuant to section 358 [***4] of the Banking Law, which provides that any loan made in violation of section 353 of the Banking Law constitutes a misdemeanor and shall be void and that the lender shall have no right to collect or receive any principal, interest or charges. Section 353 directs a lender to comply with the provisions of the Federal Truth in Lending Act and the civil penalties provided in section 358 apply to violations of that act. Although section 358 operates to render this loan void, plaintiffs are not thereby vested with a right to recover payments of principal and interest voluntarily made upon a loan otherwise valid except for defective disclosure. Since plaintiffs assert that by stipulation with the defendant several payments were made under a reservation of rights and without prejudice, their claim may well bear on the issue of voluntariness and their fourth cause of action, to the extent of such payments, remains viable, but so much of that cause of action as seeks recovery of payments voluntarily made upon the loan should be dismissed.

 

1245 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Frederico v. Moore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


57 A.D.2d 704; 395 N.Y.S.2d 535; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11749



April 7, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent town board appealed from a judgment of the Monroe Supreme Court (New York) that determined that Riga, N.Y., Dump Ordinance §§ 10, 10-A were unconstitutional and directed that the town board issue a permit to petitioner landowner to fill and grade his property.


OVERVIEW: The landowner applied for a permit to fill and grade his unimproved property. The town board denied the landowner's application, and the landowner sought review. The trial court determined that Riga, N.Y., Dump Ordinance §§ 10, 10-A were unconstitutional and directed the town board to issue a permit to the landowner. On appeal, the court modified and affirmed. The court held that the permit should have been issued because the dump ordinance merely required the filing of a proper application and contained no standard to determine whether a permit should be issued. The court recognized the town board's authority to deny a permit despite the absence of standards where the town board reserved the power to issue or withhold permits; however, there was no such reservation present in the dump ordinance. Thus, because the landowner filed the proper application, the permit should have been issued. Additionally, Riga, N.Y., Dump Ordinance § 10 precluded the unconditional denial of a fill permit. Because the dump ordinance was found to be constitutional, the trial court should not have considered the landowner's constitutional argument.


OUTCOME: The court modified the portion of the order that found the ordinance unconstitutional and affirmed.
