People ex rel. Melendez v. New York State Parole Board


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


53 A.D.2d 1004; 386 N.Y.S.2d 117; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 15801



July 29, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner sought review of a judgment entered by the Supreme Court at Special Term, in Ulster County (New York), which found that respondent, the New York State Parole Board, had complied with the New York Correction Law when it denied the prisoner parole and dismissed his petition.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was sentenced to an indeterminate term of one year to life after he pled guilty to the criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree. The Board denied the prisoner parole after it conducted a hearing to determine whether he should be paroled after one year. The prisoner sought judicial review of the Board's decision. The trial court found that the Board had complied with the provisions governing parole proceedings and dismissed the matter. The prisoner sought review of that judgment, which was affirmed. Under N.Y. Correct. Law § 212(10), the Board's actions were not reviewable when done in accordance with the law. Therefore, the prisoner was not entitled to relief because the Board had fully complied with the statutory requirements. The court stated that the Board was not required by the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution to disclose its release criteria. Furthermore, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 7, § 1910.15 adequately advised prisoners of the principal criteria for parole. Finally, the prisoner had failed to preserve the issue of whether a Board member had improperly acted as his interpreter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which had dismissed the prisoner's action seeking review of the Board's decision to deny him parole. No costs were awarded on appeal.

 

1295 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Boyle v. Kelley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


53 A.D.2d 457; 385 N.Y.S.2d 791; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13076



July 12, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, entered January 22, 1976, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant police commissioner sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which granted the motion for summary judgment of plaintiff administrator in the administrator's action to recover moneys allegedly wrongfully retained by the police commissioner, denied the police commissioner's cross motion to dismiss the complaint, and directed entry of a money judgment in favor of the administrator.


OVERVIEW: The administrator's decedent was charged with gambling offenses; a search warrant was executed and currency was taken from his apartment. The charges were dismissed, but the police commissioner retained the seized money. The administrator filed a complaint seeking return of the money. The trial court granted the administrator's motion for summary judgment and denied the police commissioner's cross motion for dismissal of the complaint. The police commissioner contended that the complaint was defective because it failed to allege that a notice of claim prescribed by N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e and N.Y. County Law § 52 had been served upon him. The administrator contended that service of such a notice was not required. The court affirmed the trial court's order. The court reasoned that the complaint could be viewed as one for money had and received or for unjust enrichment; thus, the cause of action was equitable in nature. Where an action was grounded upon equitable principles, compliance with the notice of claim statutes was not needed. A fair sense of justice required that the police commissioner not be permitted to withhold money that rightfully belonged to the administrator.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which granted the administrator's motion for summary judgment and denied the police commissioner's cross motion for dismissal of the administrator's complaint in the administrator's action to recover moneys allegedly wrongfully retained by the police commissioner.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- notice of claim -- return of seized evidence -- notice of claim requirement of section 50-e of General Municipal Law is inapplicable to plaintiff administrator's claim for moneys wrongfully retained by defendant Police Commissioner after dismissal of criminal action in which it was seized pursuant to invalid search warrant, since requirement is inapplicable to equitable claim; in any event, money should be returned as matter of fundamental fairness; accordingly, summary judgment was properly granted to plaintiff on ground that there was no defense to cause of action.

The 90-day notice of claim requirement of section 50-e of the General Municipal Law is inapplicable to the plaintiff administrator's claim [***2] for moneys wrongfully retained by the defendant Police Commissioner after dismissal of a criminal action in which it was seized pursuant to an invalid search warrant. The plaintiff's cause of action is equitable and, accordingly, there need be no compliance with the notice of claim requirement. In any event, the money should be returned as a matter of fundamental fairness, since the government may not confiscate property without due process of law. Accordingly, Special Term properly granted summary judgment to plaintiff on the ground that there was no defense to the cause of action.

 

1296 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


860 Executive Towers, Inc. v. Board of Assessors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


53 A.D.2d 463; 385 N.Y.S.2d 604; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13077



July 12, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from an interlocutory judgment of the Supreme Court in Nassau County (New York) determining applicable tax ratios, and a money judgment for expenses incurred in making proof of ratio, and appeal from an order granting petitioners' motion for partial summary judgment, fixing tax ratios based on the interlocutory judgment.


OVERVIEW: This group of consolidated appeals encompassed 20 separate certiorari proceedings, covering the years 1966 through 1973, which were consolidated for purposes of a joint trial on the issue of ratio. The respondents, petitioning taxpayers below, challenged their property tax assessments on grounds of inequality claiming that the assessments of the subject properties had been made at a higher proportionate valuation or ratio than the assessments of other real property on the same tax roll. The court here found that the appellant county did not satisfy its burden of showing that the state equalization rate's statistical methodology was not truly sound, fair, representative and, in general, designed to produce an accurate result. The interlocutory judgment that rejected the appellant's challenge to the use of the state equalization rates was affirmed. The court upheld the award to respondents, the petitioning taxpayers below, of reasonable costs and expenses in making proof of ratio.


OUTCOME: The interlocutory judgment which rejected the appellant's challenge to the use of the state equalization rates, in determining the applicable ratios for the tax years under review was affirmed and respondents were awarded reasonable expenses incurred in making their proof since they proved the correct ratios.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- equalization rate -- in tax certiorari proceedings respondent Board of Assessors of Nassau County is foreclosed from challenging methodology and underlying data employed by State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) in  [***2] promulgating State equalization rate; said rate is "objectively" and "expertly" arrived at and may be utilized as sole basis for determining ratio of assessed value to full value (see Guth Realty v Gingold, 34 NY2d 440; Real Property Tax Law, § 720, subd 3); taxing authority may challenge appropriateness of State rate but not methodology of SBEA; petitioners, challenging real property assessments on grounds of inequality, satisfied their burden for having State rate applied by making prima facie showing that collected data going into rate formulation specifically pertained to taxing unit -- petitioners were properly awarded reasonable costs and expenses in making proof of ratio (Real Property Tax Law, § 716, subd 2) -- county is collaterally estopped from challenging SBEA methodology in pending certiorari proceedings.

1. In tax certiorari proceedings, respondent Board of Assessors of the County of Nassau is foreclosed from challenging the methodology and underlying data employed by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) in promulgating the State equalization rate under the holding in Guth Realty v Gingold (34 NY2d 440), which, upon an analysis of the 1969 amendment [***3] to subdivision 3 of section 720 of the Real Property Tax Law, and a finding that the State equalization rate is "objectively" and "expertly" arrived at, determined that the State rate may be utilized as the sole basis for determining the ratio of assessed value to full value for the years 1970 forward. While the taxing authority may, under Guth, show that "the equalization ratio is inappropriate to the taxing unit, to the category of property involved and to the particular property or any other valid reason which would affect its relevancy or weight", it may not challenge the methodology of the SBEA. Proof on the methodology of the SBEA, although almost entirely inadmissible under Guth, nevertheless, did not establish that the State rate was other than "objectively" and "expertly" arrived at. Petitioners, which were challenging their real property assessments on grounds of inequality, satisfied their limited burden of having the State rate used as the sole determinant of ratio by producing a witness with a mathematical and statistical background, who testified to the general methodology employed in making up the equalization rate of the county after interviews with SBEA [***4] officials and examination of supporting data and documents.

2. Special Term properly denied the county pretrial discovery of all of the raw data underlying some 2,000 appraisals and 6,000 sales by use of a subpoena duces tecum. The county was granted a list of all appraisals and sales actually used in the pertinent surveys from which it could have discovered any serious discrepancies.

3. The erroneous refusal by the SBEA to grant the county formal standing to represent the towns in hearings before the SBEA on tentative equalization rates for those towns did not render the State rates inadmissible per se since the State rate, if not stipulated to, cannot be applied without a prima facie showing that the collected data going into the rate formulation specifically pertained to the taxing unit in issue. In addition, the county had standing to challenge the final State rates by way of an article 78 proceeding pursuant to section 760 of the Real Property Tax Law. Future challenges of individual assessing units suffering unique prejudice because of some peculiarity of the methodology of the SBEA should be made in the statutorily provided hearings before the SBEA and not in the courts [***5] upon a certiorari proceeding.

4. Petitioners were properly awarded their reasonable costs and expenses in making proof of ratio pursuant to subdivision 2 of section 716 of the Real Property Tax Law.

5. The county is collaterally estopped from challenging the SBEA methodology in pending certiorari proceedings.

1298 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brenes


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


53 A.D.2d 78; 385 N.Y.S.2d 530; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12483



July 6, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, Bronx County, rendered on June 5, 1975, and orders of said court entered on December 27, 1973 and March 12, 1975, respectively reversed, on the law, the motions to suppress evidence granted, and the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which convicted defendant, on his plea of guilty, of attempted possession of a dangerous drug in the first degree, and two orders of that court, which denied motions by defendant to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant individual was the target of a narcotics investigation and was under police surveillance. An eavesdropping warrant was obtained. A search warrant was obtained and executed about three weeks later. Narcotics as well as cutting and packaging paraphernalia were seized. Defendant was arrested and allegedly made certain inculpatory statements. Defendant filed a motion to suppress the seized evidence and the admissions. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant pleaded guilty to attempted possession of a dangerous drug in the first degree and was sentenced to an indeterminate term prison term. Execution of the sentence was stayed pending defendant's appeal. The court reversed the judgment and the orders denying the suppression of evidence, granted the motion to suppress, and dismissed the indictment, as the eavesdropping warrant was improperly issued because the police failed to prove, as required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 700.15 subdiv. 4, that normal investigative procedures were exhausted before attempting electronic surveillance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of conviction and both its orders, granted defendant's motions to suppress evidence, and dismissed the indictment because it was evident that normal investigative procedures could have been successful and use of wiretap was merely a useful additional tool, so it never should have been authorized.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping warrant -- total disregard of minimization standard in statute by recording every conversation over defendant's telephone, including those which were nonpertinent, violated defendant's constitutional rights; accordingly, judgment convicting defendant of attempted possession of dangerous drug, first degree, and orders denying motions to suppress are reversed, on law, and indictment dismissed.

After information had been received from informants, defendant became the target of a narcotics investigation. An eavesdropping warrant was obtained and [***2] three weeks later during the execution of a search warrant, narcotics and related paraphernalia were seized. The judgment convicting defendant, on his plea of guilty, to attempted possession of a dangerous drug in the first degree and orders denying motions to suppress should be reversed, on the law, and the indictment dismissed. Assuming that the eavesdropping warrant was lawfully issued, the suppression motions should have been granted because of the total disregard of the minimization standard specified by the statute ( CPL 700.30, subd 7). The taping machines recorded every conversation over the defendant's telephone, including those concededly nonpertinent. By ignoring their statutory responsibility, the monitoring officers violated defendant's constitutional rights.

Moritt v. Governor of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


53 A.D.2d 857; 385 N.Y.S.2d 584; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13687

1300 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burden

Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department

1301 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Conrad v. Home & Auto Loan Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


53 A.D.2d 48; 385 N.Y.S.2d 979; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12479



July 2, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously modified in accordance with opinion by Dillon, J., and as modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff borrowers appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court in Monroe County (New York), which denied a motion by the borrowers for summary judgment and dismissed the portion of the borrowers' complaint against defendant lender seeking recovery of payments made. In their action, the borrower sought a declaration that a loan was void under N.Y. Banking Law § 358.


OVERVIEW: The borrowers procured a loan from the lender, payable in monthly installments over a three-year period. When the borrowers had six payments remaining to make on the loan, they filed an action against the lender seeking a declaration that a loan was void under N.Y. Banking Law § 358. The borrowers sought to recover all payments made upon the loan. The lender conceded that it failed to furnish items required to be disclosed under the Truth in Lending Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1601 et seq., as incorporated in N.Y. Banking Law § 353. The lender contended that the action was barred by the one-year statute of limitations provided by 15 U.S.C.S. § 1640(e). The trial court dismissed the borrowers' cause of action to recover payments. On appeal, the court modified the judgment, holding that the borrowers' action under N.Y. Banking Law § 358 was subject to a three-year statute of limitations provided by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214(2). The court held that the borrowers' claim for payments of principal and interest voluntarily made upon the loan was without basis in law. The payments made by the borrowers after instituting an action were recoverable if the loan was void under N.Y. Banking Law § 358.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by reinstating the part of the borrowers' action seeking recovery of the final six loan payments, which were made after the borrowers filed the complaint. The court affirmed the judgment as modified.


HEADNOTES

Banks and banking -- licensed lenders -- truth in lending -- action to declare loan from licensed lender void under section 358 of Banking Law is subject to three-year Statute of Limitations; section 358 is State statute pre-existing State and Federal Truth in Lending Acts; one-year Statute of Limitations in Federal act applicable to actions to recover civil penalties does not govern present action -- plaintiffs do not have right to recover principal and interest payments voluntarily made since such action was not recognized at common law and section 358 establishes no such remedy -- claim for payments made after institution of action and under reservation of rights may bear on issue of voluntariness and to that extent first cause of action is reinstated -- failure to incorporate into section  [***2] 353 of Banking Law specific provisions of Federal act and regulations is not violative of section 16 of article III of State Constitution because bill amending section was recommended by State Banking Department -- no unconstitutional delegation of legislative power.

1. An action to declare that a loan from defendant, a licensed lender, to plaintiffs payable monthly over three years is void under section 358 of the Banking Law is subject to the three-year Statute of Limitations ( CPLR 214, subd 2). Section 358, which provides that any loan in the making of which any act shall have been done constituting a misdemeanor shall be void and the lender shall have no right to collect principal or interest, is a State statute pre-existing the State and Federal Truth in Lending Acts. Although defendant failed to furnish plaintiffs with items required to be disclosed under the Federal act, the one-year Statute of Limitations in the Federal act applicable to actions to recover civil penalties does not govern the present action. The Legislature did not intend to subject actions under section 358 to multiple Statutes of Limitations.

2. Plaintiffs do not have a right to judgment for all payments [***3] of principal and interest voluntarily made on the loan. Such an action was not recognized at common law and section 358 of the Banking Law establishes no such remedy. The statute creates a defense for the borrower in an action by the lender where disclosure requirements of section 353 have not been met and, since it deprives the lender of the right to collect, it must be strictly construed.

3. A claim by plaintiffs, for payments made after the institution of the action and under a reservation of rights and without prejudice, may bear on the issue of voluntariness and requires exploration and to that extent plaintiffs' first cause of action is reinstated.

4. The failure of the Legislature to incorporate into section 353 of the Banking Law the specific provisions of the Federal act and regulations is not violative of section 16 of article III of the State Constitution, which states that no act shall be passed which shall provide that existing law shall be made part of said act except by inserting it in such act. The bill amending section 353 was recommended by the State Banking Department (NY Const, art III, § 21).

5. Being aware of the conduct that it was requiring of licensed [***4] lenders, the Legislature exercised, rather than delegated, its legislative function when it subjected them to penalties. Furthermore to the extent that incorporation by reference is to existing law and regulations applicable to citizens of this State, it is a permissible legislative device.

 

1302 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Evans v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


53 A.D.2d 109; 385 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12485



July 2, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state governor and others, appealed a judgment entered in favor of plaintiff state employees by the Supreme Court of Erie County (New York), which declared N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 3.10 (1975) unconstitutional and granted injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The challenged executive order directed the filing of financial statements by state officers and employees in policy-making positions or earning $ 30,000 or more in exempt, non-competitive, or unclassified positions within the executive department or any other state department or agency, the head of which was appointed or nominated by the governor. The executive order prohibited those officers and employees from engaging in any other public or private employment, from serving as director or officer of any profit-making corporation, and from serving as an officer of any political party or political organization. The trial court found that the order was unconstitutional as construed and applied, and denied the governor's and others' cross motion for summary judgment. The court reversed and held that the executive order was constitutional in view of its finding that there was no state interest more compelling than the assertion of the right of the public to have relevant information concerning the conduct of its government and its employees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that determined the unconstitutionality of the executive order and granted the governor's and other's cross motion for judgment declaring the executive order constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- financial disclosure by public employees -- employee political activity -- Executive Order No. 10 of 1975, which requires State employees in policy-making positions or earning $ 30,000 or more in exempt, noncompetitive or unclassified positions in departments, head of which is appointed by Governor, to file financial disclosure statements and prohibits such persons from engaging in any other public or private employment or serving as officer of profit-making corporation or political party, is constitutional, as indicated by various Supreme Court decisions and in view of compelling State interest of public right to relevant information concerning its government and its employees.

Executive Order No. 10 of 1975 (9 NYCRR 3.10), which requires State employees in policy-making positions or earning $ 30,000 or more in exempt, noncompetitive or unclassified positions in departments, the head of which [***2] is appointed by the Governor, to file financial disclosure statements and prohibits such persons from engaging in any other public or private employment or serving as an officer of a profit-making corporation or a political party, is constitutional. The Supreme Court has upheld similar political restrictions imposed by the Hatch Act as sufficiently narrow to achieve the legitimate purpose of curtailing partisan political activity among public employees without intruding upon areas of expression and association not substantially connected to such activity. The Supreme Court has held that the government may require information based upon legitimate governmental concerns with respect to private financial transactions of public employees in the face of a claim of invasion of the right to privacy or specific constitutional guarantees. The Fifth Amendment does not protect reasonable expectations of privacy with respect to documents obtained without compulsion of self incriminatory testimony, and no assertion is made that disclosure under Executive Order No. 10 would tend to incriminate any persons covered by it. Recent Supreme Court cases support the view that such disclosure does not [***3] violate the Fourth Amendment, and indicate that the salary limit, which would restrict application to only 700 persons, violates no constitutional provision. The most compelling of State interests is the public right to relevant information concerning its government and its employees.

 

1303 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Pompey v. Parker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


53 A.D.2d 125; 385 N.Y.S.2d 959; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12488



July 2, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court Onondaga County (New York), which affirmed defendant government entity's actions that denied the property owner's application for a permit to maintain a mobile home as a permanent residence. The property owner also sought review of the trial court's determination that the government entity's ordinance was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The property owner argued that the government entity's zoning ordinance was an improper exercise of its police regulatory power and also deprived him of his fundamental right to travel and abide and thus violated the Fifth, Ninth, and Fourteenth Amendments of the Unites States Constitution. The court rejected the property owner's arguments and affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court pointed out that the property owner failed to maintain his burden of proof that the government entity's zoning ordinance was clearly unreasonable and was not really designed to accomplish a legitimate purpose affecting public health, safety, morals or general welfare. The court also noted that the government entity's exclusion of mobile homes from certain areas within its boundaries did not violate the property owner's right to equal protection. The court concluded that the property owner had failed to establish that the government entity's actions in prohibiting a mobile home from being constructed on the property owner's premises violated the property owner's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the government entity's zoning ordinance was constitutional and which granted an injunction restraining the violation of the zoning ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- mobile homes -- Zoning Ordinance of Town of Pompey constitutionally regulates use of land for mobile homes by prohibiting permanent use and allowing only one-year special permits, since regulatory scheme is presumptively valid and has not been shown to be unreasonable; while right to travel and abide is fundamental penumbral right under Constitution, ordinance does not have purpose or effect of total exclusion; ordinance does not violate due process or equal protection clauses, since it has not been shown to be based upon improper considerations; mobile home owners are not suspect category.

The Zoning Ordinance of the Town of Pompey is constitutional insofar as it regulates the use of land for mobile homes by prohibiting permanent use and allowing only one-year special permits. The regulatory scheme has not been shown to be unreasonable [***2] and is presumptively valid. While the right to travel and abide is a fundamental right within the penumbra of the Constitution, the ordinance does not effect a total exclusion of mobile homes, nor does it have that sole purpose. The ordinance does not deprive mobile home owners of due process or equal protection, since it has not been shown to have been based upon improper considerations having no substantial relation to the public health, safety, moral or general welfare. Mobile home owners do not qualify as members of a suspect category for which a classification would require a compelling State interest.

 

1304 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Browne v. Browne


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


53 A.D.2d 134; 385 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12490



July 2, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant ex-husband sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York), which awarded plaintiff ex-wife temporary counsel fees, enjoined the ex-husband from proceeding with an action for divorce brought by him in Texas, and denied a cross motion by him to dismiss that portion of the complaint which requested in personam relief.


OVERVIEW: The ex-wife first instituted an action for divorce by personally serving the ex-husband with process in Texas, the state in which he resided. The ex-husband later commenced a divorce action in Texas. Following the enactment of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(b), the ex-wife commenced another divorce action in New York. New York was the state where the parties had resided for most of the marriage. The trial court enjoined the ex-husband from proceeding in Texas and denied a cross-motion to dismiss the portion of the complaint requesting in personam relief. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the ex-husband's contacts with New York were not such that the action against him offended traditional notions of fair play and substantial justice. The court held that Rule 302(b), as applied, satisfied the mandate of constitutional due process.


OUTCOME: A judgment of the lower court enjoining the ex-husband from proceeding in a divorce action in Texas and denying his cross-motion for in personam relief in a divorce proceeding involving the ex-wife was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- jurisdiction -- CPLR 302 (subd [b]), which subjects nonresident defendant to in personam jurisdiction in matrimonial action without personal service in this State if plaintiff is resident or domiciliary provided that this State "was the matrimonial domicile of the parties before their separation", satisfies mandates of constitutional due process; plaintiff wife and defendant husband were lifelong residents of New York until October, 1973, when defendant went to Texas where he was personally served with process; marriage of parties has roots deeply embedded in New York; plaintiff and three children born of marriage continue to reside here -- defendant was properly enjoined from prosecuting Texas divorce action pursuant to  [***2] CPLR 6301; Texas decree would tend to render plaintiff's New York judgment "ineffectual".

1. CPLR 302 (subd [b]), which subjects a nonresident, nondomiciliary defendant to in personam jurisdiction in a matrimonial action without personal service in this State "if the party seeking support is a resident of or domiciled in this State" provided that this State "was the matrimonial domicile of the parties before their separation", satisfies the mandate of constitutional due process. Although married in Mississippi in 1953, both plaintiff wife and defendant husband were lifelong residents of New York until October, 1973 when defendant went to Texas where plaintiff served him personally with process in an action for divorce in November, 1974. Plaintiff and three children born of the marriage continue to reside in this State. Under such circumstances, the marriage of these parties has roots deeply embedded in New York. The State's abiding interest in those of the family who remain as domiciliaries should not be cut off because a marital partner prefers another climate. To permit defendant to circumvent obligations which had accrued during his long association with this State would [***3] be repugnant to a rational concept of justice.

2. Defendant was properly enjoined pursuant to CPLR 6301 from prosecuting a Texas divorce action which he commenced in June, 1974. A judgment of divorce in favor of defendant in Texas would violate "plaintiff's rights respecting the subject of the action" and would tend to render her New York judgment "ineffectual". A Texas decree, if procured before disposition of this New York case, could result in the dismissal of so much of plaintiff's action as seeks a divorce. Since the foreign decree will affect only the marital status, it would be duplicitous to allow defendant, who has the economic stability to protect his rights in New York, to pursue in Texas the same goal he may affirmatively achieve here in response to plaintiff's suit.

3. Plaintiff was properly awarded counsel fees.

 

1305 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Passante v. Walden Printing Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


53 A.D.2d 8; 385 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12467



July 1, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Decision reversed, and matter remitted to the Workmen's Compensation Board for further proceedings not inconsistent herewith, with costs to claimant against respondents filing briefs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimant challenged a decision from the Workmen's Compensation Board, which found in favor of defendant employer in the claimant's action for payment of death benefits to the claimant, a surviving husband.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 16 granted death benefits to any surviving wife of a deceased male employee but restricted the availability of such benefits only to those surviving husbands who demonstrated dependency upon deceased female employees. The claimant, who could not demonstrate dependency upon his deceased wife, was therefore denied death benefits, and he appealed. The court held that § 16 discriminates against the married woman whose employment contributed to the economic well-being of her family, whereas the working man was given the security of knowing that in the event of his work-related death, his widow would not suffer the loss of his entire income. The working woman was given no such guarantee. Section 16 compelled dissimilar treatment for both surviving husbands and working wives, respectively, vis-a-vis widows and working males. Such a sex-based differentiation could not be sustained. The dependency requirement applicable to husbands under § 16 was unconstitutional. The Board, therefore, was required to award death benefits to surviving husbands on the same basis as such benefits were awarded to widows.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Board's decision and remitted the matter to the Board for further proceedings not inconsistent with the court's opinion. The court awarded costs to the claimant.


HEADNOTES

Workmen's compensation -- death benefits -- dependency of surviving husband -- section 16 of Workmen's Compensation Law, insofar as it grants death benefits to any surviving wife of deceased male employee but restricts availability of such benefits only to surviving husbands who demonstrate dependency on deceased female employees is unconstitutional as violative of equal protection; statute commands dissimilar treatment for those similarly situated by discriminating against married women whose employment contributes to economic well-being of her family -- Workmen's Compensation Board shall be required to award death benefits to surviving husbands on same basis as to widows.

1. Section 16 of the Workmen's Compensation Law, insofar as it grants death benefits to any surviving wife of a deceased male employee but restricts the availability of such benefits only to surviving husbands who demonstrate dependency [***2] on deceased female employees, is unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection clause. Such a sex-based differentiation cannot be sustained. The statute commands dissimilar treatment for those similarly situated. The rationale that widows are entitled to a presumption of dependency to promote the public good by protecting workmen and their dependents not only admits of being discriminatory in purpose but also fails to convince when it appears that a substantial number of married women are not in fact dependent on their husbands for support. The statute discriminates against the married woman whose employment contributes to the economic well-being of her family. The differentiation rests on a generalization which has little relationship to present reality and cannot be tolerated under the Constitution. It gives support to a philosophy which minimizes the importance of employment for women, thus using past discrimination as a justification for continuing to burden them as a class with economic disadvantages.

2. Until further legislative action is taken, the Workmen's Compensation Board shall be required to award death benefits to surviving husbands on the same basis as [***3] they are presently awarded to widows.

Nassau Ins. Co. v. McMorris

1308 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Bank of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


53 A.D.2d 55; 385 N.Y.S.2d 49; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12480



June 24, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant guardian of the illegitimate child challenged the decision of the New York County Surrogate's Court (New York), which held the term "issue" in a will had to be construed to exclude illegitimate grandchildren of an income beneficiary of a trust established under the terms of that will.


OVERVIEW: The will established a trust and provided that when the beneficiaries should die, his one-half share of the income should be paid for the remainder of the trust term "to his issue." An heir died leaving two children, the infants represented by the guardian. The heir never married the mother of these children, nor was an order of filiation entered. Relying on precedent, the trial court ruled that the two illegitimate children could not inherit, and absent intention to the contrary, it could not be assumed that the testatrix intended illegitimate descendants as the object of her bounty. The guardian asserted that the change in conventional attitudes towards illegitimates, as reflected in statutes and decisions, would warrant a construction of the word "issue" as including illegitimates, in the absence of contrary intent to exclude them. The court held that, to the extent that precedents required this outcome, the law was not only outmoded, but also discriminatory and was to be rejected. The court held that the word "issue" had to be construed to refer to legitimate and illegitimate descendants alike in the absence of an express qualification by the testatrix.


OUTCOME: The decree of the trial court was unanimously reversed, on the law and on the facts, and upon a reading of the will, it was determined that the testatrix intended in her use of the word "issue" in her will legitimate and illegitimate descendants. The will was so construed.


HEADNOTES

Wills -- construction -- in absence of express qualification by testatrix, she intended, in her use of word "issue" in her will, to include both legitimate and illegitimate descendants.

Testatrix died in 1951, leaving a will in which she established a trust for the benefit of her two cousins, and provided that, when the first of the two should die, his one-half share of the income should be paid for the remainder of the trust term to his "issue". One cousin died in 1965, survived by a daughter and a son, and the latter died in 1972, survived by two illegitimate children. He never married the mother of these children, nor was an order of filiation ever issued. The Surrogate ruled that these two children, being illegitimate, could not inherit. That part of his decree is reversed, and it is determined that, in the absence of an express qualification by testatrix, she intended, in her use of the word "issue",  [***2] to include both legitimate and illegitimate descendants.

 

1309 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hankins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 470; 384 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12032



June 17, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law and the facts, and indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Albany County Court (New York), which convicted him of criminally negligent homicide and assault in the second degree, claiming that he was denied his rights under the U.S. Const. and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.30 to a speedy trial as a result of the approximately 41-month delay between the date of the indictment and the date of trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's trial was scheduled three months after his indictment but the prosecuting attorney requested an adjournment because a principal witness was being hospitalized. Two years later, defendant moved for a dismissal of the indictment based on the denial of a speedy trial. The trial court did not render a decision on his motion and after almost another year defendant again moved for dismissal. The trial court denied this motion, finding that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.30 had not been violated because the State was at all times ready to proceed, and a jury convicted defendant. On review, the court reversed, finding that § 30.30 was inapplicable to the case and that the State had been formally put on notice by defendant's first motion that he was not acquiescing in any delay and was insisting upon his right to a speedy trial. The prosecution made no attempt to have the case tried and offered no excuse for the denial of defendant's right to a speedy trial. The only excuse offered by the trial court for the delay was calendar congestion, which might have excused the delay prior to defendant's first motion, but offered no excuse when a reasonable time passed after the motion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions and dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- right to speedy trial -- defendant was denied his right to speedy trial as result of 41-month delay between date of indictment and date of trial; where court makes no response to motion based upon denial of right to speedy trial and prosecution makes no apparent attempt to have case tried, aside from marking it "ready", net effect is denial of only procedural device defendant has to secure his rights; while calendar congestion might be some excuse prior to first motion for dismissal upon ground of denial of speedy trial, it offers no excuse when reasonable time passes after motion -- CPL 30.30, which would have required commencement of trial within six months, is not applicable to this situation.

1. Defendant's trial, at which he was found guilty of criminally negligent homicide and assault, commenced on January 7, 1975, approximately 41 months after the date of his indictment on July 29,  [***2] 1971. The case was set for trial in October, 1971, but was adjourned to the November Term on the request of the prosecuting attorney because a principal witness was scheduled to enter the hospital. Defendant was thereupon released from custody. By a motion returnable in November, 1973, defendant moved for a dismissal of the indictment upon the ground that he had been denied a speedy trial and requested a dismissal of charges pursuant to CPL 30.30. The court did not render a decision on the speedy trial motion and so defendant moved again by motion returnable in September, 1974, which motion was denied by the court in a decision rendered December 31, 1974. Although no particular time of delay will require a dismissal, a delay of 44 months from arrest in this action, in the absence of a showing of facts which establish that the delay was compatible with the sound administration of justice, is upon its face a patent frustration of the right to a speedy trial. Where a court makes no response to a motion based upon a denial of the right to a speedy trial and where the prosecution makes no apparent attempt to have the case tried, aside from marking it "ready", the net effect is a denial [***3] of the only procedural device a defendant has to secure his right in the court where the criminal charges are pending. No excuse was offered by the prosecution for this denial of defendant's right and while calendar congestion might be some excuse prior to the first motion, it offers no excuse when a reasonable time passes after the motion.

2. CPL 30.30, which would have required the commencement of a trial within six months, is not applicable to this situation since it expressly exempts from its provisions charges relating to violations of Penal Law sections that were charged in the indictment. Furthermore, this statute was expressly made applicable only to actions commenced on or after May 1, 1972.

 

1310 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


53 A.D.2d 777; 384 N.Y.S.2d 551; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13559



June 17, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the County Court of Albany County (New York) to criminal sale of a controlled substance. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on two counts of third-degree sale of a controlled substance and one count each of possession of stolen property and possession of a weapon. On appeal, defendant argued that his plea of guilty was improperly received and that a hearing should have been conducted to determine whether he was entitled to be sentenced to probation. The court stated that the trial court carefully questioned defendant about the facts and circumstances surrounding the count to which he pleaded guilty. Moreover, it held, the record showed that the trial court fully advised defendant of his rights and that he understood what he was doing when he entered his plea of guilty. Finally, the court stated, the record clearly demonstrated that the trial court informed defendant prior to sentencing that the minimum sentence would be imposed unless the district attorney recommended special treatment pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 65.00(1)(b); here, no such recommendation was made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

 

1311 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Plaza Development Corp. v. Vogt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 396; 384 N.Y.S.2d 67; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12007



June 10, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York, which held a state obscenity law was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a movie theater owner, challenged the constitutionality of the seizure of reels of film and a projector for alleged violations of a statute prohibiting obscenity. The court affirmed the judgment in favor of plaintiff. The court held the statute was overly broad and failed to conform to the specific standard of obscenity required by the U.S. Supreme Court. In addition, the court held the seizure of the projector equipment, without the benefit of an adversary hearing, violated plaintiff's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment entered in plaintiff's favor because the obscenity statute was unconstitutionally overbroad and failed to provide for an adversary hearing.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- obscenity -- section 410.00 of Penal Law, providing for seizure of equipment used in connection with pornographic motion pictures, is unconstitutional on its face; definition of pornographic motion pictures appearing in subdivision 9 of section 410.00 is overly broad and does not conform to criteria established by United States Supreme Court -- subdivision 1 of section 410.00, permitting seizure of equipment such as projectors vital to operation of plaintiff theatre operator's business without benefit of prior or prompt hearing, violates plaintiff's rights as guaranteed by First and Fourteenth Amendments to Federal Constitution.

1. Section 410.00 of the Penal Law, pursuant to which motion picture projectors, reels of film and other items were seized upon warrants from a motion picture theatre operated by plaintiff, is unconstitutional on its [***2] face. The State may, in accordance with criteria set forth by Miller v California (413 U.S. 15), proscribe only works which depict or describe sexual conduct and the prohibited conduct must be specifically defined by the applicable State law. Moreover, State offenses must be limited to works which appeal to the prurient interest in sex, portray sexual conduct in a patently offensive way, and lack serious literary, artistic, political or scientific value. The definition of pornographic motion pictures appearing in subdivision 9 of section 410.00 as "motion [pictures] showing acts of sexual intercourse or acts of sexual perversion" is overly broad and does not conform to the criteria established by the United States Supreme Court.

2. Furthermore, subdivision 1 of section 410.00 permitted the seizure of equipment such as projectors vital to any operation of plaintiff's business without the benefit of a prior adversary proceeding and with no provision for the prompt conduct of such a hearing following the seizure. Such procedures are in direct violation of plaintiff's rights as guaranteed by the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the Federal Constitution.

 

1312 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Siciliano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


52 A.D.2d 408; 384 N.Y.S.2d 994; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12011



June 10, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Three judgments, Supreme Court, Bronx County, each rendered February 27, 1976, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the judgment from the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which convicted them, upon their pleas of guilty, of the crime of obstructing governmental administration, and sentenced each of them to probation and a fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendants claimed that it was the custom and practice in the county systematically and intentionally to mail qualification notices to prospective jurors based upon a fixed mathematical formula that specifically discriminated against women. They claimed that a substantially greater number of such qualification notices were sent to men than to women with the result that substantially more men than women were qualified for service. The court held that: (1) defendants' pleas of guilty did not waive their possible challenges to the constitutionality of N.Y. Jud. Law § 598; (2) however, because § 598 had a rational basis it withstood their constitutional challenge; (3) defendants' right to appeal following their guilty plea was governed by statute; and (4) because the record contained no persuasive evidence that would mandate judicial inquiry, the court declined to extend itself to decide constitutional issues. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments rendered by the trial court, which convicted defendants of obstructing governmental administration following their pleas of guilty.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- disqualification of public employees -- where defendants, indicted for opening drawbridges during strike, pleaded guilty and were convicted of obstructing governmental administration, judgments are affirmed; by their pleas of guilty defendants did not waive constitutional challenge to section 598 of Judiciary Law -- section 598 of Judiciary Law, which disqualifies from Grand Jury service public employees earning in excess of $ 1,000 annually, has rational basis and is not unconstitutional -- hearing on methods used to impanel Grand Jury which returned indictments is not warranted.

1. Defendants were employed by the City of New York as bridge tenders. In furtherance of a plan conceived by their union to maximize disruption during a strike by municipal employees,  [***2] each defendant opened his drawbridge, removed electrical equipment so that the bridge could not be closed, and left his post. Indicted by the Bronx County Grand Jury, defendants subsequently pleaded guilty and were convicted of obstructing governmental administration (Penal Law, § 195.05). The judgments are affirmed. By their pleas of guilty, defendants did not waive a challenge to the constitutionality of section 598 of the Judiciary Law. Such challenge was timely raised prior to the entry of the guilty pleas.

2. Section 598 of the Judiciary Law, which disqualifies from Grand Jury service public employees earning in excess of $ 1,000 annually, has a rational basis and is not unconstitutional. The fact that public service employees may serve on petit juries and in certain instances on Grand Juries in this State, negates any suggestion that section 598 rests on an assumption of bias inhering in public service employees merely by nature of their employment.

3. A hearing on the methods used to impanel the Grand Jury which returned the indictments is not warranted. There is no support in the record for the statements attributed to the County Clerk of Bronx County that it was the [***3] practice in that county to systematically and intentionally mail qualification notices to prospective jurors based on a mathematical formula which discriminated against women with the result that substantially more men than women were qualified for service. Nor can a stipulation by the District Attorney that the County Clerk engaged in a practice of summoning fewer women than men for jury qualification be equated with a practice of "systematic exclusion and discrimination" sufficient to make a factual showing that the Grand Jury was constituted illegally.

 

1313 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nik-O-Lok Co. v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 375; 384 N.Y.S.2d 211; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12002



June 3, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs, and preliminary injunction vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company complained of the decision of the trial court, which granted respondent State's motion for summary judgment in the company's action that sought to declare 1975 N.Y. Laws 666 to be invalid.


OVERVIEW: The company manufactured coin-operated locks. It brought this action to declare unconstitutional a law that prohibited pay toilets on certain real estate. The trial court granted the State's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the trial court. The court held that the statute was not invalid due to vagueness. The court held that the standard was one of reasonableness, and that the court would apply the plain meaning of the statute's language in its interpretation. In applying the usual standards of statutory construction, the court ruled that it was possible to determine that the pay toilet prohibited by the act meant one in which there was a direct charge for access to a stall in order to eliminate human waste. The statute was presumed to be a valid legislative act, and the court held that the company had not met its burden to overturn that presumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted the State's motion for summary judgment in the company's action to declare a statute unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- pay toilet facilities -- section 399-a of General Business Law, prohibiting operation of pay toilet facilities on real property, is constitutional; plaintiffs do not occupy real property where they operate pay toilet facilities and, accordingly, lack standing to argue that they have been denied equal protection and that penalty language of statute is unconstitutionally vague -- term "pay toilet facilities" is not so ambiguous that it cannot be determined what statute prohibits -- statute is valid exercise of police power and bears reasonable relation to its assumed object of increasing toilet accessibility -- statute does not deprive plaintiffs of their property without due process of law -- preliminary injunction is vacated.

1. Section 399-a of the General Business Law (L 1975, ch 666), which prohibits the operation of pay toilet [***2] facilities on real property, is constitutional. Plaintiffs are corporations that manufacture equipment suitable for installation on doors which they lease to the owners or tenants of real property who provide toilet facilities to their customers or the general public. Since plaintiffs do not occupy real property where they operate pay toilet facilities, they are not subject to the penalty feature of the statute, and, accordingly, do not have standing to argue that they have been denied equal protection or to assert that the penalty language of the statute is unconstitutionally vague.

2. The term "pay toilet facilities" is not so ambiguous that it cannot be determined what the statute prohibits. One commonly understands a "pay toilet" to mean an immediate charge for the singular use of a closet for the discharge of human waste. While the term "pay toilet facilities" might contemplate a somewhat broader field of services for which immediate payment is required, these plaintiffs have not overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality favoring a statute enacted by the Legislature.

3. The statute is a valid exercise of the police power and bears a reasonable relation to its [***3] assumed object of increasing toilet accessibility. Whether better means might have been selected or whether this statute fails of its desired purpose is not the concern of the court in resolving this phase of the action.

4. Since the statute is a valid exercise of police power, it does not deprive plaintiffs of their property without due process of law.

5. The preliminary injunction enjoining operation of the statute is vacated.

 

1314 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Director, Downstate Medical Center, State University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


52 A.D.2d 357; 384 N.Y.S.2d 189; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12030



June 1, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, entered October 29, 1975, reversed, on the law, with $ 50 costs and disbursements, petition granted and it is declared that section 5.3 (d) of the Rules and Regulations of the Department of Civil Service, as applied to the petitioner, is unconstitutional. Respondent is directed to reinstate petitioner with back pay, less the amount of compensation earned in any other employment or occupation and any unemployment benefits he may have received during such period. No fact findings were presented for review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, former state employee, appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, denied the former state employee's petition to review his employment termination and dismissed the petition in all respects.


OVERVIEW: The former state employee contended that his petition was not barred by the applicable statute of limitations and that his constitutionally protected due process rights were violated when his "resignation" was accepted on the authority of § 5.3(d) of the Rules and Regulations of the Department of Civil Service. The former employee's "resignation" was based on his absence for 10 days without leave. The court held that the former state employee's petition was not time-barred. According to the court, because the former state employee was discharged without a hearing, the former state employee was not required to seek reinstatement without four months form discharge but could delay until such time as he demanded reinstatement and was refused. The court held that the former state employee initiated the instant proceeding 10 days prior to the expiration of 4 months from the date of his demand for reinstatement. The court also held that § 5.3(d) was unconstitutional because it deprived the former state employee of due process. According to the court, the former state employee was denied disciplinary proceedings and a hearing as required under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the former state employee's petition to challenge his employment termination, and granted the former state employee reinstatement with back pay and other benefits.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- termination of employment without due process -- article 78 proceeding to review termination of petitioner's employment was not barred by four-month Statute of Limitations; he was discharged without hearing and, accordingly, was not required to seek reinstatement within four months of discharge but could delay until his demand for reinstatement had been refused -- two-month  [***2] delay in demand for resinstatement did not constitute laches -- section 5.3 (d) of Rules and Regulations of Department of Civil Service (4 NYCRR 5.3 [d]), which provides that when employee is absent without leave and explanation for 10 days, such absence shall be deemed to constitute resignation, is violative of due process clause insofar as respondent employer applied it to petitioner; section created irrebuttable presumption insofar as petitioner was not afforded notice and opportunity for hearing -- petitioner reinstated with back pay.

1. Petitioner, a civil service employee with permanent status, was advised by letter dated December 26, 1974 that, as he had neither reported for work since December 11, 1974 nor offered any explanation for his absence, his "resignation" was accepted effective December 10, 1974 (4 NYCRR 5.3 [d]). An article 78 proceeding commenced June 10, 1975 to review the termination of petitioner's employment was not barred by the four-month Statute of Limitations ( CPLR 217). Petitioner was discharged without a hearing or there was an acceptance of a resignation which he had never offered. Accordingly, petitioner was not required to seek reinstatement [***3] within four months of discharge but could delay until his demand for reinstatement had been refused. His demand by letter of February 20, 1975 for reinstatement was not answered and, therefore, the commencement of the proceeding was 10 days prior to the expiration of four months from the date of his demand.

2. The two-month delay in the demand for reinstatement did not constitute laches, especially since there was no answer to petitioner's letter demanding reinstatement. The delay was not unduly long or one calculated to prejudice the respondent employer.

3. Section 5.3 (d) of the Rules and Regulations of the Department of Civil Service (4 NYCRR 5.3 [d]), which provides that when an employee is absent without leave and without explanation for 10 work days "such absence shall be deemed to constitute a resignation effective on the date of the commencement of such absence", is unconstitutional insofar as respondent applied it to petitioner. The section, in effect, created an irrebuttable presumption insofar as petitioner was never given an opportunity to explain his absence in a hearing. Petitioner's civil service employment is a thing of substantial value in which petitioner [***4] has a property interest, i.e., a protected interest under the due process clause of the Constitution. Respondent could not deprive petitioner of that interest without affording him his rights of notice and an opportunity for a hearing.

4. Petitioner is reinstated effective December 10, 1974 with back pay.

PEOPLE v. SMITH


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT

Brand v. Bartlett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 272; 383 N.Y.S.2d 668; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11993



May 27, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Motion denied, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city marshals filed an application pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6312(a) for a preliminary injunction restraining defendants, city administrator, Commissioner of Investigation, and appellate judges, from enforcement of existing fee schedules, and suspending any accused city marshals pending a hearing and determination of charges against them.


OVERVIEW: The city marshals filed an application pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6312(a) for a preliminary injunction restraining defendants from enforcement of existing fee schedules, and suspending any accused city marshals pending a hearing and determination of charges against them, under N.Y. Civil Ct. Act § 1610. The court denied the city marshal's motion for a preliminary injunction. The court held that the city marshals failed to show a likelihood of success in the action and also failed to prove that they would suffer irreparable injury if the injunction was not granted, as well they failed to prove a clear legal right before such relief could be granted. Because the city marshals failed to establish a clear right to the relief they sought, or that they had been irreparably damages, they were not entitled to injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court denied the city marshal's motion for a preliminary injunction restraining defendants from enforcing the existing fee schedule and authorization of suspension of accused marshals pending a hearing and determination of charges against them.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- preliminary injunction -- in action for declaratory judgment and injunction, New York City Marshals seek declaration of invalidity of provisions of joint order of Appellate Divisions of First and Second Judicial Departments, which permit Marshal to charge only fees prescribed by law and set forth in schedule issued by New York City Commissioner of Investigation, empower Commissioner to issue directives regarding official day-to-day activities of Marshals and to promulgate handbook of regulations for them, establish procedures for hearing charges against them, empower Commissioner to impose penalties upon guilty ones, and allow Marshal 20 days within which to appeal from his decision, and they also allege Commissioner's directives are invalid and that section 1610 of New York City Civil Court Act is unconstitutional insofar as it authorizes suspension of Marshal pending hearing and determination of charges -- motion by them for preliminary injunction is denied -- complex issues remain to be resolved before it can be determined whether they will ultimately  [***2] succeed in action, and they have not established they are being deprived of statutorily mandated fees -- having failed to establish clear legal right to relief they seek, or that they have been irreparably damaged, they are not entitled to injunctive relief pendente lite.

In an action for a declaratory judgment and an injunction, New York City Marshals seek a declaration of the invalidity of provisions of a joint order of the Appellate Divisions of the First and Second Judicial Departments, which permit a Marshal to charge only those fees prescribed by law and set forth in an official schedule of fees issued by the New York City Commissioner of Investigation (Commissioner); empower the Commissioner to issue directives regarding official day-to-day activities of Marshals and to promulgate a handbook of regulations for them; establish procedures for hearing charges against them; empower the Commissioner, after a hearing, to impose penalties upon guilty ones, and allow a Marshal 20 days within which to appeal from his decision. They also allege that all directives relating to their official conduct and record-keeping heretofore issued by the Commissioner are invalid, and that section [***3] 1610 of the New York City Civil Court Act is unconstitutional insofar as it authorizes the suspension of a Marshal pending a hearing and determination of charges against him. A motion by them for a preliminary injunction restraining the enforcement of the provisions of the joint order, the directives of the Commissioner, and section 1610 of the New York City Civil Court Act, pending the determination of their action, is denied. Many complex issues remain to be resolved before it can be determined whether they will ultimately succeed on the merits of their action. Moreover, they have not established that they are being deprived of statutorily mandated fees, and defendants have taken the position that, if there are any discrepancies between the fee schedule fixed by the Commissioner and the fees as authorized by statute, the errors have resulted in benefit to the Marshals. Having failed to establish a clear legal right to the relief they seek, or that they have been irreparably damaged, plaintiffs are not entitled to injunctive relief pendente lite.

Debevoise v. State Tax Com.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


52 A.D.2d 1023; 383 N.Y.S.2d 698; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12922



May 27, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, petitioner taxpayer sought review of a determination from respondent State Tax Commission (New York), which denied the taxpayer's application for redetermination of New York personal income tax. In seeking review, the taxpayer sought to apportion the taxpayer's distributive share from a New York law firm according to the number of days the taxpayer was actually present and engaged in legal work within New York.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer, a partner in a New York law firm who worked in Washington D.C., sought to apportion income from the law firm according to the number of days the taxpayer was working in New York. However, the Commission attributed this income to New York sources on the same basis utilized by the law partnership in allocating the distributive share of each partner according to the proportion that partnership net income from sources outside New York bore to partnership net income from all sources. Consequently, the taxpayer received a notice of deficiency, and when the taxpayer's application for redetermination was denied, the taxpayer filed an N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding. In upholding the Commission's denial, the court held the Commission had properly rejected the taxpayer's method of allocation for two reasons. First, the court held there was an insufficient showing that the percentage of time spent in New York had any relation to the distributive share of partnership income received during the tax year. Second, the court held it was the portion of the distributive share attributable to New York sources, not the value of personal services, which was subject to taxation.


OUTCOME: In affirming the Commission's denial of the taxpayer's application for redetermination of New York personal income tax, the court refused to apportion the taxpayer's distributive share from a New York law firm according to the number of days the taxpayer was actually present and engaged in legal work within New York.

Amato v. Ward


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


52 A.D.2d 945; 383 N.Y.S.2d 891; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12812



May 24, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of decision to withhold six months of his good behavior allowances by respondent corrections department on the grounds that the procedure authorized in N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 7, § 261.3 violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it allowed prisoners to be deprived of liberty without minimal due process protections applicable to time allowance proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner argued that he was denied due process of law under the Fourteenth Amendment when the corrections department subtracted six months of good behavior time based on petitioner's disciplinary record. The corrections department argued that the procedures established for the granting of good behavior allowances under N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 7, § 261 were in accord with the requirements of the Due Process Clause. The court affirmed the sentence modification despite its ruling that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 7, § 803 provided only minimal due process to petitioner. The court reasoned that while the regulations were unconstitutional, the statute upon which they were based was not unconstitutional. Nothing in the language of the statute specifically barred the granting of minimal due process rights. The court construed the statute to avoid constitutional doubts. In order to maintain the constitutionality of a statute, the court was allowed to read into it any requirements normally necessary to sustain its constitutional validity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the correction department's withholding of good behavior allowance from petitioner.

1321 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Giuliano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


52 A.D.2d 240; 383 N.Y.S.2d 878; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11987



May 18, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered on October 22, 1974, unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Supreme Court (New York), for attempted possession of a weapon, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 265.05(9); 110.00.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his attorney negotiated with an assistant district attorney in order to obtain a plea on an E felony. One of the charges from the 20 count indictment was chosen and defendant pleaded guilty. All parties, including the trial court, were in agreement. When it was determined that the particular pleading chosen was defective, defendant appealed, seeking to have his conviction reduced to a misdemeanor. The court determined that by pleading guilty, defendant waived the defect in the pleading. The plea was sustained.


OUTCOME: The court sustained defendant's plea of guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- possession of weapon as felony -- special information charging previous conviction -- defendant who, in satisfaction of 20-count indictment, entered plea of guilty to attempted possession of weapon as felony under count charging him with possession of weapon as felony, based on possession of imitation pistol, which possession was felony only if there had been previous conviction, may not obtain reduction of conviction to misdemeanor because of failure of District Attorney to file special information required by CPL 200.60, providing that, when fact of previous conviction raises offense of lower grade to higher grade, indictment for such higher offense may not allege previous conviction, but it must be accompanied by special information charging that defendant was previously convicted of specified offense.

Defendant who, in satisfaction of a 20-count indictment,  [***2] entered a plea of guilty to attempted possession of a weapon as a felony under a count charging him with possession of a weapon as a felony based on possession of an imitation pistol with intent to use the same unlawfully against another, in violation of then subdivision 9 of section 265.05 of the Penal Law, which made such possession a felony only if there had been a previous conviction of any crime, and otherwise a misdemeanor, may not obtain a reduction of his conviction to a misdemeanor because the District Attorney neglected to file the special information required by CPL 200.60, which provides that, when the fact of a previous conviction raises an offense of a lower grade to a higher grade, an indictment for such higher offense may not allege the previous conviction, but it must be accompanied by a special information charging that the defendant was previously convicted of a specified offense, which special information may not be referred to during the trial unless the defendant disputes the previous conviction. The purpose of CPL 200.60 is to protect a defendant against proof at the trial of the damaging fact of his conviction if he does not dispute it, and, here, there was [***3] no trial. The special information required by that section is in the nature of a bill of particulars, and a plea of guilty is a waiver of a failure to provide a bill of particulars. Moreover, defendant did not plead guilty to any count in the indictment, but to a lesser, almost hypothetical crime, and, therefore, the validity of the original counts is no longer material. The record indicates that the parties had agreed that he would plead to an E felony, and it was the merest chance that this particular one was chosen. The failure to file the special information should not invalidate the plea bargain which he, the District Attorney and the court knowingly made, and certainly he should not unilaterally be given a better bargain.

Kiwanis Club of Great Neck, Inc. v. Board of Trustees


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


52 A.D.2d 906; 383 N.Y.S.2d 383; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12754

V or to the provisions of the Federal Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 2000(a) et seq.
