1323 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Patterson v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 171; 383 N.Y.S.2d 414; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11974



May 13, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, Board of the Jones Beach State Parkway Authority and bank trustee, and defendants, governor and attorney general, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, in Albany County (New York), which declared N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 153-c(1) unconstitutional, enjoined the enforcement thereof, and declared all other provisions of that section constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Board challenged a decision of the trial court which held that § 153-c(1) was unconstitutional but that other provisions of the statute relating to a 120-day waiting period were constitutional. On appeal, the court held that the trial court correctly concluded that the 10-cent rollback provision constituted an unwarranted impairment of the state's pledge to bondholders as provided in N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 158-a(1). Because there was no allusion to any traffic emergency contained in N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 153-c, and the effect of the statute merely postponed the toll increase for 120 days, after which time presumably traffic would again become congested, it could not be said that the legislation was enacted to alleviate the traffic situation, nor that the toll rollback provision was reasonably related or appropriate to that end, even if such legislative intent did exist. The court held that because other provisions did not impair the obligation to the bondholders in any degree, there was no occasion for trial court to attempt to justify what it found to be a technical impairment caused by the exercise of the police power.


OUTCOME: An order of the lower court in a suit by plaintiffs questioning the constitutionality of a statute approved by defendants was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- tolls on State parkway -- provision in section 153-c of Public Authorities Law, enacted after Jones Beach State Parkway Authority raised toll on Southern State Parkway to 25 cents, rolling that toll back to 10 cents, is unconstitutional, since it constitutes unwarranted impairment of State's pledge to Authority's bondholders not to limit or alter rights of Authority to collect revenues and fix appropriate tolls, and such impairment may not be justified as reasonable exercise of State's police power, nor on basis that it was intended to remedy allegedly ultra vires act on part of Authority in increasing toll -- remaining provisions of section 153-c, which establish public disclosure procedures to be followed prior to any future toll increases,  [***2] are constitutional -- those disclosure provisions do not impair State's contractual obligation to Authority's bondholders, but make it clear that Authority still retains ultimate and unfettered power to determine toll charges and that its rights to perform its duties are not altered, nor can it be said that security of bondholders is impaired thereby.

1. Section 153-c of the Public Authorities Law, which was enacted after the Jones Beach State Parkway Authority had raised the toll on the Southern State Parkway from 10 cents to 25 cents, rolled back that toll to 10 cents and established procedures to be followed prior to any future toll increases, including the submission to the Comptroller of a detailed report, not less than 120 days prior to the effective date of the proposed increase, explaining its necessity, the publication by the Comptroller of his recommendations concerning the increase within 60 days, the holding of a public hearing not less than 15 days prior to the effective date of the proposed increase, the subsequent reconsideration by the Authority of the proposed increase, and then the taking of such action as the Authority deemed necessary and advisable. The provision [***3] therein for the toll rollback was properly held unconstitutional, and the other provisions therein were properly held constitutional.

2. The rollback provision constitutes an unwarranted impairment of a pledge made by the State to the Authority's bondholders, contained in section 158-a (subd 1) of the Public Authorities Law, that it will not limit or alter the rights of the Authority to collect revenues and fix appropriate tolls. Nor may the impairment be justified as a reasonable exercise of the State's police power, to alleviate traffic congestion on local streets resulting from motorists' attempting to avoid paying the toll increase on the parkway, since the legislation was not enacted to alleviate the traffic situation, nor was it reasonably related to that end. And a claim that the statute was intended to remedy an ultra vires act on the part of the Authority in increasing the toll can have no relevancy to an action concerning its constitutionality. Furthermore, since review of an allegedly ultra vires act would properly be the subject of an article 78 proceeding, and is not a legislative function, it could not serve to justify the impairment of a contractual obligation.

 [***4] 3. The remaining provisions of section 153-c, the disclosure provisions, do not impair the State's contractual obligation to the Authority's bondholders. They make it clear that the Authority still retains ultimate and unfettered power to determine toll charges and that its rights to perform its duties are not altered, nor can it be said that the security of the bondholders is impaired thereby. As is apparent from the stated legislative findings which accompanied recent legislation ( Public Authorities Law, § 2504), which becomes effective July 1, 1976, and which makes the same disclosure procedures applicable to all toll increases for all public authorities, they were enacted for the purpose of requiring a full and public disclosure of the financial status and the need for additional revenues, to indicate the soundness of the internal process of public authorities and to help gain public acceptance for a necessary toll increase.

 

1324 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Civil Service Employees Asso. v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


52 A.D.2d 100; 383 N.Y.S.2d 88; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11537



May 11, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment entered November 12, 1975 modified, on the law and the facts, by reversing so much thereof as requires the appellants to make restitution of allowances received pursuant to chapter 460 of the Laws of 1975, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.

Order and judgment entered February 13, 1976 modified, on the law and the facts, by reversing so much thereof as (1) requires the appellants to make restitution of allowances received pursuant to chapter 460 of the Laws of 1975, and (2) awards plaintiffs reasonable attorney's fees, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state officials sought review of the decisions of the Supreme Court, Albany County that, in two separate cases, declared 1975 N.Y. Laws 460 unconstitutional and ordered restitution of legislative allowances for additional services in favor of respondent corporations.


OVERVIEW: The corporations challenged a statute, 1975 N.Y. Laws 460, that allowed the state officials to increase allowances for certain legislative officers and to provide allowances for new legislative offices for which no allowances were authorized. The lower court found the statute was unconstitutional because it allowed the legislature to increase its own salary and ordered that the legislative officials make restitution for the illegal amount paid. The court affirmed the finding that the statute was unconstitutional because it violated N.Y. Const. art. III, § 6, however, it modified that portion of the order that required restitution. The court the payments were improperly authorized and that restitution was not proper.


OUTCOME: The court modified the determination of the lower court that that, in two separate cases, declared 1975 N.Y. Laws 460 unconstitutional and ordered restitution of legislative allowances for additional services in favor of respondent corporations.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- legislative allowances -- chapter 460 of Laws of 1975 violates section 6 of article III of New York Constitution, which prohibits increases in allowances or salaries  [***2] during elective term of member of Legislature; statute conferring upon legislative body power to fix own salaries should be strictly construed against such power; if initial fixing of allowances were allowed each year, allowances and, accordingly, salaries could be fixed twice for each term in violation of Constitution, while burden of predicting needs of incoming Legislature for additional services does not outweigh danger of allowing legislators to fix own salaries -- restitution of allowances received prior to suit is not warranted in interests of fairness and justice, while those allowances received subsequently should be returned.

1. Chapter 460 of the Laws of 1975 violates section 6 of article III of the New York State Constitution. The "payments in lieu of expenses" provided by such statute are actually compensation to members of the Legislature for services rendered, immediately applicable to legislators whose terms began in 1975, and therefore conflict with the constitutional prohibition against any increase in allowances or salaries during a legislator's elective term. A statute conferring upon a legislative body the power to fix its own salaries should be strictly [***3] construed and, unless clearly and unmistakably provided, the power will be denied. No distinction is made in the Constitution between allowances and salaries, and to construe its provisions to allow an initial fixing of allowances at the beginning of each year would logically require that there be two initial fixing of allowances during an elective term, with the same result obtaining with respect to salaries, absent section 5 of the Legislative Law, in direct violation of the Constitution. Although the prohibition against increases in allowances requires that the Legislature predict the needs of the incoming Legislature for additional services, there have been only eight new standing committees formed since 1949, and the burden of such prediction does not outweigh the dangers of allowing legislators to fix their own compensation.

2. Restitution of allowances received prior to the commencement of suit is not warranted in the interests of fairness and justice when the reliance placed upon the funds by the legislators is weighed against the constitutional harm of allowing the payments. Allowances paid after commencement of the proceedings should be returned.

 

1325 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Flushing Nat'l Bank v. Municipal Assistance Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


52 A.D.2d 84; 382 N.Y.S.2d 764; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11534



May 4, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on December 24, 1975, unanimously affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff national bank, on its behalf and on behalf of all similar note holders, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendants, the State of New York and the Municipal Assistance Corporation for the City of New York, and declared that the New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York (EMA), 1975 N.Y. Laws 874, 875, was valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The EMA provided for a three-year moratorium on enforcement of outstanding short-term obligations of the City of New York conditioned upon an offer to exchange the short-term obligations with a replacement obligation having a date of maturity no more than 20 years after the date of maturity of the short-term obligation. The bank claimed, inter alia, that the EMA was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court disagreed. The EMA was not a law impairing the obligation of contracts violative of U.S. Const. art I, § 10 because it only established a revised schedule for payment of the obligations. The EMA did not violate N.Y. Const. art. VIII, §§ 2, 10, 12, because the city pledged its faith and credit for payment of its obligations and the city had not violated the strictures of §§ 10 and 12 relating to the city's power to levy real estate taxes. The interest rate of 6 percent was not confiscatory because it was double that available on any other judgment or accrued claim against the city under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a. Finally, the EMA involved an extension of time for payment of a debt, not the "composition of indebtedness" encompassed by the Federal Bankruptcy Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the State and the City in that it declared the New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York valid and constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- moratorium legislation -- Emergency Moratorium Act for City of New York -- New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for City of New York, enacted to assist city at time of fiscal crisis and grave public emergency, and providing for three-year moratorium on enforcement of short-term city obligations, conditioned upon offer having been made to exchange such obligation with replacement obligation having date of maturity no more than 20 years after date of maturity of short-term obligation, is valid and constitutional.

The New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York (L 1975,  [***2] chs 874, 875), which was enacted to assist the city at a time of fiscal crisis and grave public emergency, provides for a conditional three-year moratorium on enforcement of short-term city obligations, conditioned upon an offer having been made to exchange such an obligation with a replacement obligation having a date of maturity no more than 20 years after the date of maturity of the short-term obligation. In the event the short-term noteholder declines to accept the exchange offer, payment on principal is to be suspended for a three-year period, and payment on interest is to be made at the rate stated in the obligation to the date of its maturity, and thereafter at a rate of not less than 6% per annum. This legislation is valid and constitutional. It does not violate section 10 of article I of the United States Constitution, prohibiting impairment of contractual obligations, but merely extends the time to make full payment. It does not violate sections 2, 10 or 12 of article VIII of the New York State Constitution, since the city has pledged its faith and credit for payment of its obligations and has not violated the strictures relating to its power to levy real estate taxes.  [***3] The interest rate of 6% offered on matured notes is not inadequate or confiscatory, but double that available on any judgment or other accrued claim against the city. As to an alleged violation of the Federal Bankruptcy Act, what is involved is an extension of the time for payment of an indebtedness and not the "composition of indebtedness" encompassed by that act.

 

1326 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Airlift International, Inc. v. State Tax Com.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


52 A.D.2d 688; 382 N.Y.S.2d 572; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12361



April 22, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer filed an action to review a determination of respondent state tax commissioner (commissioner), which denied the taxpayer's application for revision of a determination or refund of sales and use taxes.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the taxpayer should have been exempt from paying a sales or use tax on repairs, including labor and materials, made to an aircraft while the aircraft was damaged in the state. The court confirmed the commissioner's determination and dismissed the petition. The court held that the taxpayer was not entitled to a refund or credit of sales tax paid on the ground that the material used on its damaged aircraft was used for fabrication as opposed to repair purposes. The court also determined that a taxable event had occurred in the state despite the fact that a very short period of time had elapsed between the time that the material used in the repair of the aircraft arrived in the state and the time when the material was installed in the aircraft. Finally, the court held that the sales and use tax was not violative of the due process clause or the commerce clause because there was a definite link and minimum connection between the state, the taxpayer, the property, and the transaction that was taxed.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the commissioner's denial of the taxpayer's request for a refund of the sales and use tax it previously paid and dismissed the taxpayer's petition.

 

1327 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Albany Permanent Prof'l Firefighters Ass'n, Local 2007 v. Corning


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


51 A.D.2d 386; 381 N.Y.S.2d 699; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11087; 92 L.R.R.M. 2409



April 1, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York) which confirmed an arbitration award made by a public arbitration panel to the city's professional firefighters association (association), and of a judgment of that court which dismissed a petition, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to annul a determination of the public arbitration.


OVERVIEW: The controversy which gave rise to these proceedings arose from the failure of the association, the recognized bargaining agent for the city's firefighters, and the city to reach an agreement over the terms of an employment contract. After an impasse had been reached in the negotiations, the association filed a petition with a public employment relations board (board) requesting that the dispute be referred to a public arbitration panel (panel). After a hearing, the panel awarded the association one additional holiday and wage increases, but it rejected most of the other union demands. The court affirmed. The court found that the trial court erred in concluding that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 213 provided for review of a public arbitration panel's award under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 rather than N.Y. C.P.L.R. 75. The court further found that (1) under the standards of review contained in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 75, as broadened in scope by case law, the determination of the panel was not arbitrary or capricious; and (2) the board complied with N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 209(4)(c)(ii) in supplying a list of qualified, disinterested persons to serve as panel members.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme courts' order and judgment. The court found that the arbitration panel properly awarded the professional firefighters association an extra holiday and a wage increase.


HEADNOTES

Arbitration -- binding public arbitration -- review standards -- CPLR article 75 is proper review vehicle for binding public arbitration in public employment dispute, since it allows true adversaries to protect own interests, while article 78 would be brought against arbitration panel, with no budget to defend award, or against Public Employment Relations Board (PERB), which neither reviewed nor approved award -- provision for article 78 review of PERB orders (Civil Service Law, § 213) does not apply [***2] to review of public arbitration panel award, since award is not PERB order -- article 75 review of compulsory arbitration requires determination of support for award in evidence or other basis in reason appearing in record and that award not be arbitrary or capricious; according to such standard, panel properly considered ability of employer to pay salary increase -- petitioner city had no right to transcript of testimony before panel, since it made no timely request therefor in accordance with PERB rules -- PERB supplied list of qualified, disinterested persons to serve as panel members -- accordingly, award was properly confirmed.

1. CPLR article 75 is the proper vehicle for review of a binding determination of a public arbitration panel in a public employment dispute. Although article 78 and article 75, as extended to meet the requirements of due process in a compulsory arbitration case, have virtually the same standards for review, an article 78 proceeding would be brought against either the panel, which does not have the means to defend its award, or the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB), which neither reviewed nor approved the panel's determination. An article 75 [***3] proceeding would allow the real adversaries to protect their own interests.

2. Section 213 of the Civil Service Law, which provides for article 78 review of PERB orders, is inapplicable to review of a public arbitration panel award, since the award is not an order of PERB.

3. In an article 75 proceeding to review a compulsory arbitration, the court must determine whether the award is supported by evidence or other basis in reason appearing in the record and is not arbitrary or capricious. According to such review standard, the panel properly considered the ability of the employer to pay a salary increase.

4. In view of its failure to make a timely request therefor, in accordance with PERB rules, the petitioner city had no right to a transcript of testimony before the panel, and, in any event, the city was not prejudiced, since the record contains a sufficient basis for affirmance.

5. PERB fully complied with its duty to supply a list of qualified, disinterested persons to serve as panel members.

6. Accordingly, the order and judgment confirming the award should be affirmed.

Walker v. Walker


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


51 A.D.2d 1029; 381 N.Y.S.2d 310; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11824



March 15, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which, after a hearing, adjudged him in contempt for failure to pay alimony and child support and committed him to jail.


OVERVIEW: The husband challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 245, which provided that failure to pay support was punishable as a civil contempt. The court upheld the validity of the statute, observing that even if § 245 did not clearly require adequate notice of possible imprisonment, the court should, in order to sustain the statute's constitutionality, read such a requirement into it. Moreover, there was no denial of the husband's due process rights, in that he was served with proper process and was represented by counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the contempt order against the husband.

 

1330 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Parkchester Apts. Co. v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


51 A.D.2d 277; 381 N.Y.S.2d 230; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10691



March 4, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on October 10, 1975, unanimously modified, on the law, to the extent of deleting the reference to a severance and treatment of the motion as a separate article 78 proceeding while granting the motion for a supplemental judgment and, as so modified, the judgment is affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, attorney general and others, sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, New York County (New York), which treated an application by respondent company for a supplemental judgment as a separate Article 78 proceeding, severing it from the underlying proceeding, and directing the attorney general to review the company's condominium offering plan in accordance with the law in effect when the plan was submitted.


OVERVIEW: The company submitted a condominium offering plan for filing to the attorney general, which involved a portion of its housing complex. After the attorney general's acceptance, certain tenants filed an Article 78 proceeding. The company's second offering plan involved the remaining portion of the complex. The company obtained a judgment directing the attorney general to review the second plan and respond. The attorney general refused to review the plan, as it did not contain a statement in conformity with a new law. The company filed a motion for a supplemental judgment, directing the attorney general to review the plan in accordance with the law in effect when the plan was submitted, or, alternatively, declaring the new law unconstitutional. The judgment of the trial court was affirmed, as modified to delete the severance from the underlying proceeding and treatment of the company's motion as a separate proceeding. The court granted the company's motion for a supplemental judgment. The trial court erred in treating the company's motion as an independent application for relief. The company properly sought a supplemental judgment and was not required to file a new proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was modified to delete the severance from the underlying proceeding regarding the company's condominium offering plan and treatment of the company's motion as a separate proceeding. The court granted the company's motion for a supplemental judgment. The trial court's the judgment was affirmed, as modified.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- conversion to condominium -- following affirmance on appeal of judgment obtained by petitioner in 1973 article 78 proceeding to compel Attorney-General to act on offering plan, submitted by it for filing on November 26, 1973, for conversion of quadrants of apartment complex to condominium, he refused to review said plan, solely on ground that  [***2] it did not contain statement in conformity with new law, enacted during pendency of appeals from judgment, providing that no offering plan could become effective unless 35% of tenants in occupancy agreed to purchase their apartments -- on motion by petitioner for supplemental judgment in 1973 proceeding, directing that Attorney-General apply law in effect on November 26, 1973, Special Term severed said motion from 1973 proceeding, treated it as separate article 78 proceeding, and directed that he review plan in accordance with law in effect on November 26, 1973 -- its judgment is modified, by deleting reference to severance and treatment of motion as article 78 proceeding, and, as so modified, is affirmed -- dispositive of merits is rule that negligent or willful delay by governmental agency, which prevents issuance of license until after amendment of governing statute, will afford grounds for applying pre-existing law -- nothing in legislative history of new law suggests retroactive application -- attempt by petitioner to update plan does not warrant conclusion that fresh filing was engaged in -- no jurisdictional basis existed for treating petitioner's motion as independent application  [***3] for relief.

1. On November 26, 1973 petitioner had submitted for filing with the Attorney-General an offering plan for the conversion of three quadrants of its apartment complex to a condominium, and, when he refused to act on the plan, because of the pendency of litigation concerning his acceptance of an offering plan to convert the fourth quadrant to a condominium, petitioner commenced an article 78 proceeding to compel him to act, and, in 1974, obtained a judgment in its favor, which was affirmed by the Appellate Division and by the Court of Appeals. During the pendency of the appeals, chapter 1021 of the Laws of 1974 was enacted, amending section 352-e of the General Business Law to provide that no offering plan could become effective unless 35% of the tenants in occupancy agreed to purchase their apartments. When, following the completion of the appellate process, petitioner requested that its offering plan, as updated, be reviewed in accordance with the 1974 judgment, the Attorney-General refused to review it, solely on the ground that it did not contain a statement in conformity with the new "35% requirement". Petitioner then moved for a supplemental judgment in the 1973 [***4] proceeding, directing him to review the plan in accordance with the law in effect on November 26, 1973, or, alternatively, declaring the new law unconstitutional. Special Term severed its motion from the 1973 proceeding, treated it as a separate article 78 proceeding, and directed the Attorney-General to review the offering plan in accordance with the law in effect on November 26, 1973. Its judgment is modified, by deleting the reference to a severance and treatment of the motion as a separate article 78 proceeding, and, as so modified, is affirmed.

2. Dispositive of the merits is the rule that a negligent or a willful delay by a governmental agency, which prevents the issuance of a license until after the amendment of the governing statute, will afford grounds for applying the pre-existing law. And nothing in the legislative history of chapter 1021 of the Laws of 1974 suggests retroactive application either to filings pending in the office of the Attorney-General or to plans that had not yet become effective though already accepted for filing prior to the statute's effective date. Petitioner's attempt to update its plan and prospectus does not warrant a conclusion that a fresh [***5] filing was engaged in.

3. No jurisdictional basis existed for treating petitioner's motion as an independent application for relief. The direction sought by it to the effect that the Attorney-General apply the law in existence on November 26, 1973 is fully compatible with and implemental of the prior judgment.

 

1331 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bruno v. Bruno


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


51 A.D.2d 862; 380 N.Y.S.2d 165; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11477



February 20, 1976 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband appealed from so much of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Onondaga County (New York), in an action by plaintiff wife to set aside a separation agreement, as directed him to pay alimony to the wife in a certain sum and that ordered him to pay all of the medical, dental and hospital expenses of the wife.


OVERVIEW: The parties executed a separation agreement that contained a non-merger clause regarding any future divorce suit, and provided that the wife would not seek or accept any judgment or decree granting her alimony. The wife withdrew her action at trial and the trial court entered judgment in favor of the husband on a conversion no fault basis under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(6). The wife filed a motion to set aside the separation agreement. Both parties testified as to their respective financial resources and expenses on the issue of alimony. The husband urged the validity of the separation agreement and the enforceability of the waiver of alimony. The court held that the agreement properly supplied grounds for divorce under § 170(6) because such agreements survived for that purpose and alimony could have properly been established. However, the court held that the award of alimony was excessive as it left the husband with resources that were barely sufficient to meet his current living costs. The court reduced the alimony but did not disturb the order that the husband pay the medical, dental and hospital expenses of the wife.


OUTCOME: The court modified a judgment that awarded alimony from the husband to the wife and that ordered the husband to pay for the medical, dental, and hospital expenses of the wife by reducing the amount of alimony, and as modified, affirmed the judgment.

Guarrera v. Lee Mem. Hosp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department

February 20, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff estate administrator appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York) that, in a medical malpractice action, granted summary judgment to defendants, doctor and hospital.


OVERVIEW: The estate administrator filed a medical malpractice action against defendants. However, the hospital was a public corporation, and the estate administrator did not give a 90-day notice to the city as required by N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of defendants on the grounds that the estate administrator failed to comply with N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law §§ 50-d, 50-e, 50-i. The estate administrator urged that N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 50-e was violative of constitutional equal protection guarantees. The court held that the statutory purpose of § 50-e to allow cities an opportunity to investigate while information was readily available, was valid. The court found no merit in the contention of the estate administrator that defendants were estopped from asserting the provisions of § 50-e, as the present position of the estate administrator was attributable to inadequate investigation, for which the hospital should not have been precluded from asserting the statutory bar.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order that, in a medical malpractice action filed by the estate administrator, granted summary judgment to defendants.

1334 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bott v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


51 A.D.2d 81; 379 N.Y.S.2d 172; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10668



February 5, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Determination modified by annulling so much thereof as sustains charges I and II, and by reducing the penalty imposed to a suspension of three months without pay, and, as so modified, confirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher appealed a determination of respondent Deposit School District Board of Education (board) (New York), which dismissed the teacher from employment following a suspension for alleged physical abuse of school children, failure to adhere to school regulations or to obey specific instructions given by superiors, and a complete lack of professionalism in his conduct as a teacher.


OVERVIEW: The teacher was suspended from his job without pay for the alleged physical abuse of children, his failure to adhere to school regulations or to obey specific instructions given by superiors, and a complete lack of professionalism in his conduct as a teacher. The board, appointed pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 3020-a, then dismissed the teacher and ordered that the dismissal was retroactive to the date of suspension. The teacher appealed, arguing that § 3020-a was unconstitutional, that the decision of the board was not supported by substantial evidence, that the suspension without pay was illegal, and that the penalty imposed was excessive. The court modified the board's determination by annulling the findings of child abuse and the failure to adhere to school regulations and by reducing the penalty imposed. Substantial evidence did not exist to support the charges of child abuse and failure to adhere to regulations, but substantial evidence existed to support the finding of the lack of professionalism on the part of the teacher. The penalty was excessive, as § 3020-a did not authorize the withholding of pay during a period of suspension.


OUTCOME: The court modified the board's determination dismissing the teacher from his job by annulling the findings that the teacher abused students and failed to adhere to school regulations. The court further reduced the penalty imposed on the teacher to a suspension of three months without pay.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- teachers -- dismissal of tenured teacher -- determination of school board, which dismissed tenured teacher charged with abuse of school children, failure to adhere to school regulations or to obey specific instructions given by superiors, and incompetence and lack of professionalism in his conduct as teacher, is modified, by annulling so much thereof as sustained first two charges and by reducing penalty to suspension for three months without pay -- in light of all surrounding circumstances, and particularly relatively minor transgressions specified in third charge, dismissal  [***2] would be unfair and is unjustified -- section 3020-a of Education Law is constitutional -- it does not authorize withholding of pay during period of suspension, and teacher was entitled to be paid until date of dismissal -- ceasing his compensation as of earlier date and making dismissal retroactive to date he was originally suspended was, in effect, same as dismissal plus fine, which sanctions cannot be imposed together -- no showing was made that board's decision was not made solely on record adduced before hearing panel.

1. Following hearings conducted before a panel appointed pursuant to section 3020-a of the Education Law, a school board dismissed a tenured teacher charged with abuse of school children, failure to adhere to school regulations or to obey specific instructions given by superiors, and incompetence and lack of professionalism in his conduct as a teacher. The board's determination is modified, by annulling so much thereof as sustained the first two charges and by reducing the penalty imposed to suspension for three months without pay. As to the first charge, excessive use of force could be an independent charge as to an allegation of a crime, but the record is [***3] lacking in substantial evidence that the teacher committed such a crime. The second charge refers to his alleged use of physical force in disciplining his students and his keeping children after school, but, even assuming he did so, there is no evidence that the board ever adopted the regulations allegedly violated thereby or that any valid directives containing specific instructions were not obeyed by him. The conduct complained of in the third charge, such as his alleged lack of planning and failure to handle minor disciplinary matters by himself, does appear to fit within the definitions of causes for removal set forth in section 3012 of the Education Law, and the board's findings relative thereto are supported by substantial evidence. However, in the light of all the surrounding circumstances, and particularly the relatively minor transgressions specified in the third charge, the dismissal of the teacher would be unfair and is unjustified.

2. Section 3020-a of the Education Law, defects in which were cured by the promulgation of regulations by the Commissioner of Education, is constitutional.

3. Section 3020-a does not authorize the withholding of pay during a period of suspension,  [***4] and the teacher was plainly entitled to be paid until the date of his dismissal, but, instead, his compensation ceased as of an earlier date and his dismissal was made retroactive to the date he was originally suspended. Such a result is, in effect, the same as a dismissal plus a fine, which sanctions cannot be imposed together.

4. The teacher also contends that the board's decision was not made solely on the record adduced before the hearing panel. However, although two board members were present in the principal's office when the board's attorney talked with students about the complaints against the teacher, neither member questioned any of the children, and the board's decision states that it made its determination based solely upon the evidence adduced on the record. No showing was made that this was not the case.

People v. De Jesus


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


51 A.D.2d 635; 378 N.Y.S.2d 819; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10974



January 29, 1976

1336 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Glass v. Thompson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


51 A.D.2d 69; 379 N.Y.S.2d 427; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10665



January 26, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Kings County, entered November 23, 1973, affirmed, insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs tenants sought review of the decision of the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Kings County (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants to the extent of declaring that the N.Y. City Civ. Ct. Act § 110(e) was constitutional on its face in a summary proceeding against plaintiffs seeking to dispossess plaintiffs from their apartments.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contented that the appointment of hearing officers to hear and determine summary proceedings pursuant to N.Y. City Civ. Ct. Act § 110(e) violated N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 15, that provided that Civil Court of the City of New York had jurisdiction over summary proceedings to recover possession of real property. The court noted that under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4317(b) compulsory reference to a referee could be ordered by the court only where trial required examination of a long account, including actions to foreclose mechanic's liens, or to determine an issue of damages separately triable and not requiring a trial by jury. The court stated that no state constitutional provisions curtailed the power of the legislature to provide for compulsory reference except that compulsory references could not violate rights guaranteed to the parties by the constitution, such as a right to a trial by jury where the right existed. Therefore the court held that because the parties waived a trial by jury, there was no impediment to a compulsory reference to a hearing officer to hear and determine the issues, even if all of the parties to the litigation objected to the reference.


OUTCOME: The order of the supreme court was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- summary proceedings -- subdivision (e) of section 110 of New York City Civil Court Act, which authorizes appointment of hearing officers to hear and determine actions and proceedings in Housing Part of Civil Court, is constitutional and may be utilized, even in absence of consent of parties, except where trial by jury has been properly demanded by either side -- it does not violate section 15 of article VI of State Constitution, which creates Civil Court and specifies method by which its Judges are to be selected, but does not prohibit references -- hearing officers authorized by section  [***2] 110 are in essence referees, for referees are only nonjudicial officers who exercise judicial functions -- Legislature may provide for compulsory reference in certain types of cases, and there is no constitutional limitation upon its power in this regard except that compulsory references may not violate constitutionally guaranteed rights, such as right to trial by jury -- CPLR article 43, which governs references in Supreme Court, applies in Civil Court only where procedure in that court is not otherwise regulated by provisions of New York City Civil Court Act.

1. Subdivision (e) of section 110 of the New York City Civil Court Act, which authorizes the appointment of hearing officers to hear and determine actions and proceedings in the Housing Part of the Civil Court, is constitutional and may be utilized, even in the absence of the consent of the parties, except where a trial by jury has been properly demanded by either side.

2. The appointment of hearing officers to hear and determine summary proceedings does not violate section 15 of article VI of the New York State Constitution. That section creates the Civil Court and specifies the method by which its Judges are to be selected,  [***3] but it does not prohibit references.

3. The hearing officers authorized by section 110 are in essence referees, for referees are the only nonjudicial officers who exercise judicial functions. It is well established that the Legislature may, by statute, provide for compulsory references in certain types of cases, and there is no constitutional limitation upon its power in this regard except that compulsory references may not violate constitutionally guaranteed rights, such as the right to a trial by jury. Where the parties have waived a jury trial, there is no impediment to a compulsory reference to a hearing officer to hear and determine the issues, even if all the parties to the litigation object.

4. CPLR article 43, which governs references in the Supreme Court, applies in the Civil Court only in those instances where procedure in that court is not otherwise regulated by provisions of the New York City Civil Court Act. The Legislature was fully empowered to designate a class of proceedings to be tried before a referee to be known as a hearing officer, under procedures different in scope from those previously recognized and codified in CPLR article 43.

 

1337 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Levine v. Whalen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


50 A.D.2d 503; 378 N.Y.S.2d 800; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10653



January 22, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Determination annulled, with costs to petitioner, judgment directed to be entered declaring section 2800 of the Public Health Law unconstitutional, and matter remitted for further proceedings not inconsistent herewith. Motion denied as academic, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a hearing, respondent Commissioner of Health (commissioner) revoked the operating license of petitioner convalescent home (home). The home filed a proceeding in the trial court (New York) under N.Y.C.P.L.R. 78, challenging the constitutionality of article 28 of the Public Health Code.


OVERVIEW: For 23 years the nursing home operated under state license. However, the state revoked the license due primarily to alleged structural defects relating to fire safety. N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2800 empowered the department to develop and administer the state's policies towards hospitals and related services. But the state legislature set no particular standards for the department to follow. The court held that § 2800 was unconstitutional because it attempted to delegate to the department authority to determine standards and policies for the relevant services. Thus, the legislature unconstitutionally delegated its authority. Moreover, even if the department had proper authority to develop its own regulations to interpret and apply § 2800, its regulations were arbitrary. The regulations did not inform a party what was expected. For example, the regulations allowed modification by the department of any and all the requirements found in the regulations. The regulations were promulgated, interpreted, and applied by the same department that acted as prosecutor, judge, and jury.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the determination and declared the pertinent section of the New York Public Health Law unconstitutional. The court remitted the matter.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals and asylums -- nursing homes -- section 2800 of Public Health Law, which purports to empower Department of Health with responsibility to develop and administer policy of State with respect to hospital and related services, in order "to provide for the protection and promotion of the health of the inhabitants of the state", is unconstitutional -- determination of Commissioner of Health revoking hospital operating certificate held by operator of nursing home, for violation of regulations relating to construction standards, which were adopted by virtue of authority assumed under  [***2] that section, is annulled -- even assuming he could validly prescribe construction standards for nursing homes, regulations which were adopted are so vague, confusing and meaningless as to be arbitrary and unreasonable.

1. Section 2800 of the Public Health Law, which purports to empower the Department of Health with the responsibility to develop and then administer the policy of this State with respect to hospital and related services, in order "to provide for the protection and promotion of the health of the inhabitants of the state", and does not set any standard, outline any policy, or announce any intelligible principle by which the department's exercise of discretion in this field may be measured, is unconstitutional. A determination of the Commissioner of Health revoking a hospital operating certificate held by an operator of a nursing home, for violation of regulations relating to construction standards, which were adopted by virtue of authority assumed under that section, is annulled.

2. Assuming, arguendo, that the Commissioner of Health could validly prescribe construction standards for nursing homes under article 28 of the Public Health Law, the regulations which [***3] were adopted are so vague, confusing and meaningless as to be arbitrary and unreasonable.

 

1338 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rubinstein v. French Hospital


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


51 A.D.2d 563; 378 N.Y.S.2d 457; 1976 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10826



January 19, 1976 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with $ 50 costs and disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a medical malpractice action, defendant hospital appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss plaintiff patient's complaint.


OVERVIEW: The patient alleged that she was blinded after birth due to the hospital's negligence. The hospital contended that the patient's action, which was commenced when she was 22 years of age, was barred by the statute of limitations because the patient's disability of infancy had terminated when she attained the age of 18 years and, thus, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 208, her action for medical malpractice had to be commenced within three years after her 18th birthday. The court held that the patient had timely commenced her action because she was entitled to rely upon the original version of C.P.L.R. 208 and, thus, to invoke the disability of infancy until she was 21 years of age. The court found that because the patient had already attained the age of 21 years when the amendment to C.P.L.R. 208 became effective, the result of adopting the hospital's theory would be analogous to the unconstitutional act of shortening a statute of limitations without affording an aggrieved plaintiff a reasonable opportunity to commence suit and would create the anomalous result of requiring the patient to have commenced her action prior to the enactment of the amendments in question.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the hospital's motion to dismiss.

 

1339 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


West 22nd St. Assoc. v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


144 Misc. 2d 292; 547 N.Y.S.2d 517; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 697



September 11, 1989 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Minimum Period of Required Residency of Family Member of Deceased Rent-Stabilized Tenant

Rent Stabilization Code (9 NYCRR) § 2523.5(b) (2), which provides that if a tenant is deceased at the expiration of the lease term, such tenant's family member who has not resided in the housing accommodation since the inception of the tenancy or the commencement of the relationship, but who has been residing with such tenant in the housing accommodation as a primary resident for a period of no less than two years immediately prior to the death of the tenant, shall be entitled to a renewal lease, does not violate the Due Process or Equal Protection Clauses of the State Constitution. The regulation meets the test of rationality in that the time frame chosen is reasonably related to the goal of limiting succession rights to those family members who can demonstrate that they lived with the tenant of record for at least some minimum period of measurable duration. The fact that there may conceivably be a disproportionate impact upon surviving family members who moved in with the tenant during the tenant's final illness (whatever that illness might be) does not in and of itself make out invidious discrimination forbidden by the Equal Protection Clause, nor does it raise an issue of independent constitutional significance. Thus, the regulation is not unconstitutional as written or as applied to respondents, the mother and sister of the deceased rent-stabilized tenant, who moved into the subject premises to assist the tenant, who was suffering from AIDS, and lived with him for approximately 18 months until his death.

People v. Dieudonne


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


143 Misc. 2d 559; 544 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 512



March 31, 1989 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Sexual Misconduct

The sexual misconduct statute (Penal Law § 130.20), which applies only to males, is violative of equal protection; the People have failed to demonstrate that this gender-based law is related to an important governmental objective. However, the proper remedy here is to extend the coverage of the statute to those formerly excluded, rather than declare the statute void and, therefore, judgments convicting four male defendants, upon their pleas of guilty, of attempted sexual misconduct are affirmed. The statute does not deny defendants due process of law.

 

1341 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


East Four-Forty Assocs. v. Ewell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


138 Misc. 2d 235; 527 N.Y.S.2d 204; 1988 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 299



February 18, 1988 


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Renewal Lease

1. In a holdover summary proceeding to recover possession of the subject premises from respondent, the son of the deceased tenant of record, respondent's affirmative defense that petitioner's failure to properly offer his mother a renewal lease entitled him to remain in possession after the January 31, 1985 expiration of her last renewal lease and after her death is no longer viable since, even deeming such renewal lease to have been executed by her, it would have expired on January 31, 1987.

Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Claim of Harassment by Tenant

2. In a holdover summary proceeding to recover possession of the subject premises from respondent, the son of the deceased tenant of record, respondent's affirmative defense and counterclaim, based upon a claim of harassment, must fail; a bona fide dispute [***2] exists as to respondent's entitlement to remain in possession of the premises after the death and expiration of the last renewal lease of the tenant of record.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Validity of Rent Stabilization Code Provision for Succession to Premises by Family of Deceased or Vacating Tenant of Record

3. The provisions of the new Rent Stabilization Code (eff May 1, 1987) which authorize succession to rent-stabilized premises by family members in case of death or vacatur of the tenant of record (Rent Stabilization Code [9 NYCRR] § 2523.5 [b] [1], [2]) are invalid. There is a patent inconsistency between the Rent Stabilization Law (Administrative Code of City of New York § 26-501 et seq.), which forecloses succession rights, and the succession provisions of the new code. Further, inasmuch as the succession provisions of the new code impair and diminish the existing rights of owners by requiring them to offer renewal leases to persons other than tenants of record where previously landlords were under no obligation to do so, these provisions violate the proscription against impairment or diminishment of existing rights contained in the Rent Stabilization [***3] Law (Administrative Code § 26-511 [b]); thus, the succession provisions exceed the limited scope of the authority granted by the Legislature to the Commissioner of the Division of Housing and Community Renewal to amend the code. Further, the succession provisions of the new code constitute a breach of the separation of powers doctrine in that they represent an unconstitutional intrusion into the exclusive domain of the Legislature by an administrative agency.

Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Attorney's Fees

4. In a holdover summary proceeding, petitioner landlord's cause of action for attorney's fees is dismissed inasmuch as respondent, the son of the deceased tenant of record, is not a signatory to the lease for the subject premises.

People v. Clark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


135 Misc. 2d 22; 515 N.Y.S.2d 382; 1987 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2171



March 11, 1987 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, since, unlike those more narrowly circumscribed loitering statutes which have been held to be constitutional in the wake of the Papachristou line of decisions, the instant statute neither delineates a definable act, in addition to loitering, which is to be observed nor delineates an area of a sufficiently restricted public access to sustain a proscription of mere loitering, 5 and since the "satisfactory explanation" provision impermissibly places unfettered discretion in the hands of law enforcement officers, we find that the instant statute is unconstitutional and that defendant's motion to dismiss the loitering charge on that ground should have been granted.



5 Even were we to assume that some facilities might properly be characterized as exclusively devoted to "transportation" and further assume that those facilities would otherwise qualify as places of sufficiently restricted public access to permit enforcement of a loitering statute on those premises, it is perhaps unrealistic to expect the public to readily distinguish between such specialized transportation facilities and mixed use "transportation facilities".

 [***2]


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Loitering Statute

Penal Law § 240.35 (7), which provides that a person is guilty of loitering when he "[loiters] or remains in any transportation facility, or is found sleeping therein, and is unable to give a satisfactory explanation of his presence", is unconstitutional. The statute neither delineates a definable act, in addition to loitering, which is to be observed, nor delineates an area of sufficiently restricted public access to sustain a proscription of mere loitering; further, the "satisfactory explanation" provision impermissibly places unfettered discretion in the hands of law enforcement officers.

1345 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lebron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


130 Misc. 2d 831; 501 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1986 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2436



March 4, 1986 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Statute Proscribing Operation of Motor Vehicle by Person with .10% or Greater Blood-Alcohol Level

 Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (2), which provides that no person shall operate a motor vehicle while he has .10 of one per centum or more by weight of alcohol in his blood as shown by chemical analysis, is not unconstitutionally vague nor violative of due process. The statute is not vague since it establishes an empirical standard which may be readily ascertained by the use of reliable testing procedures; further, due process does not require that a defendant, in order to conform his conduct to the statute, have the ability to identify his exact blood-alcohol level at the time he undertakes to operate a vehicle.

 

1346 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Montero


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


124 Misc. 2d 1020; 480 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3295



May 11, 1984 


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Inability to Pay Fine

1. When a defendant moves for resentencing upon a claimed inability to pay fines imposed, the court must hold an evidentiary hearing to ascertain the validity of the claim, and if it is found that defendant is unable to pay the fine despite good-faith efforts to do so, the court may not automatically convert the fine into a prison sentence but must first consider alternative measures of punishment other than imprisonment. Accordingly, judgments imposing concurrent jail sentences upon defendant for failure to pay fines imposed upon separate convictions are reversed where defendant was rearrested for failure to pay said fines, defense counsel requested that defendant be resentenced because he was unable to pay the fines due to indigency, and the court, without inquiry, sentenced defendant to said concurrent terms of imprisonment.

 [***2] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Imprisonment for Failure to Pay Fine

2. Before an indigent defendant who fails to pay a fine may be resentenced to imprisonment as punishment for the crime, all other available alternative dispositions must be considered and rejected by the sentencing court as insufficient to satisfy the penological interests of the State. Accordingly, CPL 420.10 (subd 4), which permits incarceration of a defendant who is financially unable to pay a fine, is not unconstitutional since it permits the court to consider all available sentencing alternatives and does not mandate imprisonment.

Smith v. City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Termination of Employment -- Entitlement to Cash Value of Accrued Vacation Time

 Section 92 of the General Municipal Law permits but does not require cash payments for unused accrued vacation or overtime and, where a municipality has nevertheless provided for such cash payments it may restrict the amount of those payments without running afoul of due process requirements; accordingly, where plaintiff was terminated from her noncompetitive civil service position without fault on her part, after three and one-half years' service, and had to her credit 153 days of unused vacation and accumulated overtime, her recovery is restricted to the cash value of 54 days of such unused time pursuant to mayoral executive orders providing for lump-sum cash payments to employees terminated without fault, with less than 10 years of service for unused annual leave and overtime credits up to a total of 54 days; where there is a mayoral [***2] order permitting payment, plaintiff is bound by its terms.

Dep't of Hous. Pres. & Dev. v. Debona


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


122 Misc. 2d 649; 473 N.Y.S.2d 298; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4145



August 12, 1983 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Notice of Administrative Penalties

Subdivision (k) of section D26-51.01 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York which provides for the administrative imposition of fines for a landlord's failure to comply with established heat and hot water minimum temperature standards to commence upon the posting of a notice of violation in a conspicuous place on the premises is unconstitutional as applied to respondent landlord. Reasonable notice must be given wherever possible to provide a landlord opportunity to abate a nuisance not created by him or of which he may not have and could not have actual notice by reason of control or operation, in a manner causing the least disruption to the premises and minimizing the costs which he must ultimately bear. The ordinance in question did not accomplish its avowed purpose to correct hazardous and health-impairing conditions as soon as possible [***2] where the condition complained of was not created by the landlord, neither the landlord nor his agent was notified of the complaint by the tenant and there was no reasonable way for the landlord to have known of such condition, and said condition did not come to the attention of the landlord until the proceeding was commenced against him.

People v. O.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

February 10, 1982 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

The term "arrest" as used in section 1194 (subd 1, par [1]) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which authorizes administration of a breathalyzer test within two hours of arrest, is not unconstitutionally vague on the ground that it might refer to either an "on-the-scene" arrest or an arrest occurring sometime thereafter since the term as used in the statute is sufficiently broad and definite so as to properly include an arrest occurring in either of those situations. Accordingly, the administration of a breathalyzer test to defendant within two hours of his arrest in the hospital emergency room to which he had been taken following a motor vehicle accident was proper even though the test was not administered within two hours of the moment when he was removed from the scene of the accident.

Crimes -- Assault -- Striking Victim in Eye with Egg

Defendant was lawfully convicted of assault in the third degree for recklessly causing "physical injury" (Penal Law, § 120.00, subd 2) to complainant, the Mayor of the City of New York, by striking him in the right eye with an egg, "physical injury" being defined as "impairment of physical condition or substantial pain" (Penal Law, § 10.00, subd 9), since the proof of impairment was evidenced not only by the victim's testimony of blurred vision but by medical testimony and hospital records establishing specific traumatic aberrations of the eye; the assault statute is not unconstitutionally vague since it gives "a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct is forbidden" and defendant was certainly aware that his conduct was proscribed by law; the presence of additional security forces in the courtroom were [***2] indicated in this case and did not deprive defendant of a fair trial.

 

1352 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


107 Misc. 2d 325; 433 N.Y.S.2d 973; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2885



October 30, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Theft of Services -- Constitutionality of Permissive Presumption

Since the constitutional application of a permissive presumption is to be evaluated upon the basis of an entire record, in the absence of a factual record, a constitutional challenge to the permissive evidentiary presumption contained in the theft of electrical services statute (Penal Law, § 165.15, subd 6) is premature, the trial court having improperly found the statute to be unconstitutional because it was not possible to be certain beyond a reasonable doubt that a person receiving the benefit of electrical service through a tampered meter had caused the tampering; in most cases, the "rational connection" standard is the test for determining the validity of permissive presumptions and it is only when the prosecution relies entirely upon the presumption to establish proof of the crime charged or an essential element thereof that it must be [***2] demonstrated that the basic fact proved supports the presumed fact beyond a reasonable doubt; it cannot be said, as a matter of law, that it may never be presumed beyond a reasonable doubt that a person who has received electricity through a tampered or altered meter received that benefit knowingly.

People v. Stefanik


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


103 Misc. 2d 539; 430 N.Y.S.2d 190; 1980 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2173



May 19, 1980 


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Timeliness -- Late Appeal

1. Defendant is time barred from pursuing an appeal from his October, 1976 conviction for violating New York City's Noise Code (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 1403.3-3.01) for playing his music too loudly since his notice of appeal was filed in February, 1978, well past the 30-day limit for taking appeals ( CPL 460.10, subd 1, par [a]) nor is he entitled to file a late appeal since an application for a late appeal must be made within one year from the 30-day period otherwise provided for the taking of an appeal ( CPL 460.30, subd 1) and no trespass beyond this time limit is permitted.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Conditional Discharge Revocation Proceeding

2. 1354 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Torres


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


100 Misc. 2d 138; 420 N.Y.S.2d 843; 1979 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2427



July 13, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- Mercantile License -- Continuous Course of Dealing

Generally, in New York, whether one is engaged in a particular business or profession for the purpose of a statute requiring a license therefor depends upon the extent of the services offered or rendered and the carrying on of a business within the meaning of a statute requiring a license therefor implies continuity of conduct and a continuous course of dealing rather than an isolated transaction; accordingly, since defendant, an employee of a safe company which did not have a place of business in the City of Long Beach, merely serviced an alarm system for one store within the territorial limits of the city and such limited contacts within the city cannot be deemed continuous, he cannot be guilty of violating a city ordinance requiring any person conducting a business to first obtain a mercantile license.
