People v. Cruz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


99 Misc. 2d 634; 420 N.Y.S.2d 531; 1979 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2290



July 6, 1979 


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Driving While under Influence of Alcohol -- "Vagueness" Challenge to Statute

 Section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which prohibits driving in either an "impaired" or an "intoxicated" condition, driving while impaired being a violation (subd 1) while driving while intoxicated a crime (subd 3), is unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who refused to submit to a chemical blood test and was only charged in the information with violating an unspecified subdivision of section 1192, since due process requires that a criminal statute be sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct is forbidden by the statute and provide explicit standards so that resolution of individual cases is not on an ad hoc, subjective basis and section 1192 does not define either "impaired"  [***2] or "intoxicated" thus leaving the citizenry and the courts to guess at its meaning and differ as to its application; "vagueness" challenges to statutes which do not involve First Amendment freedoms must be examined in the light of the facts of the case at hand and since, in the absence of chemical test results, the information does not establish that defendant was either obviously drunk or whether his driving ability was merely slightly diminished, the statute provides no ascertainable standards for a trial court to apply to defendant's acts and is thus unconstitutionally vague under these facts.

 

1356 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Denning


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


98 Misc. 2d 369; 413 N.Y.S.2d 837; 1979 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2083



February 2, 1979 


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Mandatory Youthful Offender -- Denial of Jury Trial

1. The mandatory youthful offender statute ( CPL 340.40, subd 7), which mandates that an eligible youth ( CPL 720.10, subd 1) who has not previously been convicted of a crime or adjudicated a youthful offender, be tried by a single-Judge trial with the maximum period of incarceration for such mandatorily adjudicated youthful offenders limited to six months (Penal Law, § 60.02, subd [b]), is constitutional, being in compliance with the standards for denying a jury trial promulgated by the Supreme Court which held that a "serious" crime for which a jury trial is guaranteed, is a crime whereby conviction may bring an authorized maximum penalty of greater than six months' imprisonment (see Baldwin v New York, 399 U.S. 66) and the fact that a mandatory youthful offender may be subject to an additional period of probation beyond the six months' incarceration [***2] does not render the statute unconstitutional since the distinction between "serious" offenses which warrant a jury trial and "petty" offenses which do not, is based solely upon the consideration of the potential maximum term of imprisonment and not upon any consideration of probation time.

Crimes -- Mandatory Youthful Offender -- Denial of Jury Trial

2. CPL 340.40 (subd 7), which denies a jury trial to mandatorily adjudicated youthful offenders whose maximum terms of imprisonment are limited to six months (Penal Law, § 60.02, subd [b]), does not deny equal protection to such mandatory youthful offenders since only those persons charged with "serious" offenses having an authorized maximum penalty in excess of six months' incarceration are entitled to a jury trial.

 

1357 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. London


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


98 Misc. 2d 298; 413 N.Y.S.2d 824; 1978 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2878



December 6, 1978 


HEADNOTES

Trial -- Nonjury Trial upon Traffic Information -- Right to Deliver Summation

Although a court must permit defense counsel to deliver a summation in a nonjury trial upon an indictment ( CPL 320.20, subd 3, par [c]), a court sitting without a jury still possesses the discretion to allow or disallow summations in a trial upon a simplified traffic information ( CPL 350.10, subd 3, par [c]) since traffic or petty offense cases which do not entail a "real likelihood" or a "practical possibility" of incarceration may not require adherence to exacting standards designed to fully safeguard a defendant's constitutional rights as do those cases in which a sentence of incarceration may well be imposed. In view of the availability of statutory remedies to the defendant in the event of her inability to pay the $ 35 fine imposed for speeding ( CPL 420.10, subd 4), the sentence imposed did not entail a "real likelihood" of imprisonment and she was therefore not [***2] denied the right to assistance of counsel by the failure of the court to allow her attorney to deliver a summation or make closing arguments.

 

1358 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reid


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


95 Misc. 2d 822; 408 N.Y.S.2d 170; 1978 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2513



May 12, 1978 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Criminal Statute

1. A strong presumption of validity attaches to a statute and the burden of proving invalidity is upon those who challenge the statute to prove such invalidity beyond a reasonable doubt. A criminal statute need only describe the proscribed conduct with a reasonable degree of certainty, such that the statute convey to one of ordinary experience and understanding, an understanding of the acts prohibited.

Crimes -- Fraudulent Accosting

2. Section 165.30 of the Penal Law which proscribes accosting "a person in a public place with the intent to defraud him of money or other property by means of a trick, swindle, or confidence game", is constitutional since the crime of fraudulent accosting, nebulous by its very nature, is sufficiently defined so as to give a reasonable man, of average intelligence, sufficient notice [***2] of what is proscribed thereby and need not delineate all statements and actions which might be calculated to swindle a member of the public.

 

1359 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mobil Oil Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


96 Misc. 2d 524; 409 N.Y.S.2d 329; 1978 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2634



May 9, 1978 


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Regulation of Commercial Speech

1. Section 3 of Westchester County Local Law No. 9 of the Local Laws of 1974, which prohibits the posting of any sign or placard stating or referring directly or indirectly to the price or prices of gasoline or diesel motor fuel, other than those signs or placards required to be posted on the pumps, at, near or about the premises in which gasoline or diesel motor fuel is sold, is unconstitutional since it seeks to regulate commercial speech, which is protected under the First and Fourteenth Amendments, without effectuating a significant government interest. Since commercial speech is constitutionally protected, a State can only regulate the time, place and manner of commercial speech, not its content, provided that the regulations serve a significant governmental interest and leave open ample alternative channels for communication of the information. Accordingly, defendant oil company cannot be convicted under the statute for placing a sign stating "Check Our Low Low Prices" on the premises since the statute is violative of defendant's First Amendment right of [***3] free speech.

Constitutional Law -- Regulation of Commercial Speech

2. Section 3 of Westchester County Local Law No. 9 of the Local Laws of 1974, which violates the First Amendment right to commercial speech by prohibiting the posting of any sign or placard stating or referring directly or indirectly to the price or prices of gasoline or diesel motor fuel, other than those signs or placards required to be posted on the pumps, at, near or about the premises in which gasoline or diesel motor fuel is sold, cannot be sustained upon the theory that the prohibited signs are misleading or could be deceptive since, where a matter advertised is easily susceptible to measurement or verification, it cannot be deemed to be so likely to mislead or deceive as to warrant its prohibition. Accordingly, since, in the case of gasoline prices, the allegation of "low low low prices" contained in signs placed on the premises of a gasoline service station by defendant oil company can easily be verified at the pump where the price is posted, the banning of such signs would be an infringement upon defendant's First Amendment rights to commercial speech.

Constitutional Law -- Regulation  [***4] of Commercial Speech

3. Section 3 of Westchester County Local Law No. 9 of the Local Laws of 1974, which prohibits the posting of any sign or placard stating or referring directly or indirectly to the price or prices of gasoline or diesel motor fuel, other than those signs or placards required to be posted on the pumps, at, near or about the premises in which the gasoline or diesel motor fuel is sold, is violative of First Amendment rights to commercial speech for not providing a realistic alternative for the owners of gasoline service stations to reach persons not deliberately seeking information thereby unconstitutionally prohibiting all effective means of advertising. The posting of the smaller signs on the pumps as required by the local law is not as effective a media for communicating the message of "low prices" as a large sign in front of the premises.

Constitutional Law -- Regulation of Commercial Speech

4. Since the overbreath principle in First Amendment cases does not apply to commercial speech, which is otherwise constitutionally protected, defendant oil company would have no standing to challenge the constitutionality of its conviction for placing a sign [***5] stating "Check Our Low Low Prices" on the premises of a gasoline service station in violation of section 3 of Westchester County Local Law No. 9 of the Local Laws of 1974, which prohibits the posting of any sign or placard stating or referring directly or indirectly to the price or prices of gasoline or diesel motor fuel, other than those signs or placards required to be posted on the pumps, at, near or about the premises in which the gasoline or diesel motor fuel is sold, if the local law was written in such a manner so as to be validly applied to defendant although unconstitutional to others, however, inasmuch as the local law bans not only those signs which are deceptive or misleading but, totally bans all signs other than prices at the pump, regardless of intent, the local law cannot be constitutionally applied to defendant and thus defendant may therefore, properly challenge its constitutionality.

 

1360 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


92 Misc. 2d 992; 402 N.Y.S.2d 273; 1977 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2628



October 21, 1977 


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Improper Exclusion of Circumstantial Evidence of Hostility of Arresting Officers

1. Defendant's convictions for possession of gambling records and driving while impaired are reversed since [***2] it was improper for the trial court to wholly exclude the evidence relating to defendant's civilian complaint against a fellow police officer of the arresting officers. Such evidence should have been allowed in attempted impeachment of the policemen's testimony. While the extent to which an examination may go for the purpose of proving the hostility of a witness is within the discretion of the court, the court has no discretionary power to rule out all the evidence which is offered for this purpose.

Crimes -- Refusal to Submit to Chemical Blood Test

2. The introduction into evidence of a defendant's refusal to submit to a chemical test to determine the alcoholic content of his blood as authorized by statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1194, subd 4), violates such defendant's Fifth Amendment privilege against self incrimination. While the results of a blood test are not inadmissible on privilege grounds since the privilege is a bar only against compelled "communications" or "testimony" and does not protect against compulsion which makes a suspect or accused the source of "real or physical evidence", any statements incident to the compelled chemical test would constitute [***3] a testimonial product which falls within the privilege. Admission of the defendant's testimonial statement of refusal carries a strong circumstantial inference of guilt at trial and constitutes a violation of a defendant's constitutional privilege from being compelled to provide the State with incriminating evidence of a testimonial nature.

Crimes -- Denial of Preliminary Hearing to Person Charged with Misdemeanor Gambling Offense

3. While there may be some doubt that the statute excluding persons accused of gambling misdemeanors from the opportunity to request a preliminary hearing ( CPL 170.75) bears some rational relationship to a legitimate governmental purpose, the failure to afford a preliminary hearing to a defendant charged with such gambling offense does not in any event mandate reversal of his conviction since a conviction after trial will not be vacated on the ground that the defendant was detained pending trial without a determination of probable cause.

 

1361 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


89 Misc. 2d 754; 393 N.Y.S.2d 239; 1977 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1974



February 17, 1977 



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed, and the motion to dismiss denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering for purpose of engaging in prostitution offense.

 Section 240.37 of the Penal Law, which prohibits a person from loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution offense, is not unconstitutionally vague, overbroad and does not unlawfully inhibit freedom of speech. The language used in the statute is sufficiently plain as to enable persons of ordinary intelligence to understand precisely what acts are proscribed. Section 240.37 does not improperly make guilt or innocence of the offense depend on the subjective conclusions of the arresting officer. The statute prohibits the specified objective acts of repeatedly beckoning, stopping and interfering "with the free passage of other persons, for [***2] the purpose of prostitution", which are all capable of determination by the trier of facts. The absence of specific guidelines describing the means by which an officer determines whether probable cause exists to make an arrest does not render section 240.37 overbroad. A criminal statute need not specify the precise observations necessary to justify an arrest in order to pass constitutional muster. The fact that prosecution under section 240.37 will involve only circumstantial evidence does not make a conviction under the statute impossible. Since section 240.37 prohibits only such communication which is "for the purpose" of criminal activity, it does not infringe upon First Amendment rights.

 

1362 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


89 Misc. 2d 789; 392 N.Y.S.2d 968; 1977 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1943



February 16, 1977 


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- aggravated harassment -- repeated phone calls made to police department.

Defendant, who made 27 telephone calls over a 3-hour and 20-minute period to the White Plains Police Department regarding a complaint which he had been told was civil rather than criminal in nature, was lawfully convicted of aggravated harassment (Penal Law, § 240.30). Defendant was repeatedly told not to call again because he was tying up the police lines. Defendant was not convicted for engaging in protected First Amendment activity. Although defendant initially had a "purpose of legitimate communication", his later calls were intended solely to harass, not to communicate. Subdivision 1 of section 240.30, which includes communications directed to an unwilling listener under circumstances wherein "substantial privacy interests are being invaded in an essentially intolerable manner", which are made "in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm" and with "intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm",  [***2] is not unconstitutionally vague.

Housing & Development Administration v. Community Housing Improv. Program, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


90 Misc. 2d 813; 396 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1977 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2162



January 26, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Modified.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- unconstitutional administration of rent control and rent stabilization laws.

1. The Civil Court of the City of New York did not have the authority to declare the rent control and rent stabilization laws (Administrative Code of City of New York, tits Y-51, YY-51) unconstitutional as administered at the conclusion of a hearing to ascertain the steps to be undertaken to achieve compliance with required housing standards. The hearing was ordered after defendants landlords were enjoined from conducting a boiler shut down in protest against the failure of plaintiff Housing and Development Administration to meet the rising costs of operating housing in the City of New York.  [***2] After the hearing, the trial court improperly permitted defendants to amend their pleadings to conform to the proof and thereby raise the constitutional questions it dealt with. Such action had the effect of transforming the contentions of defendants into an application for declaratory judgment or article 78 relief. Jurisdiction to grant such relief is limited solely to the Supreme Court ( CPLR arts 30, 78).

2. Notwithstanding the finding of no jurisdiction in the Civil Court, the findings and conclusions of that court establishing a total breakdown in the administration of rent control and rent stabilization as the result of unreasonable delays, merit further consideration. The Legislature should take whatever steps are necessary to remedy the improper administration of the rent control laws.

1365 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. Lincoln Budget Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, First Department


89 Misc. 2d 252; 390 N.Y.S.2d 774; 1976 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2805



November 12, 1976 


HEADNOTES

Courts -- power to vacate default judgments obtained by fraud -- court's power to supervise its own processes.

1. Section 217-a of the Judiciary Law, which authorizes an Administrative Judge to bring a proceeding to set aside default judgments obtained by fraud, misrepresentation or other illegality, is constitutional, being a rational expression of the traditional inherent power of a court rendering a judgment to relieve a party from that judgment for sufficient reasons and in the furtherance of justice. Petitioner commenced the proceeding against appellants to vacate several hundred default judgments obtained by appellants against consumers on installment sales contracts in actions commenced in the Civil Court of the City of New York on the grounds that appellants were without legal capacity to sue as corporations, that they unlawfully conducted business as sales finance companies without a license,  [***2] and that the installment contracts underlying the default judgments were permeated with fraud. Fraudulent practice in consumer credit transactions is a concern of vast public importance. The fact that the defenses alleged in the petition were not raised by defaulting consumers does not estop the court from itself raising such issues.

2. Since the statute is merely declaratory of the court's common-law inalienable power to supervise its own processes, there is no impediment to its application to judgments entered prior to its effective date. Moreover, since the statute is remedial and procedural in nature, it may constitutionally be utilized to test the validity of judgments entered before its enactment. In any event, courts themselves are not bound by limitations of time imposed on the litigants before them.

3. The fact that a proceeding under section 217-a of the Judiciary Law is commenced by the Administrative Judge does not disqualify the Judges of the Civil Court from hearing such proceeding. Petitioner, who commenced the proceeding in his administrative capacity, is removed from any judicial function relative to the proceeding. There has been no showing that the Judges [***3] of the Civil Court cannot fairly and impartially adjudicate the merits of the proceeding.

 

1366 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rice


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Term, Second Department


87 Misc. 2d 257; 383 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1976 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2196



March 4, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] March 5, 1976.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sodomy -- consensual sodomy statute (Penal Law, § 130.00, subd 2) is not unconstitutional by reason of fact that it excepts persons married to each other from prosecution; right to privacy attached to marital relationship has not been extended to unmarried couples.

The consensual sodomy statute (Penal Law, § 130.00, subd 2) is not unconstitutional by reason of the fact that it excepts persons married to each other from prosecution. States have the right and power to regulate sexual conduct in order to promote the health, safety and morals of their inhabitants. There is no fundamental right to commit consensual sodomy. The only exception to the statute is constitutionally mandated, based on the privacy attached to the marital relationship. Such right to privacy regarding the issue of sodomy has not been extended to [**2] unmarried couples.

People v. Sullivan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT

DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction is affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - public lewdness - public lewdness statute (Penal Law, § 245.00) is constitutional; reasonable man Would have no difficulty in determining conduct proscribed by statute.

The public lewdness statute (Penal Law § 245.00) which makes it unlawful for a person to intentionally expose "the private or intimate parts of his body in a lewd manner" is not vague and is, therefore, constitutional. Defendant was lawfully convicted for deliberately beckoning complainant, a 17-year-old female, to his automobile and displaying his intimate parts. A reasonable man would have no difficulty in determining that such act falls within the conduct proscribed by the statute.

 

1368 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McGarvey v. Regan


RJI No. 01-89-ST2153, Index No. 3735-89


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


146 Misc. 2d 223; 549 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 849



December 29, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The proceeding is dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, active police officers of a county police department, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Mil. Law § 243 on the basis that it violated the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OVERVIEW: The police officers prior to working for the police department had served in the military. Each had applied for pension credit from the retirement system for their years of active military service. Respondent Comptroller of the State of New York denied their requests on the basis that they had not been employed by the police department prior to their entering the military. The court affirmed the Comptroller's decision. N.Y. Mil. Law § 243(4) and N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 341 established that credits could only be obtained by persons who were public employees before entering the military and had reentered public service. The court agreed, however, that N.Y. Mil. Law § 243(4) and N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 341 discriminated against the police officers. But it held that the police officers failed to establish that there was not a rational basis for the discrimination. Moreover, the court noted that the statutes complied with a decision of the United States Supreme Court that held under the Veteran's Re-employment Rights Act, 38 U.S.C.S. § 2021) an employee that left a job for military service was entitled to pension protection during the absence.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the police officers' proceeding and found that state statutes that denied them pension credit for their military service was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Military Service

1. The State Comptroller and the Policemen's and Firemen's Retirement System followed existing law in denying additional retirement credits sought by petitioners, each of whom served in the United States Armed Services prior to entering public service as police officers and sought additional credits, since the additional credits are only available to a member of the retirement system who leaves public employment for military service and rejoins the civil service upon discharge from military service, and who has made any required contribution into his retirement account (see, Military Law § 243 [4]; Retirement and Social Security Law § 341 [a], [b] [6]).

Actions -- Improper Form of Action -- Conversion to Proper Form -- Article 78 to Declaratory Judgment

2. A CPLR article 78 proceeding is not the proper vehicle to challenge the constitutionality of a statute; however, the court can remedy such procedural infirmity under CPLR 103 (c) and reach the merits by converting the article 78 proceeding into an action for a declaratory judgment. Accordingly, a proceeding challenging the constitutionality of Military Law § 243 (4) and Retirement and Social Security Law § 341 (a), (b) (6), which permit additional retirement credits to members of the Policemen's and Firemen's Retirement System who leave State service, join the military, and then rejoin State service after making the required contributions into their retirement accounts, is converted into an action for a declaratory judgment.

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Military Service -- Additional Credits

3. Military Law § 243 (4) and Retirement and Social Security Law § 341 (a), (b) (6), which permit additional retirement credits to members of the Policemen's and Firemen's Retirement System who leave State service, join the military, and then rejoin State service after making the required contributions into their retirement accounts, but deny such credits to those members of the military who were not State employees prior to joining the Armed Services, are not unconstitutional; although the legislation is discriminatory in singling out one class of individuals to receive a special benefit, petitioners, police officers who were not State employees prior to joining the Armed Services, have failed to sustain their burden of establishing that the Legislature lacked a rational basis for its decision to enact the statutes in question. The United States has expressly held that under the Veteran's Re-employment Rights Act such treatment of employees, public or private, who leave a job for military service then subsequently return to their employment, is required.

 

1369 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


ILC Data Device Corp. v. County of Suffolk


Index No. 12149-88


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SUFFOLK COUNTY


146 Misc. 2d 462; 550 N.Y.S.2d 993; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 860



December 27, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff local businesses filed an action against defendant local government to declare its ordinance, governing the use of video display terminals in the workplace, invalid. The local government filed motions seeking summary judgment to dismiss the local business' lawsuit. The local businesses filed a motion for partial summary judgment that would result in striking down the ordinance in its entirety.


OVERVIEW: The local businesses argued that the local government's ordinance was invalid ab initio because the local government lacked the authority to enact its ordinance because the ordinance superseded state law related to the same subject. The local government argued that the general grant of authority found in N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 10(1) gave it the authority to enact ordinances related to the protection, order, conduct, safety, health, and well-being of persons or property. The court agreed with the local businesses and declared the local government's ordinance invalid. The court pointed out that the local government's argument rendered N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 11 (1)(f) superfluous. The court also pointed out the area of concern was a matter subject to regulation by state law and further, the ordinance was inconsistent with the general state law.


OUTCOME: The court declared the local government's ordinance invalid in an action filed by local businesses, because the local government did not have the authority to enact the ordinance at issue.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- Regulation of Video Display Terminals in Workplace

A law adopted by a County Legislature regulating the use of video display terminals (VDT) in the workplace and providing safeguards for employees who use VDTs is invalid ab initio since the County Legislature lacked the authority to enact the law; a county is a municipal corporation whose existence and powers are dependent upon the State Legislature, and even if the county law is not inconsistent with other State statutes, it is violative of Municipal Home Rule Law § 11 (1) (f), which provides that local laws may not be adopted if they apply to or affect any provision of the Labor Law or the Workers' Compensation Law, since it cannot be denied that the county law has at minimum a general relationship to the Labor Law, which is concerned with protecting the health and safety of employees (see, Labor Law § 200). Although Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 (1) (ii) (a) (12) authorizes localities to enact laws relating to the safety, health and well-being of persons therein, that statute must be read in conjunction with section 11, since no part of either statute should be construed as to render one or the other meaningless or superfluous. Moreover, inasmuch as the words of section 11, "Applies to or affects", are not specifically defined, they must be construed in their natural and most obvious sense; accordingly, if a local law is either relevant to or acts upon the Labor Law or the Workers' Compensation Law, it represents an unauthorized use of a municipality's legislative power.

 

1370 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Frontier Ins. Co. v. State


Claim No. 78288, Motion No. M-40823, Cross Motion No. CM-40876


COURT OF CLAIMS OF NEW YORK


146 Misc. 2d 237; 550 N.Y.S.2d 243; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 855



December 19, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In plaintiff insurer's action to recover fees and expenses incurred in defending an insured in a medical malpractice action, the insurer and defendant state both filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: A physician taught at a state medical school and hospital. As part of an incentive to draw highly qualified physicians to the teaching hospital, the physician was permitted to attend to private patients. She had malpractice insurance that excluded incidents within the scope of the physician's activities within the state, and covered incidents with the physician's private patients. A patient filed an action against the physician for malpractice, and the insurer denied coverage. It contended that because the incident occurred while the physician was acting as an instructor, the policy did not provide coverage. It further contended that because the physician was acting within her duties with the state, the state was required to provide coverage under N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 17. The insurer filed an action against the state, and both sides moved for summary judgment. The court denied both motions. The court found that there were insufficient facts to answer the critical question of whether the physician's duties were performed within the scope of her state employment, and further proceedings were necessary.


OUTCOME: The court denied both the motions of both the state and the insurer for summary judgment, as questions of fact remained regarding whether the physician was acting within the scope of her state employment.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Duty to Defend and Indemnify -- Voluntary Assumption of Defense

1. In an action against the State commenced by an insurance company, as subrogee, seeking legal fees and expenses it incurred in defending its insured, a physician employed as a professor of medicine by the State University system, in which the insurer contends that defendant was obligated under Public Officers Law § 17 to defend the physician in a medical malpractice action and that it wrongfully refused to do so, defendant may not escape liability by asserting that the insurance policy between the insurer and its insured provided for the insurer to become responsible for defending and indemnifying the insured only in the event "the insurance carrier for the State * * * declines to provide insurance and or indemnity [for the insured]", and that since the dispute regarding coverage was with the State, not its insurance carrier, claimant insurer was not contractually required to defend its insured and did so voluntarily, since neither the insurer nor its insured had any control over or knowledge of whether defendant would choose to fulfill its section 17 obligations by obtaining coverage through a carrier, by acting as a self-insurer, or simply by absorbing any losses. The term "carrier" in the policy refers to the entity that would be obligated to represent and indemnify the insured in an action to which section 17 applied, and it is irrelevant who that other party would be.

Subrogation -- Insurance Company as Equitable Subrogee -- Action to Recover Legal Fees and Expenses

2. In an action against the State commenced by an insurance company, as subrogee, seeking legal fees and expenses it incurred in defending its insured, a physician employed as a professor of medicine for the State University system, in which the insurer contends that defendant was obligated under Public Officers Law § 17 to defend the physician in a medical malpractice action and that it wrongfully refused to do so, defendant may not escape liability on the ground that the insurer, as a third party, cannot be subrogated to the statutory rights of the physician, where the insurer expressly excluded coverage for actions arising from the physician's State employment and reduced its premiums accordingly, since subdivision (7) of section 17 provides that the statute "shall not be construed to impair * * * the rights and obligations of any insurer under any policy of insurance", and the insurance policy in question provides that the insurer is to be subrogated to any of the physician's rights upon assuming defense or making payment under the contract. Moreover, even if the contract did not expressly so provide, the insurer would be an equitable subrogee, and section 17 contains no language preventing a subrogee from exercising any right an employee might have in the event of the State's disclaimer of liability.

Public Officers -- Duty of State to Defend and Indemnify -- Physicians/Professors in State University System -- Practice Plan

3. In an action against the State commenced by an insurance company seeking legal fees and expenses incurred in defending its insured, a physician employed as a professor of medicine in the State University system, in which the insurer contends that defendant was obligated under Public Officers Law § 17 to defend the physician in a medical malpractice action and that it wrongfully refused to do so, the State's motion for summary judgment is denied where claimant alleged that the plaintiff in the malpractice action was a patient receiving care pursuant to the physician's practice plan, whereby physicians/professors may augment their teaching income, and that the operation in which the malpractice allegedly occurred was conducted in furtherance of the physician's professorial duties, since if those allegations are true, the State would be obligated to defend and indemnify the physician. The right to earn private patient fees pursuant to a physician's practice plan may be considered as additional compensation to the faculty member of a State University medical school; the system serves to attract qualified physicians as professors, provides a supply of patients for teaching purposes, and, thus, is in furtherance of public policy and is not an unconstitutional gift of public moneys (see, NY Const, art VII, § 8). Moreover, the protection afforded by section 17 may be seen as another inducement to attract qualified personnel as faculty members in State medical schools.

Public Officers -- Duty of State to Defend and Indemnify -- Physicians/Professors in State University System -- Practice Plan

4. In an action against the State commenced by an insurance company seeking legal fees and expenses incurred in defending its insured, a physician employed as a professor of medicine in the State University system, in which the insurer contends that defendant was obligated under Public Officers Law § 17 to defend the physician in a medical malpractice action and that it wrongfully refused to do so, the insurer's motion for summary judgment is denied; although the State would be liable if the patient who sued the physician had been the subject of medical instruction, the record does not conclusively establish that that patient was actually being treated in furtherance of the physician's role as professor or if she were a purely private patient, not a member of the physician's practice plan, a system whereby qualified physicians are attracted to the faculty of medical schools by permitting them to engage in a limited private practice. Accordingly, the critical inquiry is not whether the alleged malpractice occurred when the physician was treating a practice plan patient or a nonpractice plan patient who was billed directly by the hospital or medical school; rather, the relevant inquiry must be whether the act for which the State employee was sued was one which was performed within the scope of her State employment.

 

1371 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Militello v. Frank & Leo Piotrowski Builders, Inc.


Index No. 7419/88, Calendar No. 89-29


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


145 Misc. 2d 1056; 549 N.Y.S.2d 357; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 798



December 15, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant heating company filed a motion to serve an amended answer to assert a statute of limitations defense in a derivative action by plaintiff homeowners that alleged the furnace designed and installed by the heating company resulted in the emission of gases and caused personal injuries to their minor children.


OVERVIEW: The heating company relied on N.Y. C.P.L.R 214-c, passed after the problem with the homeowners' heating system was identified and corrected. Prior to enactment of the statute a cause of action for personal injuries accrued upon the last exposure to a deleterious substance and had to be commenced within three years thereafter. The heating company argued that the statute eliminated the date of last exposure as the accrual date, applied retroactively to the homeowners' claim, and provided a statute of limitations defense. The court held that absent passage of the statute the homeowners' action would have been timely and that passage of the statute did not bar their claims. The court determined that because N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214-c did not provide a time within which parties with existing claims could initiate an action for exposure, the New York legislature did not intend to time-bar any claim which existed at the time it was enacted. The court therefore concluded that the heating company's proposed amendment lacked merit and denied its motion to amend its answer.


OUTCOME: The court denied the heating company's motion to amend its answer to add a statute of limitations defense.

 

1372 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. David


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


CITY COURT OF ROCHESTER, NEW YORK


146 Misc. 2d 115; 549 N.Y.S.2d 564; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 820



December 11, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state prosecuted defendants for violating N.Y. Penal Law § 245.01, which prohibited public exposure of a female person's breasts below the top of the areola. Defendants filed motions to dismiss the charges asserting that the statute was unconstitutional in that it discriminated against women.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, who were women, were arrested for violating N.Y. Penal Law § 245.01 for baring their breasts at a public park and refusing to cover themselves when ordered to by the police. Defendants did not contest that they were nude from the waist up, they challenged the constitutionality of the statute arguing that it unlawfully discriminated against women. The court agreed with defendants that § 245.01 discriminated on a purely sexual basis in that it commanded women to cover their breasts in public and issued no similar command to men. The court held that such sexual discriminations did not constitute a denial of equal protection of the law because the discrimination was substantially related to the achievement of the important governmental objective of public decency. The court held that § 245.01 rested upon a rational basis, and reflected the current moral standards of New York, and created a constitutionally valid classification between male and female nudity. The court found defendants guilty of violating § 245.01.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the charges on the ground that the penal statute prohibiting women from publicly exposing their breasts was an unconstitutional deprivation of equal protection of the law. .


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Exposure of Person -- Exposure of Female Breasts -- Constitutionality of Gender Classification

Penal Law § 245.01, which makes the public exposure of the breast a penal violation only for women and not for men, is not an unconstitutional deprivation of equal protection of the law, since the gender classification it establishes is substantially related to the important governmental objective of protecting public decency and prohibiting public indecency; in passing a law coping with public nudity and fixing a point of limitation on it, the Legislature maintains public morality and decency, and contributes to the preservation of peace and order by reducing the incidence of circumstances likely to create public disorder. Moreover, no fundamental right to public nudity exists and public nudity is not an essential ingredient of constitutional liberty. Given the reality that the female breast possesses the erotic properties of a primary sexual organ and the male breast does not, the sexual classification contained in the statute has a rational basis and reflects the current moral standards of New York, as expressed by the Legislature.

 

1373 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Blades v. New York State Low-Level Radioactive Waste Siting Com.


Index No. 20,110


Supreme Court of New York, Allegany County


145 Misc. 2d 1040; 549 N.Y.S.2d 332; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 795



November 27, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended March 23, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state environmental agency sought an injunction against plaintiff property owners to enjoin them from blocking access to their land. In accordance with N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 0305, the agency intended to conduct studies of the properties as potential sites for a low-level radioactive waste dump. The property owners alleged that the agency's proposed actions amounted to an unconstitutional taking without just compensation.


OVERVIEW: In 42 U.S.C.S. § 2021, the federal government required each state to provide for the storage, disposal or management of low-level radioactive waste generated within the state. The agency asserted that a preliminary injunction was necessary for reasonable testing of the land to assess the suitability of the land as a low-level radioactive waste disposal site. The agency contended that it would suffer irreparable harm if the property owners continued to deny access pending the outcome of the underlying litigation. The court agreed and granted the injunction. The federal legislature had made establishment of low-level radioactive waste dump sites a high priority, setting time limits and penalties for failure to comply. The balancing of the equities was clearly in the agency's favor. While the property owners were seeking to preserve the their land and the environment, the agency was acting pursuant to a federal mandate intended to protect the public health and welfare of all citizens. Moreover, mere testing of the land was not an unconstitutional taking and N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 0305 provided for the agency's liability in the case of actual damage to the land.


OUTCOME: The court granted the agency's motion for a preliminary injunction related to the reasonable testing of the property to assess the suitability of it for a low level radioactive waste disposal site. The court denied the property owners' request that the court declare the agency's proposed action as an unconstitutional taking. The court granted a stay of its ruling should the property owners file an appeal.

 

1374 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Erickson v. Doe


Docket No. V-162-89, A-1-88, FU 431


Family Court of New York, Rockland County


145 Misc. 2d 557; 547 N.Y.S.2d 807; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 715



November 17, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the natural father of an infant, filed an action that sought to halt the adoption proceedings by which respondents, the adoptive parents, sought to adopt the child with the consent of the mother and without the consent of the natural father pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(1)(e).


OVERVIEW: The natural father and the child's mother had an adulterous relationship that led to the child's birth. The mother reconciled with her husband and gave the child up for adoption. The natural father attempted to pay for the birth expenses and to have a relationship with the child, but he was prevented from doing so by the mother's choice. He filed a petition that sought to halt the adoption proceedings, and the court conducted a hearing. The court dismissed the natural father's petition because he did not comply with § 111(1)(e), and his consent was not required. Even though it was the mother's choice that prevented him from developing a relationship with the child, it was in the best interests of the child to continue in the relationship with the adoptive parents, instead of a stranger who had never seen him. The existence or nonexistence of a substantial relationship between parent and child was a relevant criterion in evaluating both the rights of the parent and the best interests of the child.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the natural father's petition that sought to prevent an adoption of his child without his consent.

 

1375 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tinneny


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County


145 Misc. 2d 737; 547 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 741



November 8, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charges against him for failure to produce and permit examination by a police officer of second-hand purchase and sales record book, unlawful storage of acetylene, and failure of vehicle dismantler to produce dismantler records.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with failure to produce and permit examination by a police officer of second-hand purchase and sales record book, unlawful storage of acetylene gas, and failure of vehicle dismantler to produce dismantler records and vehicle and parts of vehicles. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss and asserted that the charges based on N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a(5)(a), were unconstitutional. The court stated that it was indisputable that the automobile dismantling business was heavily regulated and as such was subject to warrantless administrative searches. The court stated that it had been decided in prior precedent that § 415-a(5)(a) was constitutional and that because defendant's motion to suppress was based solely on the claim of a search based on an unconstitutional statute, the motion to suppress was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him for failure to produce and permit examination by a police officer of second-hand purchase and sales record book, unlawful storage of acetylene, and failure of vehicle dismantler to product dismantler records.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Search -- Warrantless Search of Vehicle Dismantling Business

 Vehicle and Traffic Law § 415-a (5)(a), which requires proprietors and employees of vehicle dismantling businesses to maintain records of all motor vehicles coming into their possession together with records of the disposition of each vehicle, and authorizes an agent of the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles or a police officer to examine such records and any vehicles or parts of vehicles subject to such record-keeping requirements on the premises, is not violative of NY Constitution, article I, § 12. It is a well-established exception to the warrant requirement that closely regulated businesses are subject to administrative searches to further the particular regulatory scheme of a given industry. The vast increase in automobile thefts and related automobile crimes warrants the regulation of the automobile dismantling industry. In balancing the individual's expectation of privacy in commercial enterprises and the State's interest in regulating the automobile dismantling business, it is apparent that the type of search at issue is the most effective way to discover abuses in the industry.

 

1376 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Axelrod v. Laurino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Kings County


145 Misc. 2d 818; 548 N.Y.S.2d 405; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 767



November 8, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of the New York State Department of Health commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 seeking relief in the nature of mandamus to compel respondent Surrogate of the County of Queens to provide the Department certain adoption information specified in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 4138-b to enable the Department to fulfill its statutory mandate to administer the New York State Adoption Information Registry.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner sought relief in the nature of mandamus in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to compel the Surrogate to provide the Department with certain adoption information specified in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 4138-b to enable the Department to fulfill its statutory mandate to administer the registry established pursuant to § 4138-b. Although the court agreed with the Surrogate that it was the public policy of the State of New York to preserve the confidentiality of all parties to adoption, it granted the Commissioner's petition, holding that § 4138-b was not unconstitutional. The statute provided only for disclosure of those characteristics of biological parents which would provide adoptees with physical and historical information about their biological parents without disclosing their identities and the items of information, the release of which the statute authorized, could be neutralized or made less specific to effectuate the policy of protecting the identity of the biological parents. Furthermore, the court held that the Surrogate failed to rebut the presumption that the statute was valid and constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Commissioner's petition relief in the nature of mandamus to compel the Surrogate to provide the Department certain adoption information to enable the Department to fulfill its statutory mandate to administer the New York State Adoption Information Registry.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Adoption Information Registry -- Constitutionality of Statute Establishing Registry

In a CPLR article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus to compel respondent to provide the New York State Department of Health with certain adoption information specified in Public Health Law § 4138-b to enable the Department to fulfill its statutory mandate to administer the Adoption Information Registry established pursuant to Public Health Law § 4138-b the petition is granted. The statute is the Legislature's response to the changing attitudes and values engendered by the organized effort of adult adoptees who have sought access to information about their historical and biological origins and continues the policy of protecting the privacy rights of the biological parents while furnishing nonidentifying information to adoptees. The names of the biological parents may not be disclosed to the adoptee or anyone ales absent consent, and any violation by an employee of the Department is subject to criminal and civil penalties; moreover, the Commissioner of Health has promulgated rules and procedures designed to preserve the confidentiality of the records. Any assurance which respondent may have conveyed to the biological parents in adoption proceedings over which he presided would not have been absolute even prior to enactment of Public Health Law § 4138-b in view of the provision contained in Domestic Relations Law § 114 permitting access to adoption records upon a showing of good cause. The statute provides only for disclosure of those characteristics of the biological parents which would provide adoptees with physical and historical information about their biological parents without disclosing their identities. Respondent has failed to rebut the presumption of the statute's constitutionality and his assertion that this matter is time barred is without merit.

In re Marpole


Docket No. D 8175/89


Family Court of New York, New York County


145 Misc. 2d 549; 547 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 735



November 8, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to preclude any evidence regarding an automobile in a juvenile delinquency proceeding charging him with possession of stolen property, grand larceny, and unauthorized use of a vehicle.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was charged with possession of stolen property, grand larceny, and unauthorized use of a vehicle. Defendant and his mother signed a notice of release of stolen property, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 450.10, before he had a lawyer. They did not inspect the car within the five day statutory period, and it was released. The court found that while the prosecution violated N.Y. Penal Law § 450.10, the exclusion of any evidence regarding the automobile was improper. A juvenile was incapable of waiving counsel on his own, so N.Y. Penal Law § 450.10 was not properly applied to defendant until he either appeared with counsel or waived that right under the specific procedures of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 249-a. Defendant was given leave to object to the admission of particular evidence at trial upon showing that its admission would be fundamentally unfair.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to preclude any evidence regarding an automobile in a juvenile delinquency proceeding charging him with possession of stolen property, grand larceny, and unauthorized use of a vehicle was denied, but he was given leave to object at trial, because the remedy was too restrictive.

 

1378 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Nassau County


145 Misc. 2d 724; 547 N.Y.S.2d 987; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 720



October 31, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree pursuant to a plea bargain agreement. Defendant filed objections to the court's restitution order. The court ruled on defendant's motion.


OVERVIEW: The court imposed restitution upon defendant to the Long Island Drug Enforcement Agency Task Force (DEA). Defendant objected to imposition of restitution on the grounds that it constituted a unilateral modification of the plea bargain agreement, that the payment did not constitute "restitution" as that term was meant or intended by N.Y. Penal Law § 60.27 and was therefore unlawful, and that the restitution order was unconstitutional under the New York and United States Constitutions. The court denied defendant's motion. The court's authority to consider restitution at the time of sentencing was an additional remedy beyond those encompassed within a plea bargain entered into between the parties. The DEA was a proper victim under § 60.27. In the course of its investigation, the DEA paid $ 8,200 to purchase controlled substances from defendant. This money was never recovered. The funds were the fruits of defendant's crime and reparation was required because restitution, by definition, was the return of the fruits of a crime. Defendant's agency defense did not involve a fundamental right, but rather involved a factual assertion, and as such, defendant had waived his right to assert it.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion opposing the court's restitution order in all respects.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Restitution

A defendant who pleaded guilty to criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree pursuant to a negotiated plea bargain which included a sentence of imprisonment and a $ 5,000 fine is also ordered to make restitution to the Drug Enforcement Agency (DEA) in the amount of $ 8,200. Section 60.27(1) of the Penal Law directs the court to consider restitution in addition to any of the dispositions authorized by Penal Law article 60; restitution is to be considered at the time of sentencing whether or not it was considered part of a plea bargain. In imposing restitution, the court was acting upon recommendations made by the Probation Department in its presentence report; defendant's contentions that no victim impact statement was filed and that the District Attorney did not seek the restitution order are baseless. While Penal Law § 60.27 does not specifically define "victim", the DEA is a proper victim within the meaning of the statute; in the course of its investigation, the DEA paid $ 8,200 to purchase a controlled substance from defendant, and this money was never recovered from defendant. Clearly, these funds are the "fruits" of defendant's crime and reparation must be made since restitution, by definition, is the return of the fruits of a crime. Defendant's contention that he was a mere agent for the buyer in the transaction at issue is without merit; in accepting the plea agreement, defendant admitted his guilt of the offense charged and waived any right he had to claim the agency defense at a later time.

 

1379 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Peterson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Nassau County, First District


145 Misc. 2d 501; 546 N.Y.S.2d 813; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 683



October 24, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 402(4). The simplified traffic information issued to defendant stated that he had "improper plates" on his pickup truck, which was registered with passenger license plates rather than commercial license plates. Defendant contended that his vehicle was properly registered as a passenger vehicle and that he was not in violation of § 402(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with having improper plates on his pickup truck, pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 402(4). At trial, the police officer testified that he issued a ticket to defendant because there was no cap on the vehicle and it did not have rear seats or seatbelts. The court dismissed the traffic information against defendant, after concluding that § 402(4) was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. Section 402(4) did not give a person of ordinary intelligence notice that driving a pickup truck registered as a passenger vehicle without a cap or seats in the rear violated the statute. Further, the entire Vehicle and Traffic Law was silent as to whether a motorist should register his vehicle as a passenger or commercial vehicle. Moreover, the court was without authority to review the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles' decision to allow defendant to register his vehicle with passenger plates rather than commercial plates.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the simplified traffic information charging defendant with having improper plates on his pickup truck.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Registration

A simplified traffic information charging defendant with a violation of Vehicle and Traffic Law § 402(4) in that defendant's pickup truck had "improper plates" on it because it was registered with passenger license plates rather than commercial license plates is dismissed. The issuing officer testified that the vehicle did not have a cap on it and that it did not have seats in the rear or seat belts provided for any passenger in the rear. While Vehicle and Traffic Law § 402(4) prohibits the operation of a motor vehicle which displays number plates not proper for such vehicle, nowhere in the Vehicle and Traffic Law or in the rules and regulations promulgated by the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles does it state how to determine whether to register a motor vehicle as either a commercial or passenger vehicle nor is there a definition of a passenger or commercial vehicle. The statute gives no notice that driving a pickup truck without a cap or seats in the rear, when the vehicle is registered with passenger plates, is in violation of the statute, and is, accordingly, unconstitutionally void for vagueness. Moreover, the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles has allowed defendant to register his vehicle with passenger plates and the court does not have the authority to overturn that decision by holding that defendant's vehicle was incorrectly registered.

Kindlon v. County of Rensselaer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rensselaer County


145 Misc. 2d 412; 546 N.Y.S.2d 948; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 668



October 20, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus to compel respondent county to pay him his legal fee and expenses, in accordance with the order of the trial court, pursuant to N.Y. County Law § 722-b.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was assigned to represent an indigent defendant. The attorney requested an allowance, in excess of the maximum permitted pursuant to §772-b for his legal fee. The trial court directed payment of a lesser sum for legal fees plus disbursements. The county did not pay the claim but, pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 822.4, requested a reduction. The attorney objected and the justice advised counsel that he would act on the request unless counsel promptly requested that consideration be deferred pending the institution and determination of a lawsuit to be commenced by the attorney. Upon review, the court denied the attorney's request that the county pay his legal fees. The court found that the appellate courts had the authority to award compensation in excess of the statutory maximum in appropriate cases before such courts. The court stated that it was not persuaded by the attorney's claims that low allowances resulted in inadequate representation of indigents and rejected the argument that the fee-setting order a judicial order. Because the administrative process was not completed, the court found that it was premature to rule upon the issues raised.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request to compel the county to pay the attorney's legal fees.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client -- Assignment of Counsel -- Compensation in Excess of Statutory Limits

The court rule (22 NYCRR 822.4) granting the Presiding Justice of the Appellate Division, Third Department, the power to reduce a legal fee ordered by the trial court in "extraordinary circumstances" in excess of the statutory limits for counsel assigned to represent an indigent defendant in a criminal case (County Law § 722-b) is not invalid for being in direct and irreconcilable conflict with the State-wide statute since the setting of fee allowances pursuant to County Law § 722-b is an administrative, not judicial, function of the Trial Judge in the performance of his judicial office and the Presiding Justice has the lawful authority to administratively review such fee allowances.

Alvarado v. New York City Health & Hosp. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court, New York County


145 Misc. 2d 687; 547 N.Y.S.2d 190; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 911



October 18, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the parents of a brain dead infant, filed an action against respondents, the New York City Health & Hospitals Corporation, and the city and state of New York (hospital ). The parents challenged the validity of a determination of death regulation, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 400.16, and its application to their child.


OVERVIEW: Parents sought to prevent the withdrawal of life support from their infant child, who was determined to be brain dead, in accordance with the New York State Department of Health (DOH) regulation, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 400.16. The parents challenged the validity of the regulation and its application to their child. The issues were whether the infant was "dead" within the meaning of the regulation, and whether the regulation was consistent with current medical knowledge and law. The court answered each question in the affirmative, and held that it had no authority to interfere. The parents offered no evidence to contradict the medical testing. The regulation's definition of "death" which included the irreversible and complete cessation of the functioning of the entire brain, including the brain stem, was consistence under modern medicine and law. Finally, the DOH had the authority to promulgate the regulation, N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2800, and the regulation was within the DOH's discretionary power. The regulatory standards were specific, and the parents were not deprived of their due process rights to participate in the medical care of their child.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the validity of the determination of death regulation, and its application to the parents' child. The court had no authority to intervene in the decision to withdraw life support. The definition of death in the regulation was satisfied, the regulation was based on sound medical criteria, and it was constitutionally and validly promulgated.


HEADNOTES

Death -- Determination of Death

Respondent hospital acted properly in determining that petitioners' newborn infant is clinically brain dead and should be removed from all mechanical devices maintaining heart and lung function after following all the procedures established by the Department of Health (DOH) for determining when death has occurred (10 NYCRR 400.16). DOH, as the agency charged with "responsibility for development and administration of the state's policy with respect to hospital and related services" (Public Health Law § 2800), had authority to establish uniform standards for determining death for hospitals and other licensed facilities and it acted responsibly in codifying existing law for hospitals and providing guidance in how to proceed in this area. DOH's regulation is not unconstitutional as being "vague and overly broad" since it is based on sound medical criteria, nor has the regulation resulted in a deprivation of due process since petitioners were notified before a determination was made and were given an opportunity to obtain an independent medical evaluation and were also offered a chance to have the matter discussed with religious leaders and friends. *2

Campbell v. New York


Index No. 47236/86


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


145 Misc. 2d 248; 546 N.Y.S.2d 781; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 625



September 28, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a motion for summary judgment dismissing plaintiff owners' complaint for a determination of title to 23 parcels of land and a motion for declaratory relief that the city was the sole owner of the subject real properties. The owners cross-moved for an order striking the city's affirmative defenses and granting summary judgment in their favor.


OVERVIEW: The owners brought an action to determine title to 23 parcels of real property against the city. It was undisputed that the identity and whereabouts of the owners, other than for owners of three parcels, were set forth on each of the recorded deeds filed in the register's office prior to the commencement of the in rem tax foreclosure actions. The owners contended that they did not receive actual notice by mail or personally of the impending tax foreclosure action. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment, and the city sought declaratory relief that it was the sole owner of the parcels. The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment only as to the owners of the three parcels whose names were not on the deeds in the register's office and declared the city as legal title owner. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the owners as to all the other parcels. The court held that the procedure for affording notice by publication and actual notice only on request to owners, mortgagees, and others claiming an interest in real property which was the subject of an in rem tax foreclosure action, was insufficient to afford adequate due process notice to such parties.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment as to the owners of three parcels whose names were not in the registers' office and declared the city the legal title owner thereof of those three parcels. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the owners as to all the other parcels.

 

1383 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ford


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Kings County


145 Misc. 2d 308; 546 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 641



September 26, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a mistrial was granted in defendant's first trial, defendant filed a motion to preclude his retrial on charges of murder in the second degree, assault in the second degree, and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: After deliberations began, the jury indicated that it was deadlocked, and one juror was unable to continue because of illness. Because the alternate jurors had not been acceptable to either side, the trial court released the alternates over defense objection and granted defendant's motion for mistrial. In its motion to bar retrial, defendant argued that a retrial would have violated double jeopardy principles. The court denied the defendant's motion, holding that (1) even assuming that the discharge of the first alternate was "illegal," the trial court could not have contemplated a mistrial because deliberations continued, and thus the trial court's alleged illegal actions were not intended to "goad" defendant into a mistrial; (2) defendant's motion for mistrial barred any claim of double jeopardy; (3) nothing in the Double Jeopardy Clause mandated the existence of alternate jurors or, if such were available, the substitution of an incapacitated juror; and (4) manifest necessity existed even if the alternate jurors were present at the time the mistrial was declared because it would not have been proper for the trial court to have seated an alternate after deliberations had begun.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to preclude retrial was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy -- Mistrial -- Substitution of Alternate Juror for Deliberating Juror

Defendant's retrial is not barred on double jeopardy grounds where the court, over defendant's objection, released the two alternate jurors during jury deliberations and then declared a mistrial upon defendant's motion after two days of deadlocked deliberations when one of the jurors became ill and was unable to continue deliberating since, even if the mistrial was not based upon manifest necessity, there is no evidence that the court had an improper motive for discharging the alternate jurors or was trying to "goad" defendant into moving for a mistrial. Defendant's motion for mistrial thus bars any claim of double jeopardy. Even assuming the court's request for a "motion" constituted improper coercion causing defense counsel's mistrial motion, defendant's retrial is still not barred on double jeopardy grounds since there is no statutory or constitutional requirement that the court select any alternate jurors (see, CPL 270.30) or that an alternate juror be substituted for an incapacitated juror. Furthermore, substitution of an alternate juror for a deliberating juror presents substantial fair trial and due process problems and should be used sparingly. In any event, under the totality of the circumstances, manifest necessity existed even if the alternate jurors were present at the time the mistrial was declared.

In re Doris A.


Docket No. D 6593/88


Family Court of the State of New York, City of New York, New York County


145 Misc. 2d 222; 546 N.Y.S.2d 310; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 619



September 25, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner family services brought an action to determine whether a police officer had probable cause to arrest a youth based on a person's statement that the youth is her child and that a PINS warrant existed.


OVERVIEW: A woman said she had a PINS warrant for her daughter who was not home in awhile. The officer saw a young woman across the street, called out her name, and the girl walked in her direction. When the girl got to the police car, the officer said there was a PINS warrant and she would have to take her to the precinct. While the girl was getting into the car, the officer searched the girl's person and found numerous vials of alleged crack. Upon review, the court found that the arrest of the girl was without probable cause and unlawful. The court stated that the police officers lacked probable cause to arrest the girl because they had no suspicion of criminal activity and there was an inadequate foundation to base the arrest on the statement of a stranger that a warrant existed. Since the girl's arrest could not be supported by the mere assertion of a warrant, was not based on probable cause and was unauthorized by the runaway statute, the court suppressed the vials of alleged crack seized from her following the improper arrest. The court found that in light of the suppression of the evidence, the juvenile delinquency petition should be dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the juvenile delinquency petition in light of the suppression of certain evidence illegally obtained.
