Nissequogue v. Suffolk County Dep't of Civil Service


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Suffolk County


145 Misc. 2d 382; 546 N.Y.S.2d 916; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 661



September 15, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner village sought review of a determination of respondent county civil service department, which refused to certify that portion of the village's payroll that included respondent police officers. The officers filed a cross-petition for an order that they be paid pending the outcome of the proceeding, for permission to conduct discovery, and for a jury trial. The department filed a motion to dismiss the officers' cross-petition.


OVERVIEW: The officers were hired in 1982 and 1984. The village did not submit its payroll to the department until June 1989. The department refused to certify the officers' payroll because they were not hired from an eligibility list. The village and the officers challenged the refusal. The court denied the department's motion to dismiss the officers' petition, ruled that the department had to certify the payroll, and granted the officers' request for payment. The court ruled that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 100(5) barred the department from refusing to certify the payroll because the officers finished their probationary periods and held their positions for over three years; thus, they were presumed to have been duly appointed. The court found that § 100(5) was constitutional and did not require the initial appointment to be made from an eligibility list or approved by the department, noting that the department knew of the officers' employment by 1986, but did not act until 1989. The court concluded that § 100(5) did not prevent enforcement of the civil service laws, but did not allow the department to refuse to certify the payroll more that three years after the officers were appointed.


OUTCOME: The court denied the department's motion to dismiss the officers' cross-petition, granted the village's petition to the extent that the department could not refuse to certify its payroll, granted the officers' cross-petition to the extent that they requested payment, and otherwise denied the officers' cross-petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Appointment and Promotion -- Certification of Payroll

Although petitioner village did not, as required by law, appoint respondent police officers to their positions in November 1982 and July 1984, respectively, from any "eligibility list" prepared and certified by respondent Suffolk County Department of Civil Service (Civil Service Law § 61), respondent department may not, however, now refuse to certify the village payroll as to the two corespondent police officers since it may not refuse to certify a payroll more than three years after an employee's appointment in the absence of fraud (Civil Service Law § 100 [5]). There is no need to read the requirement of initial appointment from an eligibility list into the three-year limitation upon the certification of payrolls nor can fraud be presumed from petitioner's failure to submit its payroll for certification until July 1989 where respondent department was aware of the failure to file the payroll as well as the unlawful appointments as early as 1986.

 

1387 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nat'l Fuel Gas Supply Corp. v. Cunningham Natural Gas Corp.


Index Nos. 12913, 12914, 12915


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALLEGANY COUNTY


145 Misc. 2d 825; 548 N.Y.S.2d 588; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 761



September 6, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended January 11, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff condemnor filed an action under the provisions of the Eminent Domain Procedure Law to have damages assessed against it for the condemnation of defendant condemnee's interests in a natural gas well.


OVERVIEW: In a condemnation proceeding brought under the provisions of the Eminent Domain Procedure Law, plaintiff condemnor sought to have damages assessed against it for the condemnation of defendant condemnee's interests in a gas well. The condemnor contended that the Federal Energy Regulation Commission fixed the price of just compensation for the condemnee's natural gas. The court held that a governmentally fixed ceiling price did not constitute just compensation per se. The court held that it was not limited in fixing just compensation for the condemnee's natural gas to the price fixed by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission. The court determined that the issue of just compensation was constitutionally mandated as one for judicial determination in condemnation proceeding. The court rejected the condemnor's contention that the price fixed by the Federal Energy Regulation Commission was the price that the court must fix for the condemnee's commercially recoverable native gas in place. The court held that the contract price of natural gas was the fair market value of the commercially recoverable native gas in place.


OUTCOME: In a condemnation proceeding, the court entered a judgment determining the amount of compensation awarded to defendant condemnee for commercially recoverable native gas in place.

 

1388 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Forman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


145 Misc. 2d 115; 546 N.Y.S.2d 755; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 598



September 5, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to vacate a temporary order of protection (TOP), and a motion for a hearing to vacate TOP, which excluded defendant from his home, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 510.20. Defendant asserted the TOP was issued without affording him an opportunity to be heard, and the procedures for issuance of a TOP pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 530.12(1) and (1)(a) violated federal constitutional due process requirements.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with assault and harassment, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law §§ 120.00 and 240.25, for an alleged domestic violence incident with his wife. At defendant's arraignment, the court issued a temporary order of protection (TOP), without a hearing, and ordered defendant to stay away from his wife and home. Subsequently, the prosecution added an additional charge for criminal contempt for alleged violation of the TOP, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 215.50(3). Defendant filed a motion for hearing pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 510.20, to vacate the TOP and additional contempt charge for insufficient evidence and violation of due process of law. Upon a hearing, the court denied defendant's motion to vacate the original TOP on constitutional grounds because there was timely notice of the right to a hearing. However, the court withdrew its oral decision that there was a sufficient basis for issuance of a TOP because no evidence was taken to determine probable cause. The court granted a new TOP with specific terms of exclusion. The court dismissed the charge of criminal contempt because the terms of the TOP were vague and indefinite.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate an original temporary order of protection (TOP) on constitutional grounds because there was timely notice of the right to a hearing, but withdrew its oral decision that there was a sufficient basis for issuance of a TOP. The court granted a new TOP with specific terms of exclusion, and dismissed a charge of criminal contempt based upon a violation of the original TOP, as it was vague and indefinite.


HEADNOTES

Bail -- Recognizance -- Protection for Victims of Family Offenses -- Criminal Contempt

Defendant, charged with criminal contempt (Penal Law § 215.50 [3]) for threatening his wife with violence over the telephone in violation of a temporary order of protection (TOP) issued June 24, 1988 upon his release on his own recognizance at his arraignment on assault and harassment charges brought on the complaint of his wife, has standing to raise constitutional objections to the statute authorizing the issuance of the TOP (CPL 530.12 [1]) even though the initial TOP he is accused of violating is no longer in effect since defendant's liberty and property interests were and are restricted by each of the temporary orders of protection issued against him and the temporary nature of short-term orders may not be used to insulate them from legal challenge. The existing procedures and criteria for the issuance of temporary orders of protection satisfy the requirements of due process in that a probable cause determination must be made by a judicial officer before a TOP may be issued at arraignment; a defendant is entitled to a presentation of reasons for the issuance of a TOP by the People and the right to be heard in opposition to its issuance; the court must first be satisfied that there is a "danger of injury or intimidation" to the complainant before issuing a TOP as a condition of bail or recognizance; and a defendant has the right to a prompt evidentiary hearing following the issuance of the TOP. The fact that the court, when issuing a TOP, is not statutorily mandated to state its findings of fact and conclusions of law on the record does not impair the constitutionality of the statute as long as the reasons for the determination are apparent from the record nor does the absence of specific statutory criteria make the statute violative of due process in view of the judicially created standard that a "danger of intimidation or injury" to the complainant exist (the statute having been amended effective November 1, 1988 to list factors to be considered by the court in determining whether to issue a TOP excluding a defendant from the home). However, the criminal contempt charges against defendant must be dismissed since the provision in the TOP requiring him to "abstain from offensive conduct" against his wife is too vague and indefinite to support a charge of criminal contempt for its violation.

Glod v. Ashland Chem. Co., Div. of Ashland Oil


Index No. 89-404, RJI 37-89-0115


Supreme Court of New York, Oswego County


145 Misc. 2d 200; 546 N.Y.S.2d 748; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 616



August 22, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporations filed a motion to dismiss the action filed by plaintiffs, husband and wife. The complaint alleged that the husband sustained injuries from being exposed to chemical products that were sold, processed, or manufactured by the corporations.


OVERVIEW: The corporations' motion to dismiss was based on facial deficiencies in the husband and wife's toxic torts liability complaint that amounted to a failure to state a cause of action and the assertion that the claims were time-barred. The court granted the motion to dismiss the one claim because it did not reference any specific statute, ordinance, or regulation. Otherwise, the court denied the motion to dismiss for failure to state a cause of action. The court found that the lack of specificity in the other stated claims was not fatal because, as pleaded, they sufficiently complied with the requirements of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3013 by giving notice of the transactions or occurrences and the material elements. The court found that the three-year limitation period of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214-c for toxic torts applied to time bar the strict liability and negligence claims because the action was commenced more than three years from the date that the husband discovered his injuries. The claims based on breach of contract for the sale of goods were not time-barred because the period of limitations for such claims was four years from the date of the last tender of the delivery of the goods.


OUTCOME: The court granted in part and denied in part the motion to dismiss the various claims asserted by the husband and wife in their toxic torts personal injury action.

 

1390 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Yonkers Parking Authority v. Yonkers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


145 Misc. 2d 11; 546 N.Y.S.2d 280; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 587



August 15, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended December 6, 1989


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parking authority (Authority) filed a declaratory action against respondent city and requested a judgment vacating a notice from the city that terminated the authority's right to operate a parking garage, declaring that the city could not compete against the authority in the operation of the garage, and for attorneys' fees.


OVERVIEW: The authority was created by statute and the city and authority entered into several agreements for the authority to operate parking garages. The city sent a notice to the authority, pursuant to the provisions of one of their contracts, and terminated the agreement with respect to one of the parking garages. The authority filed a declaratory judgment action against the city and asserted that the city lacked the power to terminate the agreement or operate the garage itself in direct competition with the authority. The court invalidated the termination and held that regardless of the language of the contract the ownership of the garages reverted to the city only pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law §§ 1596-f(5), -q. The termination provision in the contract was in derogation of the statute and inoperative. Additionally, the covenant against competition in the original contract was valid and the subsequent enactment of N.Y. Laws of 1971 (ch. 491) did not invalidate the contractual provision. The court awarded the authority fees pursuant to the attorneys' fee provision of the contract.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of the authority in its action against a city to invalidate a termination of an agreement to operate a parking garage and also awarded attorneys' fees to the authority.


HEADNOTES

Public Authorities -- City of Yonkers Parking Authority -- Life Interest in Property Used as Parking Facility

The provision in the 1978 agreement for the operation of a parking garage between respondent City of Yonkers (City) and petitioner Yonkers Parking Authority (Authority), which allowed either party to terminate the agreement "upon 90 days' notice in writing", is void for being in derogation of the law granting the Authority a life interest in property transferred to it by the City for parking-related functions "for so long as its corporate existence shall continue" ( Public Authorities Law § 1596-f [1]). In addition, the 90-day cancellation provision is also in conflict with the permit of use afforded the Authority in a 1964 agreement which encompassed the property on which the parking garage was built. Furthermore, the City is barred from operating the parking garage pursuant to the anticompetition clause in the 1964 agreement. A subsequently enacted law authorizing the City "[n]otwithstanding any provisions of any general, special or local law to the contrary" to "construct, operate and maintain parking facilities" within the City (L 1971, ch 491) did not abrogate the anticompetition clause since nothing in the law precluded the City from contractually giving up its right to compete and, had the law contained language overriding the parties' 1964 agreement, it would have constituted an unconstitutional impairment of an existing contractual obligation. Lastly, the Authority is entitled to an award of reasonable attorneys' fees pursuant to the provision in the 1978 agreement requiring the City to "[p]rovide legal services as are necessary to the operation of the Garage".

 

1391 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Jane Doe v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


144 Misc. 2d 777; 545 N.Y.S.2d 490; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 500



August 11, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion for summary judgment is granted for Axelrod, upholding regulation 10 NYCRR 80.67 as constitutional and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of New York, New York County, considered an action in which plaintiffs, various members of the medical and pharmaceutical professions (professionals), challenged a regulation promulgated by defendant, the State Health Commissioner (Commissioner), which imposed stringent limitations on the prescription of certain common tranquilizers.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner promulgated a regulation which significantly changed the prescription methods for a group of commonly used sedatives collectively known as benzodiazepines. Specifically, the regulation required these drugs be prescribed in official triplicate prescriptions, prohibited refills, and limited patients to a 30-day supply. The regulation was passed in response to the Commissioner's findings that large numbers of these drugs were misused, diverted to nonmedical uses, and overprescribed by careless physicians. The professionals feared the regulation would have excessively intruded on their ability to freely treat their patients and claimed that the regulation was unconstitutional. The court found otherwise, and granted the Commissioner summary judgment, because the Commissioner had authority to pass the regulation under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3338(3), and the regulation, which was rationally based on a community health concern, did not prevent doctors from prescribing benzodiazepines but rather, prohibited such prescriptions only without the triplicate forms.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Commissioner summary judgment and upheld the regulation as constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Drugs and Druggists -- Controlled Substances -- Stricter Control on Prescription of Benzodiazepines

A regulation of the Department of Health requiring that commonly used sedatives known as benzodiazepines, such as Valium and librium (see, Public Health Law § 3306), be prescribed in official triplicate prescriptions, prohibiting refills and limiting patients to a 30-day supply (10 NYCRR 80.67), promulgated in response to the Health Commissioner's findings that large numbers of these drugs are misused, diverted to nonmedical uses and overprescribed by careless physicians, has a rational basis and is thus not unconstitutional. The record-keeping function of the triplicate system is a reasonable exercise of the State's broad police powers, and there is no basis for the belief that the State would improperly use the information. The challenged regulation will not disturb the privileged relationship between the doctor and patient nor will it reveal more information than the State must be legally entitled to know. The Commissioner clearly has the authority to require that such controlled substances be prescribed in triplicate (Public Health Law § 3308) and he fully complied with all procedural requirements in promulgating the regulation (State Administrative Procedure Act §§ 202-a, 202-b) after seeking the advice and recommendations of both the Drug Abuse Advisory Council and the Public Health Council and opening the discussion of the regulation to include the input of any interested agencies.

 

1392 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ali


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


144 Misc. 2d 543; 544 N.Y.S.2d 466; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 455



July 24, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with sexual abuse in the third degree, attempted sodomy in the third degree, and endangering the welfare of a child. Defendant sought to dismiss the count of endangering the welfare of a child, an A misdemeanor, asserting that the statute, N.Y. Penal Law § 260.10(1), was unconstitutionally vague in violation of the Due Process Clause of the Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly made minor boys touch his penis and fondle their penises. In his motion to dismiss the count of endangering the welfare of a child, defendant urged that § 260.10(1) was void for vagueness because it did not define the criminal offense with sufficient definiteness such that ordinary people could understand what conduct was prohibited. Defendant did not point specifically to any language in the statute as vague, either as applied to his own alleged offense or in general. The court denied the motion, stating that, while perhaps unusual in the context of the Penal Law, the language of § 260.10(1) used ordinary terms to express ideas, which found adequate interpretation in common usage and understanding, and was sufficient to warn of what was required and what was prohibited. Indeed, the language of § 260.10(1) was certainly sufficient to inform a "reasonable man" that making minor boys fondle his and their penises was among activities prohibited as likely to be injurious to the physical, mental, or moral welfare of a male child.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the count of endangering the welfare of a child.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Endangering Welfare of Child

Penal Law § 260.10 (1), which provides that a person is "guilty of endangering the welfare of a child when * * * [he] knowingly acts in a manner likely to be injurious to the physical, mental or moral welfare of a male child less than sixteen years old", is not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, who is charged with making 7-, 8- and 11-year-old boys fondle his and their penises, since the language of the statute is sufficient to inform a "reasonable man" that the conduct allegedly engaged in by defendant is among activities prohibited as "likely to be injurious". There can be no doubt that fondling of penises of and by young children is precisely the sort of activity intended to be covered by the statute.

 

1393 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. U.S.T.A. Nat'l Tennis Center, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Queens County


144 Misc. 2d 449; 544 N.Y.S.2d 458; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 447



July 7, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a corporation that owned and operated the tennis facility, was charged with violating § 27-361 of the New York City Building Code, N.Y.C., N.Y., Admin. Code, tit. 27, ch. 1, § 27-361. Defendant allegedly obstructed exits in the tennis center during tennis matches attended by thousands of spectators.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, which owned and managed the facility in which the tennis tournament was held, was charged with violating § 27-361 by obstructing exits during the tennis tournament. The obstruction was caused by a chain strung across stairwells and passageways, as well as the resulting groups of people who congregated in those areas until the chain was periodically removed. The court rejected defendant's assertion that the building code did not apply to people awaiting seating at tennis matches. Section 27-361 clearly and unequivocally required that exits and access pathways had to be kept clear for use at all times without obstruction. The court also concluded that the building code provided the necessary notice of the exact conduct prohibited and equated exits and access facilities with stairwells and passageways. Section 27-361 was neither unconstitutionally vague nor violative of the state and federal Due Process Clauses. The accumulation of a large group of people in the arena stairwells constituted a failure to keep exists readily accessible and unobstructed within the meaning of the provision. Defendant was found guilty.


OUTCOME: Defendant was found guilty of violating the building code provision prohibiting the obstruction of exits to the tennis facility.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- New York City Building Code -- Unobstructed Exits at Tennis Facility

Defendant, the operator of a public tennis facility in Flushing Meadow Park where the United States Open is held, violated the provision of the New York City Building Code mandating that all "exits and access facilities * * * be kept readily accessible and unobstructed at all times" ( Administrative Code of City of New York § 27-361) by allowing the passageway exits at the tennis center to become obstructed by spectators not permitted to proceed to their seats during play in order to minimize the possible diversion of the players' concentration during the match. Blocked exits are a primary cause of injuries and often death when disasters occur at public events involving the assemblage of large numbers of people and defendant clearly engaged in a course of conduct that blocked the passageways. Defendant required no prior notification of the violation ( Administrative Code § 26-246 [a] [1]) nor is the prosecution subject to discontinuance because of the prompt alleged correction of the condition upon notification. The statute is not unconstitutionally vague since it imparts fair notice that obstruction of exits and passageways by spectators is prohibited (see, Administrative Code § 27-531).

 

1394 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morbelli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


144 Misc. 2d 482; 544 N.Y.S.2d 442; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 405



June 29, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state charged defendant in the Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County (New York) with endangering the welfare of a child in violation of N.Y. Penal. Law. § 260.10(1), and, with sexual abuse in the third degree in violation of N.Y. Penal. Law. § 130.55. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information on constitutional grounds and that his statutory rights to a speedy trial had been denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that N.Y. Penal. Law. § 260.10(1), and, N.Y. Penal. Law. § 130.55 were unconstitutional as written because the language used to delineate the prohibited conduct was vague under the due process clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions. Alternately, defendant argued that the statutes were unconstitutional as applied. He also argued that the document filed by the people, that converted the complaint to an information, was insufficient to constitute a corroborating affidavit as required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.30 and, accordingly the charges had to be dismissed because more than 90 days had elapsed since commencement of the action. The court rejected defendant's arguments and denied the motion. The court noted that legislative enactments were presumed constitutional unless unconstitutionality was demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt. The court examined the language of both statutes and found them to be constitutional as a person of reasonable intelligence would have had sufficient notice that the alleged conduct was prohibited. The court also noted that the supporting document used to convert the complaint to an information was sufficient.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motions to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Presumptions

1. Legislative enactments are presumed constitutional and the burden of demonstrating that a statute is unconstitutional rests with the party seeking to invalidate it; unconstitutionality must be shown beyond a reasonable doubt. Moreover, a law is void if it fails to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct is forbidden by the statute, or if the failure to provide explicit standards for application encourages arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.

Crimes -- Endangering Welfare of Child -- Constitutionality of Statute -- Vagueness -- Application

2. Penal Law § 260.10 (1), which defines the crime of endangering the welfare of a child, is neither unconstitutionally vague nor unconstitutional as applied to defendant, a 40-year-old man who was accused of placing his arm up the dress of a 16-year-old girl and fondling her leg and upper thigh, since the void-for-vagueness argument has already been rejected by the Court of Appeals in deciding a case under the predecessor statute to section 260.10 (1), i.e., former Penal Law § 483; the court concluded that the statute provided adequate notice to all persons subject to its mandate of both the nature of the conduct prohibited and what is required of them to refrain from violating its proscription. The conduct prohibited under the statute requires a showing that the defendant had to have some degree of control over the minor child as a predicate to the imposition of criminal liability, and based on the misdemeanor complaint lodged against defendant, sufficient facts have been alleged to create an issue of fact where defendant and the complainant were riding on a bus transporting air travelers from an airport to Manhattan. Moreover, defendant's motion to dismiss is premature: at the very least a trial is warranted to establish a full record with respect to defendant's control over the complainant.

Crimes -- Sexual Abuse -- "Intimate Part" -- Leg and Thigh

3. In a prosecution for sexual abuse in the third degree (Penal Law § 130.55), the leg and thigh may be considered an "intimate part" of the complainant's body within the definition of "sexual contact" (see, Penal Law § 130.00 [3]), since a determination of what is "intimate" involves considerations other than mere anatomical location, and what is "intimate" cannot be determined independently of the circumstances of the alleged behavior. Penal Law § 130.55 is not void for vagueness since a person of reasonable intelligence would have sufficient notice of what conduct is prohibited; "sexual" may connote a sphere of behavior associated with libidinal gratification rather than any specific acts.

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial -- Conversion of Complaint to Information

4. In a sex crimes prosecution involving a 16-year-old female victim, defendant was not denied his statutory right to a speedy trial by the fact that the document filed by the People to convert the complaint to an information lacked an attestation, used the male gender to describe the complainant, and may not have been subscribed in New York State, since the existence of a form notice together with the subscription of the deponent are sufficient to verify the document, since the inadvertent use of the male gender was merely a formal error not affecting the validity of the document, which can be corrected without the complainant being required to swear to a new affidavit, and since even if the complainant is a New York State resident the court considers CPL 20.20 (2) (b) would confer jurisdiction on the court over a violation of Penal Law § 210.45 committed out of State under the circumstances.

 

1395 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McGowan v. New York Tel. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


144 Misc. 2d 625; 544 N.Y.S.2d 423; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 456



June 21, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion is granted as to the fourth, fifth and sixth causes of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, firemen and their wives, filed a complaint against defendants, operator and other companies, seeking money damages for injuries allegedly sustained as a result of fighting a fire on the operator's premises. Defendants filed a motion for summary judgment claiming that the action was time barred and that the revival statute, 1986 N.Y. Laws 682, § 4, did not apply.


OVERVIEW: The firemen were exposed to polyvinyl chloride at the fire and claimed that after the applicable statutes of limitation had expired, it became clear that their exposure had lasting effects. Therefore, plaintiffs claimed that the revival statute applied to revive their claims for injuries caused by the latent effects of PVC exposure. The court found that the complaint was filed in a timely manner under the revival statute and rejected defendants' contention that the failure to expressly plead the revival statute or to expressly allege latent effects of PVC exposure rendered the complaint fatally defective. The court granted plaintiffs leave to amend. The court granted defendants' motion as to claims against the other companies. The court found that actions based upon N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 205-a were revived, but granted defendants' motion as to those claims because plaintiffs failed to specify any statute, ordinance, or rule violated by defendants.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment as to claims that were revived.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Revival of Time-Barred Action -- Toxic Tort

The revival statute, which provides that every action for personal injury caused by the latent effects of exposure to certain toxic substances, including polyvinyl chloride (PVC), that would otherwise be time barred as of July 30, 1986 may be commenced within one year from that date (L 1986, ch 682, § 4), applies to revive plaintiffs' action commenced in June 1987 to recover for injuries caused by the latent effects of exposure to PVC while fighting a fire at defendant telephone company's building in February 1975, even though plaintiffs failed to expressly plead the revival statute in the complaint or to expressly allege latent effects of exposure to PVC, since plaintiffs are granted leave to amend the complaint ( CPLR 3025 [b]). In view of the legislative determination that PVC can cause latent severe ill effects to those who ingest it, the revival statute as it relates to PVC must be deemed constitutional to the same extent it has been deemed constitutional with regard to DES (see, Hymowitz v Lilly & Co., 73 NY2d 487). In addition, the fact that certain injuries caused by PVC exposure were discovered shortly after the incident does not preclude a claim that more severe latent effects were not discovered until expiration of the applicable limitations period nor does any prejudice to defendants from the passage of time between the incident and the action render the revival of plaintiffs' action unconstitutional. However, although plaintiffs' causes of action regarding safety violations that created hazards additional to those firefighters already face ( General Municipal Law § 205-a) are revived by the revival statute, they must be dismissed for failure to specify or identify the statutes, ordinances or rules defendants neglected or failed to comply with.

 

1396 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Leader v. Adduci


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


144 Misc. 2d 497; 544 N.Y.S.2d 414; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 413



June 5, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant appealed an administrative decision by respondent state commissioner of motor vehicles, which, without a hearing, denied the applicant's request for a driver's license under former N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 510(6), now N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1193(2)(c). Section 1193(2)(c) prohibited the issuance of a driver's license to a person who had been twice convicted of driving under the influence where personal injury resulted.


OVERVIEW: The applicant was convicted of driving under the influence, where there were personal injuries to himself and a passenger. Later, the applicant was involved in a one-car accident from which his second conviction resulted. His license was revoked, and his administrative appeal was denied. On judicial review, the court rejected the applicant's contentions that § 1193(c)(2) was unconstitutionally vague in its failure to define "personal injury," holding that the applicant failed to rebut the presumption that the ordinary meaning was adequate to put a person on notice of prohibited conduct. The injuries that the applicant sustained in the first automobile accident were "personal injuries" as contemplated by § 1193(c)(2). The court rejected the commissioner's argument that the applicant had waived his due process arguments by failing to supply documentation to his claims or request a hearing. The applicant's written request for review and his representations concerning the availability of witnesses that would have confirmed his assertion that he was not injured in the second accident were adequate to invoke his procedural rights. The court annulled the commissioner's determination.


OUTCOME: The court granted the applicant's petition to set aside the commissioner's administrative decision that denied the applicant his driver's license and ordered that the applicant was granted a driver's license. The court held that the applicant was entitled to a fact-finding hearing upon the commissioner's future consideration of revocation of his driver's license.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while Under Influence of Alcohol or Drugs -- Permanent Revocation of License Based on Two Injury-Related Accidents

The fact that the term "personal injury" is not defined in the statute prohibiting the reissuance of a driver's license to a person "twice convicted of driving * * * while intoxicated * * * where personal injury has resulted" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1193 [2] [c]) does not render the statute unconstitutionally vague since the use of ordinary, common terms, commonly understood in everyday discourses may convey sufficient meaning to withstand a constitutional challenge brought on vagueness grounds. In addition, the term "personal injury" encompasses relatively minor injuries and injuries to self in view of the State's strong interest in removing drunk drivers from the road and the fact that "societal costs are incurred regardless of who is injured". However, petitioner, a truck driver whose application for a new driver's license was denied on the ground that a second driving while intoxicated conviction resulted in a minor head cut to himself, was denied procedural due process and is entitled to have the determination annulled since he had no notice whatsoever that permanent revocation of his license based on two alleged injury-related accidents was under consideration nor any opportunity for a fact-finding hearing as to whether any personal injury resulted from the second conviction.

 

1397 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sound Distributing Corp. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County


144 Misc. 2d 1; 542 N.Y.S.2d 489; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 331



May 30, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted; the determination of the Authority is annulled and the matter remitted for a proper final determination.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensed beer distributor brought an action seeking an annulment of the determination by respondent New York State Liquor Authority (Authority) denying its application for a warehouse permit for the storage of alcoholic beverages.


OVERVIEW: The licensed beer distributor sought an annulment of the Authority's determination denying its application for a warehouse permit. The court held that the Authority's failure to submit to the Secretary of State its rule delegating plenary licensing authority to a licensing board, as required by the state constitution, rendered the delegation defective. The court held that the Authority's failure to comply with the rule-making procedures required by the State Administrative Procedure Act in delegating plenary licensing authority to a licensing board rendered the delegation defective. The petition was granted, the determination of the Authority was annulled, and the matter was remitted for a proper final determination.


OUTCOME: The court granted the licensed beer distributor's petition to annul the Authority's determination denying the beer distributor's application for a warehouse permit and remitted the matter for a proper final determination.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses -- Delegation of Licensing Authority to Licensing Board -- Constitutional Filing Requirement

1. The State Liquor Authority's delegation of authority for licensing to the licensing board is a "rule or regulation" which must be filed with the Secretary of State pursuant to NY Constitution, article IV, § 8 in order to be effective, since such delegation establishes a course of conduct for the future. Although a rule or regulation which relates to an agency's organization or internal management is excepted from the constitutional mandate, the fact that the Authority's delegation includes internal management matters, insofar as it designates the individuals who are to serve on the licensing board, does not make the rest of the delegation exempt from filing. Accordingly, the Authority's delegation of licensing authority to the licensing board is constitutionally defective, since the Authority has failed to file that delegation with the Secretary of State.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses -- Delegation of Licensing Authority  to Licensing Board -- State Administrative Procedure Act

2. The State Liquor Authority's delegation of licensing authority to the licensing board constitutes a "rule" within the meaning of the State Administrative Procedure Act since the delegation is, on its face, a rule of "procedure or practice" (State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2] [a]) which directly affects that segment of the general public over which the Authority exercises direct authority. Accordingly, such delegation of licensing authority is defective in light of the Authority's failure to follow the appropriate rule-making procedures required by State Administrative Procedure Act § 202.

Administrative Law -- Judicial Review -- Finality of Administrative Determination

3. Inasmuch as the State Liquor Authority's delegation of licensing authority to the licensing board is defective for failure to satisfy the filing mandate of NY Constitution, article IV, § 8 and the rule-making procedures of the State Administrative Procedure Act, a determination of the licensing board denying petitioner's application for a warehouse permit is not a valid final act of the State Liquor Authority which may be reviewed by the court in a CPLR article 78 proceeding; moreover, the lack of finality requires the invalidation of the determination. Accordingly, in a CPLR article 78 proceeding to review the Authority's determination denying petitioner's application, the petition is granted, the determination is annulled and the matter is remitted for a proper final determination.

 

1398 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Weingarten v. Lewisboro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


144 Misc. 2d 849; 542 N.Y.S.2d 1012; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 373



May 24, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's cross motion for summary judgment is granted to the extent that this court declares that section 277 (1) of the Town Law and section 45.11 of the Town of Lewisboro Land Development Regulations and the $ 5,000 fee imposed thereunder are constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners filed a motion for summary judgment requesting that the court declare § 277(1) of the Town Law and § 44.15 of the Town of Lewisboro Land Development Regulations unconstitutional in an action involving the taking of certain land for development.


OVERVIEW: This was an action for declaratory judgment wherein the landowners challenged the constitutionality of § 277(1) of the town law and § 44.15 of the town development regulations. The statute and the regulation authorized the planning board of defendant town to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, the amount was to be determined by the town board of trustees. The landowners moved for summary judgment such that the court would declare the statute and the regulation unconstitutional. The court held that the statute and the regulation were not unconstitutional and that no equal protection violation had occurred. The imposition of the fee in lieu of the reservation on new developments held a rational relationship to the legitimate purposes of the challenged legislation. Therefore, the town's cross motion for summary judgment was granted to the extent that the court declared the statute and the regulation constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the town's motion for summary judgment in an action filed by the landowners to declare a town statute and a town regulation unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Future Event

1. The fact that a court may be required to determine the rights of parties upon the happening of a future event does not mean that a declaratory judgment will be merely advisory; in the typical case where the future event is an act contemplated by one party, it is assumed that the parties will act in accordance with the law and, thus, the court's determination will have the immediate and practical effect of influencing their conduct. Accordingly, in an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, with the money to be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, the matter is imminent and not beyond the control of the parties, thereby being ripe for adjudication, where plaintiffs are owners of undeveloped subdividable parcels in some phase of the administrative process leading to subdivision approval, and where defendant town admits that the Planning Board has never required reservation of any park land as a condition of development approval and that it has been the prevailing practice to require all developers to make payment in lieu of park land reservation pursuant to section 277 (1), since it is a virtual certainty that plaintiffs will be assessed with the $ 5,000 per lot fee charged by the town as a condition of subdivision approval.

Parties -- Standing -- Lack of Aggrievement

2. In an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and local regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, with the money to be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, plaintiff developer lacks standing in light of a prior judicial decision holding that the town lacked power to impose a recreation fee on its "cluster" development subdivision plan under Town Law § 281; while that matter is currently on appeal, it is clear that within the framework of the instant action, plaintiff cannot be injured monetarily since the town is precluded from imposing a fee in lieu of reservation of park land upon plaintiff's section 281 cluster development even if it is determined that defendant town has the power to impose such a fee on a conventional, i.e., Town Law § 277, subdivision. Accordingly, the matter is not ripe as to plaintiff and the complaint insofar as it seeks declaratory relief on behalf of that plaintiff is dismissed.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Presumption of Constitutionality

3. Every law is presumed constitutional and only when unconstitutionality is shown to exist beyond a reasonable doubt is the presumption overcome; the presumption of constitutionality is not limited to State statutes, since it applies to ordinances of municipalities as well.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Recreational Areas -- Per Se Constitutionality of Requirement That Fee be Paid in Lieu of Setting Aside Park Lands in Proposed Development

4. In an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and local regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, defendant town has shown that a nexus exists between the payment of the fee, which shall be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, and the legitimate State interest sought to be advanced, viz., to contribute to the need for expanding the existing recreational facilities within the town, since the legislation is designed to alleviate the particular effects that developments will create. The benefits derived from this exaction need not solely accrue to plaintiffs' subdivisions as plaintiffs' individual needs cannot be isolated from community-wide needs where the town has determined, for economic reasons, to develop large regional parks instead of small satellite parks. Moreover, inasmuch as a genuinely valid purpose for the legislation has been established, plaintiffs' per se constitutional attack is without merit since they have not alleged that they are denied an economically viable use of their property.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Recreational Areas -- Constitutionality of Requirement  "As Applied" to Developers That Park Land be Set Aside or Fee be Paid

5. In an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and local regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, with the money to be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, the requirements for subdivision approval do not constitute an unconstitutional taking as applied to plaintiff developers, since the fee, $ 5,000 per lot, is inextricably related to the currently high property values existing in the town and is substantially less than the actual value of the land the town could concededly require plaintiffs to set aside upon approval of the subdivision; it is the actual value of the land which the town could require reserved as a condition of subdivision approval which sets the upper limit upon the fee in lieu of reservation, and so long as the fee does not exceed that level, it is not arbitrary or capricious.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Recreational Areas -- Constitutionality of Requirement That Park Land be Set Aside or Fee  be Paid  as Condition for Subdivision Approval

6. In an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and local regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, with the money to be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, no equal protection violation has occurred since the imposition of the fee in lieu of reservation on new developments bears a rational relationship to the legitimate purposes of the challenged legislation, which is to provide recreational facilities to new residents. Inasmuch as there is no suspect classification or fundamental right at issue, the rational basis test should be applied to plaintiffs' contention that equal protection was violated by having so few property owners bear so large a proportion of the recreational expenditures of the town.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Recreational Areas -- Payment of Fee in Lieu of Setting Aside Land for Park Use -- Illegal Tax

7. In an action to declare unconstitutional a statute (Town Law § 277 [1]) and local regulations authorizing a town Planning Board to require payment of money in lieu of reservation of park land as a condition of approving a subdivision plat, with the money to be used for the purchase and development of recreational areas within the town, plaintiffs' contention that the imposition of the $ 5,000 per lot fee constitutes an illegal tax has been foreclosed by a Court of Appeals decision which held that such a fee was not a tax but a reasonable form of municipal planning for the general community good (see, Jenad, Inc. v Village of Scarsdale, 18 NY2d 78).

 

1399 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fraioli's Hydro-Power, Inc. v. Department of Environmental Protection


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


143 Misc. 2d 866; 542 N.Y.S.2d 934; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 502



May 16, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In view of the foregoing, the petitioner's application is denied and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner truck washing company brought an action against respondent requesting relief under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 from a denial of its request for a variance, which would permit it to use the city's water supply for continued business operation.


OVERVIEW: The city declared a drought emergency that directly effected the truck washing business. The truck washing business applied for a variance, which was denied. The truck washing business argued that it had been granted a variance during previous drought emergencies and that it had met the test for a variance in this instance. The court held that the truck washing business could not just contend that it would suffer an economic hardship, but it had to prove the hardship, which it failed to do. The court reasoned that there were alternatives available to the truck washing business. The court concluded that the truck washing business was not entitled to a formal hearing with formal findings prior to it being denied a variance. The court determined that because there were alternatives available, the issuance of variance would have provided the truck washing business preferential treatment over its competitors.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Drought Emergency Rules -- Ban on Car Washing

The denial of a variance from the New York City Stage II Drought Emergency Rules barring the washing of vehicles with water from the public water system so as to permit petitioner to conduct its truck washing business is not arbitrary and capricious even if petitioner will suffer economic hardship since there are alternative sources of water and alternative means available to petitioner to operate the business. The issuance of a variance to petitioner would afford it a preferential treatment over its competitors and such a result is contrary to the purposes of the Drought Emergency Rules in that it would circumvent the city's effort to deter the use of water for nonessential purposes. Petitioner is not entitled to a hearing since a governmental agency acting in an administrative capacity pursuant to a statute granting it powers is not required to hold formal hearings and issue formal findings prior to rendering a determination. In addition, the fact that the Stage II regulations may make the cost of conducting petitioner's business greater, or even make it impossible to continue in business, is no reason to invalidate the regulations.

 

1400 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lee


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Allegany County


144 Misc. 2d 11; 543 N.Y.S.2d 613; 1989 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 348



May 16, 1989 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Town Court of the Town of Genesee (New York), which convicted him of misdemeanors for "wasting" oil, in violation of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-1305(1) and "polluting" the land, in violation of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-1305(2) and 6 N.Y Comp. Code R. & Regs. § 556.5(a). Defendant claimed that the prohibition against "wasting" oil in N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. § 71-1305 (1) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant also contended that he was never properly arraigned on the second charge pertaining to pollution, that the prosecution failed to prove its case beyond a reasonable doubt, and that his procedural due process rights were violated by delay in rendering of the decision, resulting in delay in the ability to appeal. The court found that defendant waived any objection he had in regard to arraignment on the "pollution" count. The court concluded that defendant was not deprived of procedural due process, because there was no constitutional guarantee of a "speedy appeal" corresponding to that of a "speedy trial" On the key question whether the statute was unconstitutionally void for vagueness, the court concluded that the definition of "waste" was not sufficiently set forth in the law. Thus, while the court concluded that there was adequate proof of the offense of "pollution, the burden of proof on the "waste" conviction was not met and the conviction was set aside. Because it was not apparent to what extent a fine imposed pertained to the waste count, the matter was remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the pollution conviction and it reversed the waste conviction, so it remanded the case for resentencing.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Pollution Control -- "Waste" of Oil

Defendant's conviction in Town Court for "polluting" the land ( ECL 71-1305 [2]; 6 NYCRR 556.5 [a]) is affirmed since there was adequate proof beyond a reasonable doubt of the elements of the offense of "pollution". Defendant waived any objection to the lack of a proper arraignment on the "pollution" charge, which was commenced by the filing of an amended information at the threshold of the trial, since the trial went forward after counsel reached an understanding concerning the charges presented by the amended information during off-the-record discussions. Defendant's procedural due process rights were not violated by the trial court's eight-month delay in rendering its decision and the five-month delay in filing its return since extenuating circumstances, including the illness of the Trial Judge, excuse the delays and defendant was not unduly prejudiced inasmuch as he was not incarcerated. However, defendant's conviction for "wasting oil" ( ECL 71-1305 [1]) must be reversed since the prohibitory statute and implementing regulations (6 NYCRR 550.3 [az]) are unconstitutionally vague in that the failure to specify what degree of criminal intent is required, when the crime of waste occurs and the absence of any quantitative measure, result in the failure to give a reasonable person notice of the nature of what conduct is prohibited and what conduct is required.
